# 4.8. OpenFlow plus OVS match field catalog, full reference for 60+ fields

> **State:** Content written during the 2026-04-24 expansion. The file was created in that batch as a complement to section 4.1 (OF 1.2 OXM TLV historical) with detailed per-field reference treatment.
> **Block:** IV, OpenFlow evolution.
> **Part:** Part 4.
> **Language status:** Mixed. Header, learning objectives, context, §4.8.1 anatomy template, and §4.8.2 through §4.8.X.15 (metadata, register, L2, ARP, IPv4, IPv6, L4, ICMP, tunnel, conntrack, MPLS, prerequisite tables, lazy-wildcarding scenario, the §4.8.15 backfill of 12 fields, source citation, key takeaways, references, and the medium-tier deep dives §4.8.X.1 through §4.8.X.15) are now English. Sections §4.8.X.16 onward (vlan_pcp, the IPv6 deep dives, conntrack original-tuple fields, MPLS deep dives, NSH, register deep dives reg0 through reg15, dp_hash, conj_id, xreg, xxreg) remain Vietnamese pending the next translation pass.
> **Ebook mapping:** `ovs-fields(7)` (100 plus fields) plus OpenFlow 1.3 and 1.5 spec §A.2.3.
> **Plan:** `plans/phase-h-foundation-depth.md` §4 first-wave rollout.
> **Prerequisites:** Part 3.1 (OF 1.0 12-tuple), Part 4.0 (OF 1.1 multi-table), Part 4.1 (OF 1.2 OXM TLV), Part 4.2 (OF 1.3 meters).
> **Template applied:** Template B per-field 9-attribute anatomy (per the `ovs-fields(7)` pattern).
> **Primary offline source:**
> - `sdn-onboard/doc/compass_artifact_wf-*.md`, Chapter B (OpenFlow match field evolution), Chapter D (OXM TLV).
> - `sdn-onboard/doc/ovs/Day 4-lab4-ovs flow table.pdf`, WASTC 2021 Lab 4 flow table.
>
> **Authoritative upstream sources:**
> - [man `ovs-fields(7)`](https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man7/ovs-fields.7.html), 100 plus match fields.
> - OpenFlow 1.3 spec §7.2.3 "Flow Match Structures".
> - OpenFlow 1.5 spec §A.2.3 "Flow Match Field Structures" (OXM TLV).
> - OVS source `include/openvswitch/meta-flow.h` (MFF_* enum).

Standard environment: Ubuntu 22.04 LTS plus Open vSwitch 2.17.9. Sample output reproduced from `ovs-ofctl dump-flows` plus `ovs-appctl ofproto/trace`.

---

## Learning objectives

1. **Remember** the 12 foundation match field groups in OpenFlow plus OVS extensions: Metadata, Register, Tunnel, L2, ARP, IPv4, IPv6, L4 (TCP/UDP/SCTP), ICMP, MPLS, Conntrack, Packet-type.
2. **Comprehend** the 9-attribute anatomy per field: Name, Width, Format, Masking, Prerequisites, Access, OpenFlow version support, OXM/NXM identifier, Semantics.
3. **Apply** the correct field prerequisites (for example, `eth_type=0x0800` required before matching on `nw_src` or `nw_dst`) to avoid the "field not in normal form" error.
4. **Analyze** trace output to see which fields are inspected versus wildcarded, deriving how lazy wildcarding generates the megaflow mask (Part 9.2).
5. **Evaluate** why a flow rule does not match the expected packet: wrong field alias, prerequisite conflict, version mismatch (OF 1.0 versus 1.3 and later OXM).

---

## Context: why we need a full catalog

OpenFlow 1.0 (December 2009) had **12 hard-coded match fields**, with each field either fully matched or fully wildcarded:

```
in_port, dl_src, dl_dst, dl_type, dl_vlan, dl_vlan_pcp,
nw_src, nw_dst, nw_proto, nw_tos, tp_src, tp_dst
```

OpenFlow 1.2 (December 2011) moved to the OXM TLV extensible format (covered in Part 4.1). From that point the match-field set has grown continuously:
- OpenFlow 1.3 (April 2012): full IPv6 plus meter identifier.
- OpenFlow 1.4 (August 2013): optical port fields.
- OpenFlow 1.5 (December 2014): packet_type, PBB I-SID, actset_output, TCP flags.
- OVS Nicira extensions (NXM): registers reg0 to reg15, xreg, xxreg, conntrack fields, pkt_mark, metadata alias.
- OVN extensions: tun_metadata[0-63] for Geneve TLV, ct_nw_src/dst/proto (conntrack original-direction).

By OVS 2.17, the `ovs-fields(7)` man page lists more than 100 match fields. Memorising every one is impossible for a new engineer, but **grouping plus prerequisite mastery** is achievable. This Part organises the fields into 12 groups, with the important fields in each group getting a Template B breakdown.

---

## 4.8.1 9-attribute anatomy per field (the shared template)

Each field is described under the following template (the `ovs-fields(7)` 9-attribute pattern):

| Attribute | Meaning |
|---|---|
| **Name** | Field name plus aliases (for example `nw_src` = `ip_src` = `ipv4_src`) |
| **Width** | Number of bits in the field |
| **Format** | How the value is parsed (hex, decimal, MAC, IPv4, IPv6, string, set) |
| **Masking** | Whether masking is supported (bitwise, CIDR prefix, exact-only) |
| **Prerequisites** | Other match fields that must be set first (for example `eth_type=0x0800` for IP) |
| **Access** | Read-only in match / read-write through the `set_field` action |
| **OpenFlow version** | 1.0 / 1.1 / 1.3 / 1.5 / NXM (Nicira extension) |
| **OXM/NXM identifier** | The symbolic name (used in the spec parse) |
| **Semantics** | The field's meaning in the packet or pipeline context |

In addition: an example match syntax, related actions, failure scenarios, and upstream references.

---

## 4.8.2 Group A, Metadata fields (6 foundation fields)

Metadata fields are not part of the wire packet; they are **context** from the pipeline or the bridge:

### 4.8.2.1 `in_port`, the ingress port

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `in_port` (alias: `in_port_oxm` for OF 1.2 and later) |
| **Width** | 16-bit (OF 1.0) / 32-bit (OF 1.1 and later) |
| **Format** | Integer port number or port name |
| **Masking** | Exact match only |
| **Prerequisites** | None |
| **Access** | Read-only in match |
| **OpenFlow version** | 1.0 all versions |
| **OXM/NXM** | `OXM_OF_IN_PORT` (OF 1.2 and later), `OXM_OF_IN_PHY_PORT` (OF 1.1 and later, bonded case), `NXM_OF_IN_PORT` |
| **Semantics** | The datapath port number where the packet arrived. Used to differentiate ingress from different ports |

**Reserved port values:**

| Port name | Value | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| `LOCAL` | 0xfffe | Bridge internal port |
| `CONTROLLER` | 0xfffd | OpenFlow controller (packet_in direction) |
| `NORMAL` | 0xfffa | Standard L2 learning-switch behaviour |
| `FLOOD` | 0xfffb | Flood out of every port except the ingress |
| `ALL` | 0xfffc | Output to every physical port |
| `IN_PORT` | 0xfff8 | Send back out the ingress port |
| `ANY` | 0xffffffff | Wildcard (match any port) |
| `TABLE` | 0xfff9 | Submit the packet to the OpenFlow pipeline lookup |

**Match examples:**

```
priority=100,in_port=eth0,actions=output:2           # match ingress eth0
priority=100,in_port=LOCAL,actions=ct(commit)        # match the bridge-internal port
```

**Failure scenarios:**

- Using a numeric port number (`in_port=1`) instead of the port name (`in_port=tap-vm-web`) makes the flow rule break when ovs-vswitchd restarts and reassigns the port number.

### 4.8.2.2 `metadata`, 64-bit pipeline metadata

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `metadata` |
| **Width** | 64-bit |
| **Format** | Hex |
| **Masking** | Bitwise mask supported |
| **Prerequisites** | None |
| **Access** | Read via match; write via the `Write-Metadata` instruction or the `set_field` action |
| **OpenFlow version** | 1.1 and later |
| **OXM/NXM** | `OXM_OF_METADATA` |
| **Semantics** | A 64-bit opaque value set by the `Write-Metadata` instruction in an earlier table and matched in a later table. Used to pass state across tables in a multi-table pipeline |

**Use case example:**

```
# Table 0 classify + set metadata
table=0,priority=100,ip,nw_src=10.0.1.0/24,
  actions=write_metadata:0x10/0xff,goto_table:10

# Table 10, policy based on metadata
table=10,metadata=0x10/0xff,actions=output:5
```

**Failure scenarios:**

- `metadata` only carries through the `Goto-Table` instruction. **It does not carry through recirculation** (recirc resets metadata). If you need to preserve a value across a recirculation, use a register instead.

### 4.8.2.3 `pkt_mark`, Linux skb mark bridging

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `pkt_mark` |
| **Width** | 32-bit |
| **Format** | Hex or decimal |
| **Masking** | Bitwise |
| **Prerequisites** | None |
| **Access** | Read and write (through the `set_field` action) |
| **OpenFlow version** | NXM extension |
| **OXM/NXM** | `NXM_NX_PKT_MARK` |
| **Semantics** | Tied to the Linux `skb->mark`. Used to pass state between OVS and `iptables`/`nftables`, or with a kernel L7 filter |

**Use case example:**

```
# OVS marks the packet for an iptables policy
actions=set_field:0x42->pkt_mark,output:eth0

# iptables then matches the mark
iptables -A FORWARD -m mark --mark 0x42 -j ACCEPT
```

### 4.8.2.4 `actset_output`, OF 1.5 action set output port

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `actset_output` |
| **Width** | 32-bit |
| **Format** | Integer port number |
| **Masking** | Exact match |
| **Prerequisites** | None |
| **Access** | Read-only (set automatically by the `output` action inside an action set) |
| **OpenFlow version** | 1.5 and later |
| **OXM/NXM** | `OXM_OF_ACTSET_OUTPUT` |
| **Semantics** | The port number that the `output` instruction in the current action set points to. Lets an egress table match based on the output decision taken at ingress |

Sample use: in an OF 1.5 egress pipeline, match `actset_output=5` to apply a QoS policy to traffic destined for port 5.

### 4.8.2.5 `skb_priority`, Linux QoS priority

A 32-bit field tied to `skb->priority`. Used together with the `set_queue` action to select a Linux HTB class.

```
actions=set_queue:2,output:eth0    # equivalent to setting skb_priority
```

### 4.8.2.6 `tun_metadata[0-63]`, Geneve TLV carry

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `tun_metadata0` through `tun_metadata63` |
| **Width** | Variable (1 byte to 128 bytes per metadata slot) |
| **Format** | Hex |
| **Masking** | Bitwise |
| **Prerequisites** | Tunnel port (Geneve) |
| **Access** | Read and write |
| **OpenFlow version** | NXM extension |
| **OXM/NXM** | `NXM_NX_TUN_METADATA0` through `NXM_NX_TUN_METADATA63` |
| **Semantics** | A Geneve Variable-Length TLV option. OVN uses `tun_metadata0` to carry the logical ingress port and `tun_metadata1` to carry the logical egress port over the Geneve header |

For the full details on the Geneve TLV class `0x0102` see Part 9.27.

---

## 4.8.3 Group B, Register fields (reg0-reg15 plus xreg plus xxreg)

A register is **scratch space** that lives per packet in the pipeline. It is not part of the wire packet, and it is not shared between packets. Use it to pass state between tables, or between stages of the same table.

| Register family | Count | Width | OXM alias |
|---|---|---|---|
| `reg0` through `reg15` | 16 | 32-bit each | `NXM_NX_REG0` through `NXM_NX_REG15` |
| `xreg0` through `xreg7` | 8 | 64-bit (two 32-bit registers concatenated) | `OXM_OF_PKT_REG0` through `OXM_OF_PKT_REG7` |
| `xxreg0` through `xxreg3` | 4 | 128-bit (four 32-bit registers concatenated) | NXM extension |

### 4.8.3.1 Template B for `reg0` (representative of the register group)

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `reg0` |
| **Width** | 32-bit |
| **Format** | Hex |
| **Masking** | Bitwise plus bit-range (for example `reg0[0..15]`) |
| **Prerequisites** | None |
| **Access** | Read and write (through the `load`, `move`, and `set_field` actions) |
| **OpenFlow version** | NXM extension (OVS specific) |
| **OXM/NXM** | `NXM_NX_REG0` |
| **Semantics** | A 32-bit per-packet scratch register. Preserved across the pipeline but lost when the packet exits |

**Load and use example:**

```
# Table 0: load 0x42 into reg0
table=0,priority=100,in_port=1,actions=load:0x42->NXM_NX_REG0[],resubmit(,10)

# Table 10: match reg0 to choose the route
table=10,reg0=0x42,actions=output:5
table=10,reg0=0x00,actions=drop
```

**Bit-range match:**

```
# Match only bits 0 through 15 of reg0
table=10,reg0=0x0042/0xffff,actions=output:5
```

### 4.8.3.2 How OVN uses registers

OVN reserves a fixed register map in the logical-flow to OpenFlow translation:

| Register | OVN semantics |
|---|---|
| `reg0` | ACL flags (`reg0[0]=ct_hint`, `reg0[1]=committed`, and so on) |
| `reg13` | Logical datapath tunnel ID |
| `reg14` | Logical ingress port |
| `reg15` | Logical egress port |
| `xxreg0` / `xxreg1` | IPv6 source and destination working copy |

An operator debugging an OVN OpenFlow trace must memorise this map. See `ovn-architecture(7)` §Tunnel Encapsulation for the canonical table.

---

## 4.8.4 Group D, L2 Ethernet fields (9 foundation fields)

### 4.8.4.1 `eth_src`, `eth_dst`

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `eth_src` (alias: `dl_src`), `eth_dst` (alias: `dl_dst`) |
| **Width** | 48-bit (6 bytes) |
| **Format** | MAC `aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:ff` |
| **Masking** | Bitwise mask per byte (for example `01:00:00:00:00:00/01:00:00:00:00:00` for the multicast bit) |
| **Prerequisites** | None |
| **Access** | Read and write (through `mod_dl_src`, `mod_dl_dst`, `set_field`) |
| **OpenFlow version** | 1.0 onward |
| **OXM/NXM** | `OXM_OF_ETH_SRC`, `OXM_OF_ETH_DST` |
| **Semantics** | Ethernet source and destination MAC. Bit 0 of byte 0 is the multicast bit (1 = multicast or broadcast, 0 = unicast) |

**Match examples:**

```
# Multicast traffic: the first byte has bit 0 set
priority=100,eth_dst=01:00:00:00:00:00/01:00:00:00:00:00,actions=...

# Broadcast (exact)
priority=100,eth_dst=ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff,actions=flood

# Match an OUI vendor (first 3 bytes)
priority=100,eth_src=00:50:56:00:00:00/ff:ff:ff:00:00:00,actions=...
```

### 4.8.4.2 `eth_type`, EtherType

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `eth_type` (alias: `dl_type`) |
| **Width** | 16-bit |
| **Format** | Hex |
| **Masking** | Exact match |
| **Prerequisites** | None |
| **Access** | Read-only (written indirectly through `push_vlan` and `pop_vlan`) |
| **OpenFlow version** | 1.0 onward |
| **OXM/NXM** | `OXM_OF_ETH_TYPE` |
| **Semantics** | The EtherType of the packet. A prerequisite for matching any L3 field |

**Common values:**

| Value | Protocol | Field that requires this prerequisite |
|---|---|---|
| `0x0800` | IPv4 | `nw_src/dst`, `nw_proto`, `nw_tos`, and so on |
| `0x86dd` | IPv6 | `ipv6_src/dst`, `ipv6_label` |
| `0x0806` | ARP | `arp_op`, `arp_spa/tpa/sha/tha` |
| `0x8100` | VLAN 802.1Q | (VLAN fields are checked automatically) |
| `0x88a8` | Q-in-Q (802.1ad) | PBB fields |
| `0x8847` | MPLS unicast | `mpls_label/tc/bos/ttl` |

### 4.8.4.3 `vlan_tci`, `vlan_vid`, `vlan_pcp`, VLAN fields

| Attribute | `vlan_tci` (classic) | `vlan_vid` (OXM) | `vlan_pcp` (OXM) |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Width** | 16-bit (full TCI) | 12-bit | 3-bit |
| **Format** | Hex | Decimal VLAN ID | Decimal 0 through 7 |
| **Masking** | Bitwise | Bitwise | Exact |
| **Prerequisites** | None | None (the VLAN-present flag is implicit) | None |
| **OpenFlow version** | 1.0 `vlan_tci`; 1.2 and later use the `vlan_vid` plus `vlan_pcp` OXM pair |
| **OXM** | NXM | `OXM_OF_VLAN_VID` | `OXM_OF_VLAN_PCP` |

**CFI/Present bit:** bit 12 of `vlan_tci` is the CFI (OF 1.0) or the Present flag (OF 1.1 and later OXM). The expression `vlan_vid=0` combined with CFI=0 means "untagged"; the expression `vlan_vid=0x1000` combined with CFI=1 means "tagged with priority 0".

**Match example for VLAN 100:**

```
priority=100,vlan_vid=100,actions=strip_vlan,output:2
priority=100,dl_vlan=100,actions=mod_vlan_vid:200,output:3
```

### 4.8.4.4 `conj_id`, Conjunctive Match

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `conj_id` |
| **Width** | 32-bit |
| **Format** | Decimal |
| **Masking** | Exact |
| **Prerequisites** | The packet must have already passed through a `conjunction()` action |
| **Access** | Read-only |
| **OpenFlow version** | NXM extension |
| **OXM/NXM** | `NXM_NX_CONJ_ID` |
| **Semantics** | The ID of the conjunction that matched. Lets you compress a cross-product ACL into far fewer rules |

**Cross-product compression example:**

Without conjunction, 3 sources times 3 destinations equals 9 rules. With conjunction the rule count drops to 3 plus 3 equals 6 rules:

```
# Source rules (conj 1, component 1/2)
priority=100,ip,nw_src=10.0.1.1,actions=conjunction(1,1/2)
priority=100,ip,nw_src=10.0.1.2,actions=conjunction(1,1/2)
priority=100,ip,nw_src=10.0.1.3,actions=conjunction(1,1/2)

# Destination rules (conj 1, component 2/2)
priority=100,ip,nw_dst=10.0.2.1,actions=conjunction(1,2/2)
priority=100,ip,nw_dst=10.0.2.2,actions=conjunction(1,2/2)
priority=100,ip,nw_dst=10.0.2.3,actions=conjunction(1,2/2)

# Final rule that matches conj_id
priority=100,conj_id=1,actions=output:5
```

The compression factor is N times M rules collapsed into N plus M rules. OVN ACLs use this pattern for `Port_Group` cross-products.

### 4.8.4.5 `packet_type`, OF 1.5 non-Ethernet packets

A 32-bit field that identifies the packet namespace and type. The default value `0:0` means Ethernet. OF 1.5 supports other `packet_type` values for raw IP (no Ethernet header) and for NSH. This is rarely used in OVN deployments.

---

## 4.8.5 Group E, ARP fields (5 fields)

### 4.8.5.1 Template B for the ARP group

| Field | Width | Semantics | OXM |
|---|---|---|---|
| `arp_op` | 16-bit | ARP opcode: 1=Request, 2=Reply, 3=RARP Request, 4=RARP Reply | `OXM_OF_ARP_OP` |
| `arp_spa` | 32-bit IPv4 | ARP sender protocol address | `OXM_OF_ARP_SPA` |
| `arp_tpa` | 32-bit IPv4 | ARP target protocol address | `OXM_OF_ARP_TPA` |
| `arp_sha` | 48-bit MAC | ARP sender hardware address | `OXM_OF_ARP_SHA` |
| `arp_tha` | 48-bit MAC | ARP target hardware address | `OXM_OF_ARP_THA` |

**Shared prerequisite:** `eth_type=0x0806`.

**Match example for an ARP request to a specific IP:**

```
priority=200,arp,arp_op=1,arp_tpa=10.1.0.1,actions=local,controller
```

**Failure scenarios:**

- Matching `arp_spa` without `eth_type=0x0806` causes a flow-spec error: "ARP field requires eth_type 0x0806". OVS suggests adding the prerequisite, or using the `arp` alias.

---

## 4.8.6 Group F, L3 IPv4 fields (6 fields)

### 4.8.6.1 `ipv4_src`, `ipv4_dst`

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `ipv4_src` (aliases: `ip_src`, `nw_src`), `ipv4_dst` (aliases: `ip_dst`, `nw_dst`) |
| **Width** | 32-bit |
| **Format** | IPv4 dotted `10.0.0.1` or CIDR `10.0.0.0/24` |
| **Masking** | CIDR prefix or bitwise mask |
| **Prerequisites** | `eth_type=0x0800` (or the `ip` alias) |
| **Access** | Read and write (`mod_nw_src`, `mod_nw_dst`) |
| **OpenFlow version** | 1.0 onward |
| **OXM/NXM** | `OXM_OF_IPV4_SRC`, `OXM_OF_IPV4_DST` |

**Match examples:**

```
# Match a subnet
priority=100,ip,nw_src=10.0.0.0/24,actions=output:5

# Match a host exactly
priority=100,ip,nw_dst=10.0.0.1,actions=drop

# Mask an arbitrary bit pattern
priority=100,ip,nw_src=10.0.0.0/0xffffff00,actions=...
```

### 4.8.6.2 `nw_proto`, IP protocol number

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `nw_proto` (alias: `ip_proto`) |
| **Width** | 8-bit |
| **Format** | Integer 0 through 255, or a protocol name |
| **Masking** | Exact |
| **Prerequisites** | `eth_type=0x0800` or `eth_type=0x86dd` |
| **OXM/NXM** | `OXM_OF_IP_PROTO` |

**Common values:**

| Value | Protocol | Field that requires this prerequisite |
|---|---|---|
| 1 | ICMP | `icmp_type`, `icmp_code` |
| 2 | IGMP | None |
| 6 | TCP | `tcp_src`, `tcp_dst`, `tcp_flags` |
| 17 | UDP | `udp_src`, `udp_dst` |
| 41 | IPv6 encapsulation | None |
| 47 | GRE | None |
| 50 | ESP (IPsec) | None |
| 51 | AH (IPsec) | None |
| 58 | ICMPv6 | `icmpv6_type`, `icmpv6_code` |
| 89 | OSPF | None |
| 132 | SCTP | `sctp_src`, `sctp_dst` |

**Convenient alias examples:**

```
priority=100,tcp,tp_dst=80,actions=...   # eth_type=0x0800, nw_proto=6
priority=100,udp,tp_dst=53,actions=...   # eth_type=0x0800, nw_proto=17
priority=100,icmp,actions=...            # eth_type=0x0800, nw_proto=1
```

### 4.8.6.3 `nw_tos`, `nw_ecn`, `nw_ttl`

| Field | Width | Format | Semantics |
|---|---|---|---|
| `nw_tos` | 6-bit | Integer 0 through 63 | IPv4 DSCP (the upper 6 bits of the TOS byte). Used for QoS classification |
| `nw_ecn` | 2-bit | Integer 0 through 3 | ECN (the lower 2 bits of the TOS byte). 0=not-ECT, 1=ECT(1), 2=ECT(0), 3=CE (congestion experienced) |
| `nw_ttl` | 8-bit | Integer 0 through 255 | IPv4 TTL. Decremented at each hop |

**Prerequisites:** `eth_type=0x0800` (IPv4) or `eth_type=0x86dd` (IPv6 uses Hop Limit and Traffic Class instead of TTL and TOS).

**Related actions:**
- `mod_nw_tos`, `mod_nw_ecn`, `mod_nw_ttl`
- `dec_ttl`, decrement TTL or Hop Limit per router semantics

---

## 4.8.7 Group G, L3 IPv6 fields (7 fields)

### 4.8.7.1 `ipv6_src`, `ipv6_dst`

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `ipv6_src` and `ipv6_dst` |
| **Width** | 128-bit |
| **Format** | IPv6 colon notation `fe80::1` or CIDR `2001:db8::/32` |
| **Masking** | CIDR prefix or 128-bit bitwise mask |
| **Prerequisites** | `eth_type=0x86dd` (the `ipv6` alias) |
| **OpenFlow version** | 1.2 and later |
| **OXM/NXM** | `OXM_OF_IPV6_SRC`, `OXM_OF_IPV6_DST` |

**Match examples:**

```
priority=100,ipv6,ipv6_dst=2001:db8::/64,actions=output:5
priority=100,ipv6,ipv6_src=fe80::/10,actions=drop  # link-local
```

### 4.8.7.2 `ipv6_label`, Flow Label

The 20-bit IPv6 Flow Label (RFC 6437). Used for multi-protocol ECMP hashing.

### 4.8.7.3 `ipv6_exthdr`, Extension Headers bitmap

A 9-bit bitmap that reports which IPv6 extension headers are present in the packet:

| Bit | Extension header |
|---|---|
| 0 | Hop-by-Hop (NONEXT stays 0) |
| 1 | Routing |
| 2 | Fragment |
| 3 | Destination Options |
| 4 | Authentication (AH) |
| 5 | ESP |
| 6 | No Next Header |
| 7 | Unexpected repeats |
| 8 | Unexpected order |

**Match example for a packet that carries a fragment extension:**

```
priority=100,ipv6,ipv6_exthdr=0x04/0x04,actions=drop
```

### 4.8.7.4 `nd_target`, `nd_sll`, `nd_tll`, IPv6 Neighbor Discovery

IPv6 Neighbor Discovery (RFC 4861) replaces ARP. OVS supports matching the relevant fields directly:

| Field | Width | Semantics |
|---|---|---|
| `nd_target` | 128-bit IPv6 | Target address of the ND message |
| `nd_sll` | 48-bit MAC | Source Link-Layer Address option |
| `nd_tll` | 48-bit MAC | Target Link-Layer Address option |

**Prerequisites:** `eth_type=0x86dd`, `nw_proto=58`, `icmpv6_type` in `{135, 136}`.

An OVN gateway router uses `nd_target` the same way it uses `arp_tpa` to act as an IPv6 ND proxy.

---

## 4.8.8 Group H, L4 Transport fields

### 4.8.8.1 `tcp_src`, `tcp_dst`, `tcp_flags`

| Field | Width | OXM |
|---|---|---|
| `tcp_src` (alias `tp_src`) | 16-bit | `OXM_OF_TCP_SRC` |
| `tcp_dst` (alias `tp_dst`) | 16-bit | `OXM_OF_TCP_DST` |
| `tcp_flags` | 12-bit bitmap | `OXM_OF_TCP_FLAGS` (OF 1.5 and later, or NXM) |

**Prerequisites for all three fields:** `eth_type=0x0800` (or `0x86dd`), `nw_proto=6`, or simply the `tcp` alias.

**`tcp_flags` bitmap:**

| Bit | Flag | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| 0 | FIN | End of connection |
| 1 | SYN | Open connection |
| 2 | RST | Reset |
| 3 | PSH | Push buffer |
| 4 | ACK | Acknowledgement |
| 5 | URG | Urgent |
| 6 | ECE | ECN Echo |
| 7 | CWR | Congestion Window Reduced |
| 8 | NS | Nonce Sum |

**Match example for SYN-only (initiate connection):**

```
priority=100,tcp,tcp_flags=+syn-ack,actions=ct(commit,zone=1)
```

### 4.8.8.2 `udp_src`, `udp_dst`

Same shape as TCP but with the prerequisite `nw_proto=17`:

```
priority=100,udp,tp_dst=53,actions=ct(commit)  # DNS
priority=100,udp,tp_src=68,tp_dst=67,actions=output:controller  # DHCP
```

### 4.8.8.3 `sctp_src`, `sctp_dst`

Prerequisite is `nw_proto=132`. Rarely used in hyperscale cloud workloads, common in telecom.

---

## 4.8.9 Group I, ICMP fields (4 fields)

### 4.8.9.1 `icmp_type`, `icmp_code` (IPv4)

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Width** | 8-bit each |
| **Format** | Integer 0 through 255 |
| **Masking** | Exact |
| **Prerequisites** | `eth_type=0x0800`, `nw_proto=1` (the `icmp` alias) |
| **OXM** | `OXM_OF_ICMPV4_TYPE`, `OXM_OF_ICMPV4_CODE` |

**Common values:**

| Type | Code | Description |
|---|---|---|
| 0 | 0 | Echo Reply |
| 3 | 0 to 15 | Destination Unreachable (codes: 0=Net, 1=Host, 3=Port, 4=Fragmentation needed, and so on) |
| 5 | 0 to 3 | Redirect |
| 8 | 0 | Echo Request (ping) |
| 11 | 0 to 1 | TTL Exceeded |
| 12 | 0 | Parameter Problem |

### 4.8.9.2 `icmpv6_type`, `icmpv6_code`

Prerequisite is `eth_type=0x86dd`, `nw_proto=58`. Important types:

| Type | Description |
|---|---|
| 1 | Destination Unreachable |
| 2 | Packet Too Big (used by PMTUD) |
| 3 | Time Exceeded |
| 128 | Echo Request |
| 129 | Echo Reply |
| 133 | Router Solicitation |
| 134 | Router Advertisement |
| 135 | Neighbor Solicitation |
| 136 | Neighbor Advertisement |
| 137 | Redirect |

---

## 4.8.10 Group C, Tunnel fields (six representative foundation fields)

### 4.8.10.1 `tun_id`, Tunnel ID (VNI, Geneve key, GRE key)

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `tun_id` (aliases: `tunnel_id`, `tun_key`) |
| **Width** | 64-bit (OVS can carry a 24-bit VNI plus padding) |
| **Format** | Integer |
| **Masking** | Bitwise |
| **Prerequisites** | None (this is tunnel metadata) |
| **Access** | Read and write (set automatically based on the tunnel port type) |
| **OpenFlow version** | NXM extension |
| **OXM/NXM** | `NXM_NX_TUN_ID` |
| **Semantics** | Holds the VXLAN VNI (24-bit), Geneve VNI (24-bit), or GRE key (32-bit). When a tunnel port is configured with `key=flow`, OVS sets `tun_id` from a flow action on each packet |

**Match example for VXLAN VNI 100:**

```
priority=100,tun_id=100,actions=output:br-int
```

### 4.8.10.2 `tun_src`, `tun_dst`, tunnel endpoint

The outer IP address of the tunnel: 32-bit IPv4 or 128-bit IPv6. OVN matches `tun_src=<remote chassis IP>` to identify the sending chassis.

### 4.8.10.3 `tun_flags`, tunnel flags

| Bit | Flag |
|---|---|
| 0 | OAM |
| 1 | Don't Fragment |
| 2 | Checksum present |
| 3 | Key present |
| 4 | CSUM |

---

## 4.8.11 Group J, Conntrack fields (9 fields)

### 4.8.11.1 `ct_state`

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `ct_state` |
| **Width** | 32-bit bitmap |
| **Format** | Flag list (`+new`, `-est`, `+rel`, and so on) |
| **Masking** | Per-flag |
| **Prerequisites** | The packet must have already passed through a `ct()` action (no header prerequisite) |
| **Access** | Read-only (set by the `ct()` action) |
| **OpenFlow version** | NXM extension |
| **OXM/NXM** | `NXM_NX_CT_STATE` |

**Bitmap flags:**

| Flag | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `new` | First packet of the connection |
| `est` (`established`) | Connection is established |
| `rel` (`related`) | Related connection (for example an ICMP error tied to a TCP session) |
| `rpl` (`reply`) | Reply direction |
| `inv` (`invalid`) | Could not be parsed |
| `trk` (`tracked`) | Packet has already passed through `ct()` |
| `snat` | Source NAT has been applied |
| `dnat` | Destination NAT has been applied |

**Stateful firewall example:**

```
# Allow new outbound, then commit
table=0,priority=200,ip,ct_state=+new+trk,
  actions=ct(commit,zone=1),output:uplink

# Allow established replies
table=0,priority=100,ip,ct_state=+est+trk,actions=output:inside

# Drop invalid
table=0,priority=100,ip,ct_state=+inv+trk,actions=drop
```

### 4.8.11.2 `ct_zone`, `ct_mark`, `ct_label`

| Field | Width | Semantics |
|---|---|---|
| `ct_zone` | 16-bit | Conntrack zone (namespace isolation). OVN uses one zone per logical switch or router |
| `ct_mark` | 32-bit | Conntrack mark, persistent across every packet in the same connection |
| `ct_label` | 128-bit | Conntrack label bitmap, used to tag a connection with classification metadata |

**Examples:**

```
# Commit a connection into zone 42 with mark 0xab
actions=ct(commit,zone=42,exec(set_field:0xab->ct_mark))

# Match a connection in zone 42 with mark 0xab
priority=100,ct_zone=42,ct_mark=0xab,actions=output:5
```

### 4.8.11.3 `ct_nw_src`, `ct_nw_dst`, `ct_nw_proto`, `ct_tp_src`, `ct_tp_dst`

These are the **original** tuple fields of the connection before NAT was applied. The OVN Load Balancer matches `ct_nw_dst=<backend IP>` to undo source NAT on reply traffic returning to the VIP.

| Field | Width |
|---|---|
| `ct_nw_src` | 32-bit IPv4 (or 128-bit IPv6) |
| `ct_nw_dst` | 32-bit IPv4 |
| `ct_nw_proto` | 8-bit |
| `ct_tp_src` | 16-bit |
| `ct_tp_dst` | 16-bit |

---

## 4.8.12 Group K, MPLS plus IP fragment (5 fields)

### 4.8.12.1 The 4 MPLS fields

| Field | Width | Semantics |
|---|---|---|
| `mpls_label` | 20-bit | MPLS label |
| `mpls_tc` | 3-bit | Traffic Class (used for QoS) |
| `mpls_bos` | 1-bit | Bottom of Stack |
| `mpls_ttl` | 8-bit | MPLS TTL |

**Prerequisites:** `eth_type=0x8847` (MPLS unicast) or `0x8848` (multicast).

### 4.8.12.2 `ip_frag`

| Attribute | Value |
|---|---|
| **Name** | `ip_frag` (alias: `nw_frag`) |
| **Width** | 2-bit |
| **Format** | Flag: `no`, `first`, `later` |
| **Prerequisites** | `eth_type=0x0800` or `0x86dd` |
| **OXM/NXM** | `NXM_NX_IP_FRAG` |

**Values:**

- `no`, the packet is not fragmented.
- `first` (alias `yes`), the first fragment, which carries the L4 header.
- `later`, a subsequent fragment, which does not carry the L4 header.

**Match example to drop later fragments:**

```
priority=200,ip,ip_frag=later,actions=drop  # drop every later fragment
```

---

## 4.8.13 Prerequisite chain summary table

A new operator often hits the error "field X not in normal form". This table is a quick reference:

| If you want to match | The prerequisite is |
|---|---|
| `nw_src`, `nw_dst`, `nw_proto`, `nw_tos` | `eth_type=0x0800` (use the `ip` alias) |
| `ipv6_src`, `ipv6_dst`, `ipv6_label` | `eth_type=0x86dd` (alias `ipv6`) |
| `arp_op`, `arp_spa`, `arp_tpa`, `arp_sha`, `arp_tha` | `eth_type=0x0806` (alias `arp`) |
| `tcp_src`, `tcp_dst`, `tcp_flags` | `ip, nw_proto=6` (alias `tcp`) |
| `udp_src`, `udp_dst` | `ip, nw_proto=17` (alias `udp`) |
| `sctp_src`, `sctp_dst` | `ip, nw_proto=132` (alias `sctp`) |
| `icmp_type`, `icmp_code` | `ip, nw_proto=1` (alias `icmp`) |
| `icmpv6_type`, `icmpv6_code` | `ipv6, nw_proto=58` (alias `icmp6`) |
| `nd_target`, `nd_sll`, `nd_tll` | `icmp6, icmpv6_type=135` or `136` |
| `mpls_label`, `mpls_tc`, `mpls_bos`, `mpls_ttl` | `eth_type=0x8847` (alias `mpls`) |
| `ct_state`, `ct_zone`, `ct_mark`, `ct_label` | The packet has already passed through `ct()` (carries the `+trk` flag) |
| `conj_id` | The packet has already passed through a `conjunction()` action |

Prefer the short aliases (`ip`, `tcp`, `arp`, `icmp`). They set the prerequisite for you. The flow spec stays cleaner and the error rate drops.

---

## 4.8.15 Backfill v3.5, 9-attribute anatomy completeness for 12 missing fields

> **Purpose:** the J.1 audit during plan v3.5-KeywordBackbone found 12 match fields from the REF that did not yet have full 5-axis 9-attribute anatomy entries in this catalog. This section backfills them, following the Template B definition from 4.8.1.

### 4.8.15.1 `in_phy_port`, the physical port after tunnel decapsulation

**Bucket:** Pipeline metadata.
**Bits:** 32-bit OpenFlow port number.
**Maskable:** No.
**Writable:** No (read-only metadata).
**Prerequisites:** None.
**OpenFlow version introduced:** OF 1.2 and later (`OXM_OF_IN_PHY_PORT`).
**OVS version introduced:** OVS 1.7 and later.
**NXM/OXM:** `OXM_OF_IN_PHY_PORT` (class `0x8000`, type 1).
**Example:**

```
priority=100,in_port=2,in_phy_port=5,actions=goto_table:1
```

**How it works in practice:** when a packet arrives over a tunnel port (Geneve, VXLAN, or GRE), `in_port` points at the logical tunnel port and `in_phy_port` points at the physical NIC port that the tunnel runs over. This lets a policy distinguish "the packet arrived over tunnel A but the underlay used NIC `eth0`" from "the underlay used `eth1`". The field is useful for debugging multi-NIC plus multi-tunnel topologies, and for tracking the source physical port during a security audit.

### 4.8.15.2 `ipv6_flabel`, IPv6 Flow Label

**Bucket:** L3 IPv6 QoS.
**Bits:** 20-bit (the low 20 bits of a 32-bit field in the IPv6 header).
**Maskable:** No.
**Writable:** No (read-only).
**Prerequisites:** `eth_type=0x86dd` (IPv6).
**OpenFlow version introduced:** OF 1.2 and later (`OXM_OF_IPV6_FLABEL`).
**OVS version introduced:** OVS 1.11 and later.
**NXM/OXM:** `OXM_OF_IPV6_FLABEL` (class `0x8000`, type 28).
**Example:**

```
priority=100,ipv6,ipv6_flabel=0x12345,actions=output:2
```

**How it works in practice:** the Flow Label (RFC 6437) is set by the sender to identify a single IPv6 flow. Its primary use case is **ECMP hashing** on intermediate routers and switches. Matching `ipv6_flabel` lets a policy work by flow ID without inspecting the 5-tuple. The field is useful for QoS classification (for example, `flow_label = 0x100000` lands in the premium queue) or traffic engineering (force a flow onto a specific path). Compared to IPv4: IPv4 has no Flow Label, so a router has to hash the 5-tuple for ECMP. This is one of the reasons IPv6 improves core-network performance.

### 4.8.15.3 `ipv6_exthdr`, IPv6 Extension Headers bitmap

**Bucket:** L3 IPv6.
**Bits:** 9-bit (encoded inside a 16-bit field).
**Maskable:** Yes (CIDR-style mask).
**Writable:** No.
**Prerequisites:** `eth_type=0x86dd`.
**OpenFlow version introduced:** OF 1.2 and later (`OXM_OF_IPV6_EXTHDR`).
**OVS version introduced:** OVS 1.11 and later.
**NXM/OXM:** `OXM_OF_IPV6_EXTHDR` (class `0x8000`, type 39).
**Example:**

```
priority=200,ipv6,ipv6_exthdr=0x4/0x4,actions=drop
```

**How it works in practice:** this 9-bit bitmap reports which IPv6 extension headers are present (Hop-by-Hop, Routing, Fragment, Destination Options, AH, ESP, and so on). For the bit assignment see section 4.8.7.3. The main use case is a **security policy that blocks packets carrying unusual extension headers** (Routing Type 0 was deprecated after the RH0 attack, but it can still appear). The expression `ipv6_exthdr=0x4/0x4` (bit 2 means Fragment) drops every fragmented IPv6 packet, a counter to fragmentation-based evasion. OVS implements this by parsing the extension-header chain on the slow path. Not every NIC that offloads Geneve or VXLAN supports extension-header parsing.

### 4.8.15.4 `pbb_isid`, Provider Backbone Bridge I-SID

**Bucket:** L2 Provider Bridge (802.1ah).
**Bits:** 24-bit Service Instance ID.
**Maskable:** Yes.
**Writable:** Yes (`set_field:0x123456->pbb_isid`).
**Prerequisites:** PBB encapsulation (`eth_type=0x88e7`, the MAC-in-MAC EtherType).
**OpenFlow version introduced:** OF 1.4 and later (`OXM_OF_PBB_ISID`, optional capability).
**OVS version introduced:** OVS 2.6 and later.
**NXM/OXM:** `OXM_OF_PBB_ISID` (class `0x8000`, type 37).
**Example:**

```
priority=100,eth_type=0x88e7,pbb_isid=0x012345,actions=output:2
```

**How it works in practice:** PBB (IEEE 802.1ah) is a carrier-Ethernet standard for service providers. It encapsulates a customer frame inside a backbone frame, with a 24-bit I-SID identifying the service instance. The backbone frame adds a 22-byte header in front of the customer Ethernet header: B-DA, B-SA, B-Tag (the backbone VLAN), and I-Tag (which carries the I-SID). The provider uses the I-SID to map a customer service onto the backbone topology. Matching `pbb_isid` lets a core switch forward by service ID instead of by customer VLAN, which solves the 4096-VLAN limit.

> **Curriculum scope:** PBB is a highly specialised carrier feature and is rare in datacenter overlays (OVN does not use PBB, it uses Geneve and VXLAN). The field is included here so the catalog covers the OF specification fully, but real OVS deployments rarely use it.

### 4.8.15.5 `mpls_tc`, MPLS Traffic Class

**Bucket:** MPLS QoS.
**Bits:** 3-bit Traffic Class field in the MPLS shim header.
**Maskable:** No.
**Writable:** Yes (`set_field:5->mpls_tc`).
**Prerequisites:** MPLS (`eth_type=0x8847` unicast or `0x8848` multicast).
**OpenFlow version introduced:** OF 1.2 and later (`OXM_OF_MPLS_TC`).
**OVS version introduced:** OVS 1.11 and later.
**NXM/OXM:** `OXM_OF_MPLS_TC` (class `0x8000`, type 35).
**Example:**

```
priority=100,eth_type=0x8847,mpls_label=100,mpls_tc=5,actions=set_queue:1,output:2
```

**How it works in practice:** the MPLS shim header (4 bytes) carries: a 20-bit Label, a 3-bit TC, a 1-bit BoS, and an 8-bit TTL. The TC field (also called "EXP" in older RFCs) is used for QoS marking and maps to a DiffServ class. Matching `mpls_tc` lets a core switch prioritise traffic by class (TC=5 means EF, Expedited Forwarding, used for voice). The action `set_field:N->mpls_tc` rewrites this value at the ingress edge router to mark a packet entering the MPLS domain.

### 4.8.15.6 `mpls_bos`, MPLS Bottom of Stack

**Bucket:** MPLS.
**Bits:** 1-bit (0 means another label follows, 1 means this is the last label in the stack).
**Maskable:** No.
**Writable:** No (read-only, derived from the stack depth).
**Prerequisites:** MPLS.
**OpenFlow version introduced:** OF 1.3 and later (`OXM_OF_MPLS_BOS`).
**OVS version introduced:** OVS 1.11 and later.
**NXM/OXM:** `OXM_OF_MPLS_BOS` (class `0x8000`, type 36).
**Example:**

```
priority=100,eth_type=0x8847,mpls_bos=1,actions=pop_mpls:0x0800,goto_table:5
```

**How it works in practice:** MPLS can stack multiple labels (a label switching path can run inside another tunnel). When BoS equals 1, the current label is the last one in the stack. After this label is popped, the packet returns to the underlying Ethernet or IP frame. Matching `mpls_bos=1` is common at the **edge router** to know when all labels have been popped and the original L2 or L3 packet has been restored. The action `pop_mpls:<ethertype>` only works when BoS equals 1 (it pops the last label and resets `eth_type` to the supplied value).

### 4.8.15.7 `sctp_src` and `sctp_dst`, SCTP source and destination ports

**Bucket:** L4 Transport.
**Bits:** 16-bit each.
**Maskable:** Yes (range mask).
**Writable:** Yes (`set_field:5060->sctp_dst`).
**Prerequisites:** SCTP (`eth_type=0x0800` or `0x86dd`, plus `ip_proto=132`).
**OpenFlow version introduced:** OF 1.2 and later (`OXM_OF_SCTP_SRC` and `OXM_OF_SCTP_DST`).
**OVS version introduced:** OVS 2.0 and later.
**NXM/OXM:** `OXM_OF_SCTP_SRC` (type 17) and `OXM_OF_SCTP_DST` (type 18).
**Example:**

```
priority=100,ip,ip_proto=132,sctp_dst=2905,actions=output:2
```

**How it works in practice:** SCTP (RFC 4960) is the third major L4 protocol after TCP and UDP. It is used mainly for telecom signalling (Diameter, M3UA, SIGTRAN). It is uncommon in datacenters, but telecom workloads (5G core, IMS) running in OpenStack containers may need OVN policy that matches SCTP. SCTP brings multistreaming, multihoming, and a built-in heartbeat. OVN ACLs currently support only port-based SCTP matching (they do not inspect SCTP chunks).

### 4.8.15.8 `tunnel_id` (alias `tun_id`), 64-bit Tunnel ID

**Bucket:** Tunnel metadata.
**Bits:** 64-bit.
**Maskable:** Yes.
**Writable:** Yes (`set_field:0x5678->tun_id`).
**Prerequisites:** None (always available; the value is 0 if the packet did not arrive over a tunnel).
**OpenFlow version introduced:** OF 1.3 and later (`OXM_OF_TUNNEL_ID`).
**OVS version introduced:** OVS 1.1 and later (`NXM_NX_TUN_ID`); OVS 2.4 and later (`OXM_OF_TUNNEL_ID`).
**NXM/OXM:** `NXM_NX_TUN_ID` (class `0x0001`, type 16) or `OXM_OF_TUNNEL_ID` (class `0x8000`, type 38).
**Example:**

```
priority=100,tun_id=0x1234,actions=set_field:0x5->reg0,goto_table:8
```

**How it works in practice:** `tunnel_id` is 64-bit metadata attached to a packet that arrived over a tunnel port. The encoding depends on the tunnel type:
- **VXLAN:** the 24-bit VNI sits in the low 24 bits; the high 40 bits are zero.
- **Geneve:** the 24-bit VNI is in the same position as VXLAN.
- **GRE with the key option:** a 32-bit GRE key in the low 32 bits.
- **STT:** the full 64-bit context ID.

When a packet leaves `br-int` through a tunnel port, `ovs-vswitchd` encapsulates it with the VNI or key set to the value of `tunnel_id` from the flow rule that ran before the output. OVN uses `tunnel_id` to carry the logical datapath ID (the logical switch or router), and combines it with `reg14` and `reg15` (the logical inport and outport) to form the full pipeline metadata. This is the core of cross-chassis transitions in OVN.

### 4.8.15.9 `xreg0` through `xreg7`, extended 64-bit registers

**Bucket:** Pipeline state register.
**Bits:** 64-bit each (8 registers).
**Maskable:** Yes.
**Writable:** Yes.
**Prerequisites:** None.
**OpenFlow version introduced:** OF 1.3 and later (`OXM_OF_PKT_REG`).
**OVS version introduced:** OVS 2.4 and later.
**NXM/OXM:** `OXM_OF_PKT_REG0` through `OXM_OF_PKT_REG7` (class `0x0001`, types 0 through 7).
**Example:**

```
priority=100,actions=load:0xCAFEBABE12345678->xreg0,goto_table:5
table=5,xreg0=0xCAFEBABE12345678,actions=output:2
```

**How it works in practice:** `xreg0` through `xreg7` are 64-bit aliases for pairs of consecutive 32-bit registers (`xreg0 = reg0 + reg1`, `xreg1 = reg2 + reg3`, and so on). There are only 8 xreg slots because there are only 16 physical 32-bit registers. The aliases let a flow rule match or set a 64-bit value as a single chunk (for example, a 48-bit MAC address fits in a 64-bit register). OVN uses xreg for IPv4 next-hop scratch (4 bytes of address plus a 48-bit MAC do not fit in a single 32-bit register).

### 4.8.15.10 `xxreg0` through `xxreg3`, extended 128-bit registers

**Bucket:** Pipeline state register.
**Bits:** 128-bit each (4 registers).
**Maskable:** Yes.
**Writable:** Yes.
**Prerequisites:** None.
**OpenFlow version introduced:** An OVS extension (not standard OF).
**OVS version introduced:** OVS 2.6 and later.
**NXM/OXM:** Custom OVS extension (no class assigned in the OF specification).
**Example:**

```
priority=100,actions=load:0x20010db8000000000000000000000001->xxreg0,goto_table:5
```

**How it works in practice:** `xxreg0` through `xxreg3` are 128-bit aliases for groups of four consecutive 32-bit registers (`xxreg0 = reg0 + reg1 + reg2 + reg3`, `xxreg1 = reg4 + reg5 + reg6 + reg7`, and so on). They are especially useful as **IPv6 address scratch** (a 128-bit address fits in a single xxreg). OVN uses `xxreg0` for IPv6 ND and NA processing (see Part 13.17 §13.17.2). Be careful: writing to `xxreg0` overwrites `reg0`, `reg1`, `reg2`, and `reg3` at the same time, which destroys any REGBIT previously set in `reg0`.

### 4.8.15.11 NSH fields, `nsh_spi`, `nsh_si`, `nsh_c1` through `nsh_c4`

**Bucket:** Service Function Chaining (NSH, RFC 8300).
**Bits:** `nsh_spi` is 24-bit, `nsh_si` is 8-bit, `nsh_c1` through `nsh_c4` are 32-bit each.
**Maskable:** Yes for `nsh_c1` through `nsh_c4`.
**Writable:** Yes.
**Prerequisites:** NSH (`eth_type=0x894f`, or `packet_type=(1, 0x894f)` for OF 1.5 and later packet-type-aware pipelines).
**OpenFlow version introduced:** OF 1.5 and later with an experimenter extension (NSH was not standardised in the OF base).
**OVS version introduced:** OVS 2.8 and later.
**NXM/OXM:** Experimenter class with NSH-specific types.
**Example:**

```
priority=100,nsh,nsh_spi=0x123,nsh_si=255,actions=output:2
priority=100,nsh,nsh_si=128,actions=set_field:0xdeadbeef->nsh_c1,goto_table:5
```

**How it works in practice:** NSH (Network Service Header, RFC 8300) is the protocol for **Service Function Chaining (SFC)**, the practice of forwarding a packet through a chain of services (firewall, IDS, load balancer, DPI). The NSH header carries:
- **SPI (Service Path Identifier),** 24-bit: identifies the chain.
- **SI (Service Index),** 8-bit: the current position in the chain (decremented at each service hop).
- **C1 through C4** (Context Headers), 32-bit each: service-specific metadata (for example tenant ID or application class).

Use case: a cloud provider deploys SFC for a tenant. A packet enters with `SPI=1` (the "web tenant A" chain), traverses `SI=255` (firewall), then `SI=254` (IDS), then `SI=253` (output). Each service decrements `SI` and forwards to the next service. NSH defines an overlay logic that is independent of the physical path, instead of hard-coding the topology.

The curriculum only introduces NSH at a shallow level so the catalog can cover the OF specification fully. Real OVN production deployments usually do NOT use NSH; they use simpler techniques such as a chain of logical routers.

### 4.8.15.12 `packet_type`, packet-type-aware pipeline (OF 1.5 and later)

**Bucket:** Pipeline metadata.
**Bits:** 32-bit (16-bit namespace plus 16-bit type).
**Maskable:** No.
**Writable:** Yes (the `decap` action resets the value).
**Prerequisites:** None (always available, defaults to `(0, 0)` for Ethernet).
**OpenFlow version introduced:** OF 1.5 and later (`OXM_OF_PACKET_TYPE`).
**OVS version introduced:** OVS 2.8 and later.
**NXM/OXM:** `OXM_OF_PACKET_TYPE` (class `0x8000`, type 44).
**Example:**

```
# Match a bare IPv4 packet (no Ethernet header)
priority=100,packet_type=(1,0x0800),ip_proto=6,actions=encap(ethernet),output:2
```

**Common values:**

| `packet_type` | Encapsulation |
|---------------|---------------|
| `(0, 0)` | Ethernet (default) |
| `(0, 0x8847)` | MPLS unicast |
| `(0, 0x86dd)` | IPv6 |
| `(1, 2)` | Bare IPv4 (no Ethernet) |
| `(1, 0x894f)` | NSH |

**How it works in practice:** OF 1.0 through 1.4 assume that every packet has an Ethernet header. OF 1.5 and later, with a packet-type-aware pipeline, can match and process non-Ethernet packets (bare IP, NSH, MPLS-only). Matching `packet_type=(1,2)` lets the pipeline process a bare IPv4 packet (for example, the output of an IPsec tunnel in transport mode). The `encap(ethernet)` action adds an Ethernet header back so the packet can be sent out a normal port. Main use cases: SFC and NSH integration, IPsec gateways, and GTP-U tunneling for 5G.

---

## 4.8.16 Prerequisite chain update for the 12 new fields

This table extends 4.8.13 with the 12 fields backfilled above:

| New field | Prerequisite | OXM type code |
|-----------|--------------|---------------|
| `in_phy_port` | (none, always available) | 1 |
| `ipv6_flabel` | `eth_type=0x86dd` | 28 |
| `ipv6_exthdr` | `eth_type=0x86dd` | 39 |
| `pbb_isid` | `eth_type=0x88e7` | 37 |
| `mpls_tc` | `eth_type=0x8847` or `0x8848` | 35 |
| `mpls_bos` | `eth_type=0x8847` or `0x8848` | 36 |
| `sctp_src/dst` | `eth_type=0x0800` or `0x86dd`, `ip_proto=132` | 17 and 18 |
| `tunnel_id` | (none, default 0) | 38 |
| `xreg0..7` | (none) | OF 1.5 `PKT_REG` types 0 through 7 |
| `xxreg0..3` | (none) | OVS extension |
| `nsh_spi/si/c1-c4` | `nsh` (`eth_type=0x894f` or `packet_type=(1,0x894f)`) | Experimenter |
| `packet_type` | (none, default `(0,0)`) | 44 |

## 4.8.14 Final scenario, lazy wildcarding in practice

**Context:** Part 9.2 §9.2.2 introduces lazy wildcarding. This section demonstrates it directly with a trace.

**Setup:**

```shell
$ sudo ovs-vsctl add-br br-lazy
$ sudo ovs-vsctl add-port br-lazy tap0 -- set Interface tap0 type=internal
$ sudo ovs-ofctl -O OpenFlow15 add-flow br-lazy \
    "table=0,priority=100,tcp,tp_dst=80,actions=output:LOCAL"
```

**Trace with a TCP packet to port 80:**

```shell
$ sudo ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-lazy \
    "in_port=tap0,dl_src=aa:bb:cc:00:00:01,dl_dst=aa:bb:cc:00:00:02,\
     dl_type=0x0800,nw_src=10.1.0.5,nw_dst=10.1.0.10,nw_proto=6,\
     tp_src=50000,tp_dst=80"

Flow: tcp,in_port=1,vlan_tci=0x0000,dl_src=aa:bb:cc:00:00:01,
      dl_dst=aa:bb:cc:00:00:02,nw_src=10.1.0.5,nw_dst=10.1.0.10,
      nw_tos=0,nw_ecn=0,nw_ttl=0,tp_src=50000,tp_dst=80,tcp_flags=0

bridge("br-lazy")
-----------------
 0. tcp,tp_dst=80, priority 100
    output:LOCAL

Final flow: unchanged
Megaflow: recirc_id=0,eth,tcp,tp_dst=0x50/0xffff
Datapath actions: 65534
```

**Anatomy of the megaflow line in the trace:**

```
Megaflow: recirc_id=0,eth,tcp,tp_dst=0x50/0xffff
          ^^^^^^^^^^^  ^^^  ^^^ ^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^
          always       added by added by  exact match on port 80
          inspected    the tcp  the tcp
                       prereq   prereq
                       (eth)    (nw_proto=6)
```

Fields that were inspected (not wildcarded):
- `recirc_id`, implicit (always inspected).
- `eth_type`, prerequisite of `tcp`.
- `nw_proto`, prerequisite of `tcp`.
- `tp_dst=0x50/0xffff`, the rule matches port 80 exactly.

Fields that were NOT inspected (wildcarded):
- `eth_src` and `eth_dst`, not matched in the rule.
- `nw_src` and `nw_dst`, not matched.
- `tp_src`, not matched.
- `tcp_flags`, `nw_ttl`, and `nw_tos`, not matched.

The kernel installs the megaflow with a match on only `tp_dst=80` plus mask `0xffff`, and wildcards every other field. One megaflow then covers an unbounded number of packets with different source IPs, source ports, or MAC addresses, as long as the destination port is 80.

This is lazy wildcarding in action. It connects the theory in Part 9.2 §9.2.2 to the OpenFlow field catalog of this part.

---

## Key takeaways

First, OpenFlow grew from a hard-coded 12-tuple in OF 1.0 to a 100-plus extensible field set in OF 1.5 plus NXM, through the OXM TLV. Part 4.1 tells the history of that transition. This part lists the resulting catalog.

Second, the 12 foundation field groups are: Metadata, Register, Tunnel, L2, ARP, IPv4, IPv6, L4, ICMP, MPLS, Conntrack, and `packet_type`. An operator should memorise the groups, not every field individually. Look up `ovs-fields(7)` when you need a specific field.

Third, the 9-attribute anatomy (Name, Width, Format, Masking, Prerequisites, Access, OpenFlow version, OXM/NXM, Semantics) is the canonical mental model for any field. When debugging a flow rule that does not match, always verify: is the prerequisite set? Is the mask correct? Does the OpenFlow version support the field?

Fourth, registers (`reg0` through `reg15`, plus xreg and xxreg) are not part of the wire packet. They are per-packet scratch pads in the pipeline. OVN reserves a fixed register map (`reg13` for the datapath, `reg14` for the ingress port, `reg15` for the egress port). An OVN operator must memorise this map.

Fifth, conntrack fields (`ct_state`, `ct_zone`, `ct_mark`, `ct_label`, `ct_nw_*`) only match after the packet has passed through a `ct()` action. This is the key distinction from stateless matching: no ct-prefixed field is checked for a packet that has not yet been tracked.

Sixth, conjunctive match (`conj_id` plus the `conjunction()` action) compresses cross-product rules from N times M down to N plus M. OVN ACLs apply this pattern to `Port_Group` cross-products extensively. Understanding conjunction lets you read `ovn-sbctl lflow-list` output without being overwhelmed by a flood of `conjunction(X, Y/Z)` lines.

---

## 4.8.X Deep dive into the medium-tier match fields

This section is for the medium-tier match fields, the ones that appear in production rules very frequently but are not absolutely foundational the way `eth_dst` is for the L2_LKUP stage. Each field gets its own subsection below, presented under a 20-aspect treatment using natural English headings: concept, history and background, position in the architecture, role, why it exists, problems it solves, importance, how it works, OXM TLV byte layout, match and write examples, how an operator masters this, classification, source-code anchor, failure scenarios, synthetic exercise, and comparison with other systems.

The L2 group (5 fields: `in_port`, `eth_src`, `eth_dst`, `eth_type`, `vlan_vid`) is covered in §4.8.X.1 through §4.8.X.5. The secondary L3, L4, and conntrack groups will be added in later expansion passes. Until then, the quick-reference content lives in the native sections: §4.8.6 (L3 IPv4), §4.8.7 (L3 IPv6), §4.8.8 (L4), and §4.8.11 (conntrack).

### 4.8.X.1 `in_port`, the ingress port of a packet entering the datapath

**Concept.** `in_port` is a match field that identifies the **physical or logical port** through which a packet entered the OVS datapath. It is the most foundational field in all of OpenFlow: roughly 90 percent of production flow rules carry `in_port` on the match side, because the ingress port is the first natural distinction between traffic from different tap interfaces, different tunnels, or different uplinks. In OVN, the `in_port` value on `br-int` is the `tunnel_key` of the Logical_Switch_Port that `ovn-controller` mapped at table 0. Field type: **fundamental metadata**, subtype "ingress identifier".

**History and background.** OpenFlow 1.0 (2009) defined `OFPFW_IN_PORT` as 16-bit. OpenFlow 1.1 (2011) widened it to 32-bit to support deployments with more than 65k ports (chassis carrying many SR-IOV VFs). OpenFlow 1.2 (2011) standardised it as the OXM TLV `OXM_OF_IN_PORT` (`oxm_class = 0x8000 OPENFLOW_BASIC`, `oxm_field = 0`, length 4 bytes). Between 2010 and 2014, OVS used the legacy NXM form (`NXM_OF_IN_PORT`, 2 bytes) alongside the OXM standard. Starting with OVS 2.3 (2014), `in_port` is serialised as OXM by default whenever the controller speaks OF 1.2 or later.

**Position in the architecture.** The field belongs to the metadata group. It is not a true packet header. The datapath assigns the value when the packet enters, and the value is not part of the Ethernet byte stream. In the `struct flow` definition (`include/openvswitch/flow.h`), the corresponding member is `in_port.ofp_port` (a 32-bit OpenFlow port number). The upstream OVS MFF identifiers are `MFF_IN_PORT` (in `meta-flow.h`) for the 16-bit legacy form and `MFF_IN_PORT_OXM` for the 32-bit OXM form.

**Role.** Primary role: distinguish the packet's source so that policies can differ per source (for example, a packet from a VM tap `tap0` is subject to tenant A's ACL, while a packet from uplink `eth1` is subject to the underlay ACL). Secondary role: parameter for the `IN_PORT` action (the reserved port `0xfff8`), used when a packet must be sent back out the same ingress port (hairpin, some NDP scenarios). Scope: per packet, fixed throughout the pipeline (not overwritten by recirculation).

**Why it exists.** Before OpenFlow, switch silicon already had an "ingress port" concept inside the CAM lookup, but it was not exposed to a programmable control plane. OpenFlow generalised this concept into a first-class field, so a controller can write a flow such as `priority=100,in_port=2,actions=output:5` without knowing which switch silicon sits underneath. It was one of the 12 original fields of OpenFlow 1.0 (alongside 6 L2 fields, 4 L3 fields, and 2 L4 fields in the "OFP_MATCH" structure).

**Problems it solves.** Concretely: replacing static bridge port-mapping configuration (such as Linux bridge `brctl addif`) with runtime-programmable flow rules. The mechanism: the datapath assigns `in_port` at packet entry; the classifier matches the field like any other field; the `output:N` action specifies the egress port; the reserved `IN_PORT` action allows sending back out the ingress port. Scope: every multi-port forwarding scenario, from basic L2 learning to sophisticated SDN policy.

**Importance.** A medium-tier field by the catalog scale, but extremely high-frequency. It appears in nearly every flow on `br-int` (OVS) and `br-ex` (the OVN gateway chassis). An operator must know: the difference between numeric and named port values, the reserved ports (`LOCAL=0xfffe`, `CONTROLLER=0xfffd`, `NORMAL=0xfffa`, `FLOOD=0xfffb`, `ALL=0xfffc`, `IN_PORT=0xfff8`, `ANY=0xffffffff`, `TABLE=0xfff9`), and the fact that a numeric port number can change after `ovs-vswitchd` restarts if the interface does not have a persistent ofport.

**How it works.** When a packet enters through a netdev (kernel datapath) or a DPDK port (userspace datapath), the datapath reads the `dp_port` from context (kernel: `OVS_KEY_ATTR_IN_PORT` inside the netlink attribute; userspace: directly from the port table) and translates it to `ofp_port` through a lookup table in `ofproto/ofproto-dpif.c`. The translated value lands in `struct flow.in_port.ofp_port` and then enters the classifier TSS lookup. The `in_port=N` match is an exact 32-bit comparison and does not allow a mask (per OF 1.5 spec §A.2.3.1).

**OXM TLV byte layout.** A 4-byte header: `oxm_class` is 2 bytes equal to `0x8000` (OPENFLOW_BASIC); `oxm_field|hasmask` is 1 byte equal to `0x00` (field=0, hasmask=0); `oxm_length` is 1 byte equal to `0x04`. Then comes a 4-byte big-endian port number. Total TLV: 8 bytes. Compare with `OXM_OF_IN_PHY_PORT` (`oxm_field=1`, used on bonded or aggregate ports to return the real physical port). The `in_phy_port` field is analysed in section 4.8.15.1.

**How an operator masters this.** Four skills: (1) **Read**: `ovs-ofctl show br-int | grep "<port_name>"` to map port name to ofport number before writing the flow. (2) **Edit**: prefer the port name (`in_port=tap0`) in manually authored rules so the rule does not break when the ofport renumbers; for controller-installed rules, the numeric form is fine because the controller maintains its own mapping. (3) **Observe**: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep in_port=` counts flows per ingress port. (4) **Debug**: `ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=2,...'` simulates a packet entering port 2 and shows its path through the pipeline.

**Classification.** A metadata field (not a header), exact-match only (no mask), read-only in the match clause. Writing through `set_field` is possible only in the very rare OF 1.5 packet-out scenario. No prerequisite. Different from `metadata` (which can be written by `Write-Metadata`) and `reg0` through `reg15` (which can be written freely by `set_field`).

**Match examples.** OVS `ovs-ofctl` syntax:

```
priority=100,in_port=1,actions=output:2                    # ingress port 1 to port 2
priority=100,in_port=tap0,actions=resubmit(,10)            # port name (text)
priority=100,in_port=LOCAL,actions=ct(commit),NORMAL       # bridge-internal port
priority=100,in_port=2,actions=in_port                     # hairpin: send back out port 2
```

OXM hexstream syntax (used by debug tools):

```
NXM:  04 00 00 02         # NXM_OF_IN_PORT, length=2, port=2
OXM:  80 00 00 04 00 00 00 02   # OXM_OF_IN_PORT, length=4, port=2
```

**Observation and verification.** Three methods: (1) `ovs-ofctl show br-int` lists every current ofport with its name, link state, and peer info. (2) `ovs-vsctl --columns=name,ofport list interface` reads the mapping from OVSDB. (3) `ovs-appctl dpctl/show` shows the datapath-level `dp_port`. (`dp_port` is internal to the kernel or userspace datapath and differs from the ofport.)

**Source code reference.** Function anchors `ofp_to_u16` and `u16_to_ofp` (OVS `lib/ofp-util.c`) handle 16-bit and 32-bit conversion for compatibility. Function `extract_metadata_from_packet_metadata` (`lib/odp-util.c`) extracts `dp_port` from the datapath netlink attribute. Branch baseline: OVS v2.17.9.

**Failure scenarios.** (a) Using `in_port=1` numerically in a manual rule, then restarting `ovs-vswitchd`: ofport 1 may now be a different interface, and the rule matches the wrong target. Fix: use the port name. (b) Using `in_port=LOCAL` in a production rule on `br-int`: when a VM on the same host injects a packet through the Linux stack, the rule matches LOCAL, but a trace may be misread as a tunnel. Fix: add an L3 condition that distinguishes the two cases.

**Synthetic exercise.** A Mininet topology with one OVS bridge `s1` and three hosts `h1`, `h2`, `h3`. Use `ovs-ofctl add-flow s1 'priority=100,in_port=1,actions=output:2'` to allow `h1` to `h2`, plus `priority=100,in_port=2,actions=output:1` for `h2` to `h1`. Verify that `h1` and `h2` can ping each other, and that `h1` and `h3` cannot (because there is no flow `in_port=1` to port 3).

**Comparison with other systems.** The Linux bridge has an "incoming bridge port" notion in `br_input.c`, but it is not exposed in rules; it is only used internally for FDB learning. A classic Cisco IOS ACL on an interface implicitly binds to the inbound interface (similar to `in_port`) through the command `ip access-group N in`. NSX-T has `source_logical_port_uuid` in distributed firewall rules, semantically equivalent to `in_port` mapped through a Logical_Switch_Port. P4 models the same idea as `standard_metadata.ingress_port` in `v1model.p4`, inherited directly from the OpenFlow concept.

---

### 4.8.X.2 `eth_src`, the source MAC address of an Ethernet frame

**Concept.** `eth_src` is a 48-bit match field that holds the source MAC address of an Ethernet frame (6 bytes at byte offset 6 through 11 in the Ethernet header). Legacy alias: `dl_src` (data-link source). The field matters for three problems: anti-spoofing (block MAC spoofing by a tenant), MAC learning (the FDB of an L2 switch), and identity-based ACL (filter by host identity). Field type: **L2 header field**, subtype "source identifier".

**History and background.** OpenFlow 1.0 (2009) defined `OFPMT_DL_SRC` as 6 bytes in the match struct, with exact match but no mask. OpenFlow 1.1 (2011) added bitwise masks for source and destination MAC (especially useful for matching the multicast bit, `01:00:00:00:00:00/01:00:00:00:00:00`). OF 1.2 (2011) standardised the OXM forms `OXM_OF_ETH_SRC` (`oxm_field=4`) and `OXM_OF_ETH_SRC_W` (the masked variant, `hasmask=1`).

**Position in the architecture.** In `struct flow` the field is `dl_src` (a 6-byte array). The upstream OVS MFF identifier is `MFF_ETH_SRC` (in `meta-flow.h`). In the taxonomy of section 4.8, this field belongs to Group D, L2 Ethernet fields (alongside `eth_dst`, `eth_type`, and the VLAN fields).

**Role.** Primary role in OVN: enforce `Logical_Switch_Port.port_security` at stage `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2`. `northd` compiles this into a flow `match=(inport=="lsp1" && eth.src=={MAC1, MAC2})` with priority 50 and action `next`. A packet that does not match falls through to a priority-0 drop. Role in standalone OVS: input to the MAC learning table (FDB). The action `learn(table=10, priority=100, NXM_OF_ETH_DST[]=NXM_OF_ETH_SRC[], output:NXM_OF_IN_PORT[])` learns the source MAC and ingress port so that a future packet with this destination MAC knows the egress port.

**Why it exists.** Before OpenFlow, switch silicon learned the source MAC implicitly through hardware "MAC learning", and the control plane could not control or block this. OpenFlow exposed the source MAC as a first-class field for three reasons: (a) controller-driven learning instead of self-learning, (b) programmable anti-spoofing rules, and (c) identity-based policy. It is one of the 4 original L2 fields in OF 1.0.

**Problems it solves.** Concretely, MAC spoofing: a tenant inside a VM can send a packet with `eth.src` faking another VM's MAC (the Linux kernel tap driver does not filter source MAC). Consequences: ARP cache poisoning, MITM attacks. The OVN solution: declare an allowed-MAC list in `Logical_Switch_Port.port_security`. `northd` compiles this into a drop flow at stage `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2`. Scope: tenant boundary at the tap port. It does not protect against spoofing inside the overlay tunnel (the tunnel endpoint must be trusted).

**Importance.** Medium tier. In OVN, every LSP that has port security generates an `eth.src` match flow at stage 0 of the logical pipeline. In standalone OVS with a learning bridge, every packet that passes through a `learn` flow matches `eth.src`. An operator must know: the MAC format (hex with colon separators), byte-level bitwise masks, the multicast bit (bit 0 of byte 0), and the unicast or locally-administered bit (bit 1 of byte 0).

**How it works.** The datapath parses the Ethernet header and extracts bytes 6 through 11 into `flow.dl_src`. The classifier TSS compares either all 6 bytes exactly, or with a bitwise mask if the rule has one. With a mask, the packet matches when `(packet.dl_src & rule.mask) == (rule.dl_src & rule.mask)`. OXM TLV layout: a 4-byte header (`oxm_class=0x8000`, `oxm_field=4`, `hasmask=0`, `length=6`) plus 6 bytes of value. With a mask: `length=12` plus 6 bytes of value plus 6 bytes of mask.

**Bitwise mask use cases.** (a) Match all multicast or broadcast: `eth_src=01:00:00:00:00:00/01:00:00:00:00:00` matches when bit 0 of byte 0 is 1. (b) Match an OUI vendor: `eth_src=00:50:56:00:00:00/ff:ff:ff:00:00:00` matches every MAC that starts with `00:50:56` (VMware's OUI). (c) Match locally-administered MACs: `eth_src=02:00:00:00:00:00/02:00:00:00:00:00` matches when bit 1 of byte 0 is 1.

**Match and write examples.**

```
# Anti-spoofing for tenant 1: only allow the 2 declared MACs
priority=50,in_port=tap0,eth_src=AA:11:22:33:44:55,actions=resubmit(,10)
priority=50,in_port=tap0,eth_src=AA:11:22:33:44:56,actions=resubmit(,10)
priority=0,in_port=tap0,actions=drop

# Multicast detection
priority=100,eth_src=01:00:00:00:00:00/01:00:00:00:00:00,actions=drop  # invalid multicast source

# MAC rewrite (set_field write)
priority=100,actions=set_field:00:11:22:33:44:55->eth_src,output:2

# Legacy alias
priority=100,dl_src=00:50:56:aa:bb:cc,actions=output:3
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: understand the MAC format and the bit semantics. (2) **Edit**: write rewrite rules with `set_field:...->eth_src`, or the legacy `mod_dl_src:...`. (3) **Observe**: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep eth_src` counts hits per source MAC. (4) **Debug**: `ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,eth_src=AA:11:22:33:44:55,...'` shows which rule matches.

**Source code reference.** Function anchor `mf_set_value` (`lib/meta-flow.c`) for the `MFF_ETH_SRC` write path. Function `parse_dl_src` (`lib/match.c`) parses the text input. Branch baseline: OVS v2.17.9.

**Failure scenario.** Forgetting `port_security` when creating an LSP lets the tenant spoof the gateway MAC and run an MITM attack on the subnet. Fix: run `ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security lsp1 "AA:11:22:33:44:55"` immediately after creating the LSP, and audit every LSP periodically with `lsp-get-port-security`.

**Synthetic exercise.** An OVN topology with one Logical_Switch and two LSPs. Set port security on `lsp-good` (MAC matches the VM) but leave `lsp-bad` empty. From a VM on `lsp-bad`, send a packet with `eth.src=AA:11:11:11:11:11` (spoofed). Verify the packet passes (because empty port security means allow all). Run `lsp-set-port-security lsp-bad "AA:22:22:22:22:22"`, then send the spoofed packet again. Verify it is dropped at table 8.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables `-m mac --mac-source AA:11:...` on the `FORWARD` chain in bridge mode is equivalent. Cisco port-security `switchport port-security mac-address AA11.2233.4455` on an access port plays the same role. NSX-T `LogicalPort.address_bindings`, the `mac_address` field, is semantically equivalent to OVN's port_security.

---

### 4.8.X.3 `eth_dst`, the destination MAC address of an Ethernet frame

**Concept.** `eth_dst` is a 48-bit match field that holds the destination MAC address (bytes 0 through 5 of the Ethernet header). Legacy alias: `dl_dst`. This is the central field for the L2 forwarding decision: a switch looks up its FDB by `eth_dst` to learn the egress port. In OVN, `eth_dst` is the input to stage `LS_IN_L2_LKUP` (table 27), the foundational stage of the Logical_Switch pipeline. Field type: **L2 header field**, subtype "destination identifier".

**History and background.** Same history as `eth_src`: OF 1.0 exact, OF 1.1 with mask, OF 1.2 OXM (`OXM_OF_ETH_DST` `field=3`, masked variant `_W` with `hasmask=1`). OVS adds the legacy `NXM_OF_ETH_DST`. Using a mask on `eth_dst` is especially common for multicast groups (the IPv4 multicast map `01:00:5e:XX:XX:XX`, the IPv6 multicast map `33:33:XX:XX:XX:XX`).

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.dl_dst` is 6 bytes. The MFF identifier is `MFF_ETH_DST` (in `meta-flow.h`). In the taxonomy of section 4.8 it lives in Group D, L2 Ethernet.

**Role.** Primary role: the L2 forwarding decision. In OVN, stage `LS_IN_L2_LKUP` (table 27) emits one flow per LSP with match `(eth.dst == AA:11:...)` and action `outport = lsp1; output;`. This is the "MAC table" of the distributed virtual switch. In standalone OVS with learning, the `NORMAL` action looks up the FDB by `eth.dst` automatically; in a controller-driven setup, a `learn()`-installed flow does the same. Secondary role: detect broadcast (`eth_dst=ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff`) for ARP request flooding, and detect multicast for IGMP snooping.

**Why it exists.** L2 forwarding is the most fundamental function of a switch, so OpenFlow could not omit this field. Since OF 1.0 it has been at the top of the match struct (alongside `eth_src` and `eth_type`). Before OpenFlow, switch silicon handled hardware MAC learning and lookup itself, with no programmable interface. OpenFlow lets the controller intervene in this entire logic.

**Problems it solves.** Concretely: how does a switch decide where to send a packet based on the destination MAC. Traditional answer: MAC learning plus flooding fallback (broadcast on a miss). OVN answer: `northd` knows the LSP-to-MAC configuration (through `Logical_Switch_Port.addresses`) and compiles a static flow `eth.dst==MAC` to `output:LSP_port` at stage L2_LKUP. No learning needed, no spurious flooding. Scope: known unicast MACs. Unknown unicast, broadcast, and multicast are handled in a separate stage (`LS_IN_L2_UNKNOWN`).

**Importance.** Medium tier in catalog terms but foundational to the LS pipeline. Stage `LS_IN_L2_LKUP` is where the MAC table maps to an output port, and every VM-to-VM traffic flow passes through it. An operator must know: the unicast versus multicast versus broadcast bit semantics, multicast-group masks, and the OVN compile pattern (priority 50 per LSP MAC plus a priority 0 MC_FLOOD fallback).

**How it works.** The datapath parses Ethernet header bytes 0 through 5 into `flow.dl_dst`. The classifier matches either exactly or with a bitwise mask. In OVN compilation, `consider_lsp_l2_lookup()` (`northd/northd.c`) iterates over every LSP of the Logical_Switch and emits a flow with match `eth.dst == lsp.addresses.mac` and action `outport = "<lsp>"; output;`.

**OXM TLV byte layout.** `oxm_class=0x8000`, `oxm_field=3`, `hasmask=0`, `length=6`, value 6 bytes. Masked variant: `hasmask=1`, `length=12`, value 6 bytes plus mask 6 bytes. Total exact: 10 bytes. Total masked: 16 bytes.

**Match examples.**

```
# L2 forwarding by destination MAC (OVN style)
priority=50,metadata=0x1,eth_dst=AA:11:22:33:44:01,actions=load:0x2->NXM_NX_REG15[],resubmit(,32)
priority=50,metadata=0x1,eth_dst=AA:11:22:33:44:02,actions=load:0x3->NXM_NX_REG15[],resubmit(,32)
priority=0,metadata=0x1,actions=load:0xffff->NXM_NX_REG15[],resubmit(,32)  # MC_FLOOD

# Broadcast ARP
priority=100,eth_dst=ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff,actions=flood

# IPv4 multicast group (224.0.0.0/4 maps to 01:00:5e:00:00:00 with mask 01:00:5e:00:00:00)
priority=100,eth_dst=01:00:5e:00:00:00/ff:ff:ff:80:00:00,actions=resubmit(,igmp_snoop_table)

# IPv6 multicast group (ff02::/16 maps to 33:33:00:00:00:00)
priority=100,eth_dst=33:33:00:00:00:00/ff:ff:00:00:00:00,actions=resubmit(,nd_table)

# MAC rewrite before sending over a tunnel (OVN logical-to-physical translation)
priority=100,actions=set_field:AA:11:22:33:44:99->eth_dst,output:5
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: understand the OVN L2_LKUP compile pattern, read the flow with `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=27 | grep eth_dst`. (2) **Edit**: in OVN through `lsp-set-addresses` (change the MAC of an LSP); in standalone OVS through manual flow add. (3) **Observe**: packet and byte counters per destination MAC entry, which reveal asymmetric traffic. (4) **Debug**: if VM A cannot ping VM B, run `ovn-trace` with `eth.dst=B_MAC` and inspect which flow matches at stage 27.

**Source code reference.** Function `build_lswitch_destination_lookup_bmcast` (`northd/northd.c`) builds the broadcast and multicast flows at L2_LKUP. Function `consider_port_binding_l2_lookup` builds the per-LSP flow. Branch baseline: OVN v22.03.

**Failure scenarios.** (a) Two LSPs in the same Logical_Switch share a MAC (a misconfiguration). `northd` skips one LSP, packets to that MAC flood, and traffic is duplicated. Fix: audit with `lsp-list ls1` and look for duplicate MACs. (b) Forgetting to set `addresses` on an LSP means `northd` does not emit an L2_LKUP flow, so packets to that MAC always flood. Fix: `lsp-set-addresses lsp1 "AA:11:..."`.

**Synthetic exercise.** Mininet with one OVS bridge `s1` and three hosts `h1`, `h2`, `h3` with the matching MACs. Install three flows: `eth_dst=h1.mac` to `output:1`, `eth_dst=h2.mac` to `output:2`, `eth_dst=h3.mac` to `output:3`, plus `eth_dst=ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff` to `flood` for ARP. Verify ping between every pair works, and read each flow's counter to confirm the path.

**Comparison with other systems.** The Linux bridge `fdb` table self-learns `eth_dst` entries; the command `bridge fdb show` displays the table. Cisco MAC address-table `show mac address-table` is equivalent. NSX-T MAC learning has the logical-switch field `replication_mode`, which decides the flooding behaviour. All of these look up the destination MAC, but OVN is distinct: declarative, centrally compiled, and not self-learning at runtime (which reduces the source of drift).

---

### 4.8.X.4 `eth_type`, the EtherType that classifies the Ethernet payload

**Concept.** `eth_type` is a 16-bit match field that holds the EtherType. The EtherType field sits right after the destination and source MAC in the Ethernet II header (bytes 12 and 13) and identifies the protocol of the payload. Legacy alias: `dl_type`. This field is the **prerequisite** for almost every L3 and higher field: to match `nw_src` you must first match `eth_type=0x0800` (IPv4); to match `arp_op` you need `eth_type=0x0806`; to match `ipv6_src` you need `eth_type=0x86dd`. Field type: **L2 header field**, subtype "protocol classifier".

**History and background.** The EtherType has been defined since the Ethernet II specification in the 1980s. OF 1.0 included it in the match struct as a 16-bit field, exact-match only. OF 1.2 standardised it as `OXM_OF_ETH_TYPE` (`oxm_field=5`). The field has been stable since 1.0. The only later change is that OF 1.5 (2014) added the `packet_type` field for non-Ethernet packets (NSH, MPLS), and in those cases `eth_type` may not apply (see 4.8.4.5 `packet_type`).

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.dl_type` is 16-bit big-endian. MFF identifier: `MFF_ETH_TYPE` (in `meta-flow.h`). Notably, the OVS classifier uses `eth_type` as a **first-level dispatch** in TSS: flows with `eth_type=0x0800` (IPv4) live in a different subtable from flows with `eth_type=0x86dd` (IPv6), which keeps the cross-product small.

**Role.** Primary role: classify the payload protocol so the right L3 parsing path is chosen. Secondary role: prerequisite enforcement. OVS validates a rule on parse and rejects a rule that references `nw_src` without `eth_type=0x0800` (error message: "field nw_src missing prerequisite eth_type=0x0800"). The field is also the input behind the shorthand aliases: `ip` is `eth_type=0x0800`, `ipv6` is `eth_type=0x86dd`, `arp` is `eth_type=0x0806`.

**Why it exists.** Every Ethernet packet carries an EtherType, and switch silicon already parses it to dispatch the right hardware ACL for IPv4, IPv6, ARP, or MPLS. OpenFlow exposes the field to the control plane so a rule can distinguish the protocol and avoid nonsense (for example, a rule matching `nw_src=10.0.0.1` should not apply to an ARP packet, where `arp_spa` is the right field).

**Problems it solves.** Concretely: how do we ensure that an L3 rule applies only to the right L3 protocol. The answer: enforce a prerequisite. A rule that wants to match L3 IPv4 must carry `eth_type=0x0800`. The OVS classifier uses a prefix tree to dispatch by `eth_type` before entering the L3 subtable. Scope: distinguishing the six main protocol families (IPv4, IPv6, ARP, RARP, MPLS unicast and multicast, PBB, NSH).

**Importance.** Medium tier in catalog terms but 100 percent frequency in any L3 or higher rule. An operator must know: the common EtherType table (`0x0800` IPv4, `0x86dd` IPv6, `0x0806` ARP, `0x8035` RARP, `0x8100` VLAN 802.1Q, `0x88a8` QinQ 802.1ad, `0x8847` and `0x8848` MPLS, `0x88e7` PBB, `0x894f` NSH). Note that a VLAN-tagged packet has `eth_type=0x8100` in the outer header, but the `eth_type` that OVS exposes is the EtherType **inside**, after the VLAN has been logically popped (the true L3 EtherType).

**How it works.** The datapath parses the Ethernet header. If bytes 12 and 13 equal `0x8100` (VLAN), the datapath parses the next 4 bytes (TPID plus TCI), then reads the real EtherType at bytes 16 and 17 into `flow.dl_type`, and populates `vlan_tci` at the same time. If the packet is QinQ (`0x88a8` outer plus `0x8100` inner), both layers are parsed. `flow.dl_type` is always the "real" EtherType after the VLAN unwrap, which means an L3 rule does not need to know whether a VLAN is present.

**OXM TLV byte layout.** `oxm_class=0x8000`, `oxm_field=5`, `hasmask=0`, `length=2`, value 2 bytes big-endian. Total: 6 bytes. The OF specification does not allow a masked variant (exact match only).

**Common EtherType and prerequisite chain table.**

| EtherType | Protocol | Downstream fields that require this prerequisite |
|---|---|---|
| `0x0800` | IPv4 | `nw_src`, `nw_dst`, `nw_proto`, `nw_tos`, `nw_ttl`, `ip_frag` |
| `0x86dd` | IPv6 | `ipv6_src`, `ipv6_dst`, `ipv6_label`, `ipv6_exthdr`, `nd_target` |
| `0x0806` | ARP | `arp_op`, `arp_spa`, `arp_tpa`, `arp_sha`, `arp_tha` |
| `0x8035` | RARP | `arp_op` (legacy) |
| `0x8100` | VLAN 802.1Q | (VLAN fields are handled automatically) |
| `0x88a8` | QinQ 802.1ad | (PBB outer) |
| `0x8847` | MPLS unicast | `mpls_label`, `mpls_tc`, `mpls_bos`, `mpls_ttl` |
| `0x8848` | MPLS multicast | (same MPLS fields) |
| `0x88e7` | PBB | `pbb_isid` |
| `0x894f` | NSH | `nsh_spi`, `nsh_si`, `nsh_c1` through `nsh_c4` |

**Match examples.**

```
# Filter for IPv4 traffic only
priority=100,eth_type=0x0800,actions=goto_table:10
# Same idea using the alias
priority=100,ip,actions=goto_table:10

# ARP responder
priority=100,eth_type=0x0806,arp_tpa=10.0.0.1,actions=...
# Using the alias
priority=100,arp,arp_tpa=10.0.0.1,actions=...

# IPv6
priority=100,eth_type=0x86dd,ipv6_src=2001:db8::/32,actions=...
priority=100,ipv6,ipv6_src=2001:db8::/32,actions=...   # alias

# Drop unexpected MPLS traffic
priority=200,eth_type=0x8847,actions=drop
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: recognise the aliases (`ip`, `ipv6`, `arp`, `mpls`, `tcp`, `udp`); they all implicitly set the right `eth_type`. (2) **Edit**: prefer aliases in manual rules for readability, and double-check the prerequisite when writing a custom flow. (3) **Observe**: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | head -50` shows the distribution of flows by `eth_type` and the traffic mix. (4) **Debug**: when you see "field X missing prerequisite eth_type=Y", add `eth_type=Y` or use the alias.

**Source code reference.** Function `mf_check_src_prereqs` (`lib/meta-flow.c`) validates the prerequisite chain. Function `parse_ofp_str` (`lib/ofp-parse.c`) handles the aliases. Branch baseline: OVS v2.17.9.

**Failure scenario.** Forgetting `eth_type=0x0800` when matching `nw_src` makes OVS reject the rule with "field nw_src missing prerequisite". Fix: add the prerequisite explicitly, or use the alias `ip,nw_src=10.0.0.1`.

**Synthetic exercise.** On an OVS bridge install three flows: `priority=100,ip,actions=resubmit(,10)`, `priority=100,arp,actions=resubmit(,20)`, and `priority=100,ipv6,actions=resubmit(,30)`. Send one ARP packet from a Mininet host: the counter on flow 2 increases. Send one IPv4 ICMP ping: the counter on flow 1 increases. Verify the `eth_type` dispatch is correct.

**Comparison with other systems.** The Linux iptables `-p` flag (proto) implicitly combines the EtherType with the IP protocol. A Cisco ACL using `permit ip` versus `permit icmp` versus `permit tcp` is the same idea. An NSX-T DFW rule has a `service` field (predefined HTTP, HTTPS) that abstracts EtherType plus L4 port. P4 uses a `parser` state machine to parse the EtherType and branch into the IPv4 or IPv6 state, which is a direct inheritance of the OpenFlow concept.

---
