### 4.8.X.5 `vlan_vid`, the 12-bit VLAN ID inside an 802.1Q tag

**Concept.** `vlan_vid` is a 12-bit match field that holds the VLAN ID (values 0 through 4095, where 0 and 4095 are reserved) of the 802.1Q tag attached to an Ethernet frame. The field is extracted from the 16-bit TCI (Tag Control Information) of the 802.1Q tag, leaving out the 3 bits of PCP and the 1 bit of DEI/CFI. It has a special twist: bit 12 of the TCI (the "VLAN Present" bit in OF 1.1 and later OXM) reports whether a tag is present at all. That gives two reserved values: `0x1000` matches "any VLAN tag present", and `0x0000` matches "no tag". Field type: **L2 header field**, subtype "VLAN identifier".

**History and background.** 802.1Q VLAN was standardised in 1998. OF 1.0 (2009) defined `dl_vlan` as 16-bit (the full TCI), with a reserved value `OFP_VLAN_NONE=0xffff` for untagged frames. OF 1.1 (2011) split it into `vlan_vid` (12-bit) and `vlan_pcp` (3-bit), encoding bit 12 of the OXM value as the "Present" flag (Pratt-style encoding to keep exact-match semantics). OF 1.2 OXM standardises `OXM_OF_VLAN_VID` (`oxm_field=6`, `hasmask` supported) and `OXM_OF_VLAN_PCP` (`oxm_field=7`).

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.vlan_tci` is 16-bit and holds the entire TCI (OVS keeps the classic format). When serialising to OXM, OVS extracts the 12-bit VID plus the present flag. The MFF identifiers are `MFF_VLAN_TCI` for the legacy form and `MFF_VLAN_VID` for the OXM form.

**Role.** Primary role: distinguish VLAN traffic on a trunk port and dispatch it to different policy or forwarding. Role inside OVN: on a tap LSP that has a `tag` set, `ovn-controller` installs a flow at table 0 that strips the VLAN tag (`strip_vlan`) before the packet enters the OVN logical pipeline. On the outbound side, before sending out the tap, OVN pushes a VLAN tag (`mod_vlan_vid:N`). In standalone OVS on a trunk port, a rule dispatches `vlan_vid=100` to tenant 1's pipeline and `vlan_vid=200` to tenant 2's pipeline.

**Why it exists.** 802.1Q VLAN was the most common way to segment broadcast domains on Ethernet before overlays (VXLAN, Geneve). OpenFlow had to support `vlan_vid` matching to integrate with legacy datacenter deployments using VLAN trunks. The OF 1.0 design that used `dl_vlan=0xffff` for untagged was not clean (it mixed data values with a metadata flag). OF 1.1 redesigned the encoding around an explicit "Present" flag.

**Problems it solves.** Concretely: how do we distinguish "packet has no VLAN tag" from "packet has a VLAN tag with VID=0" from "packet has a VLAN tag with VID=N". The OF 1.1 plus OXM solution: a 13-bit encoding (12-bit VID plus 1-bit present). `vlan_vid=0x0000` means no tag. `vlan_vid=0x1000` with mask `0x1000` means any tag is present. `vlan_vid=N` for N from `0x0001` to `0x0fff` means a tag is present with VID=N (bit 12 implicit set).

**Importance.** Medium tier. Frequency is high in datacenters that use trunk ports (OpenStack provider VLAN, baremetal trunks). In cloud-native overlay-only deployments (VXLAN, Geneve), `vlan_vid` is less common. An operator must know: the "Present flag" encoding semantics, the difference between mask `0x1000` (any tag) and mask `0x0fff` (any VID), and the actions `strip_vlan`, `pop_vlan`, and `mod_vlan_vid`.

**How it works.** The datapath parses the Ethernet header. If bytes 12 and 13 equal `0x8100` (TPID), the datapath parses the 16-bit TCI at bytes 14 and 15 and stores it in `flow.vlan_tci` in raw TCI format. When serialising to OXM, OVS encodes the value: if the TCI has CFI=1 (present), it sets bit 12 of the OXM value and copies the 12-bit VID; if there is no TCI (untagged), the value is `0x0000`.

**OXM TLV byte layout (`vlan_vid`).** `oxm_class=0x8000`, `oxm_field=6`, `hasmask=0`, `length=2`, value 2 bytes with encoding: bit 12 is the present flag, bits 0 through 11 are the VID. Masked variant: `hasmask=1`, `length=4`, value 2 bytes plus mask 2 bytes.

**Special-value table.**

| Match expression | OXM value | OXM mask | Meaning |
|---|---|---|---|
| `vlan_vid=0` | `0x0000` | (none) | No tag (untagged frame) |
| `vlan_vid=0x1000/0x1000` | `0x1000` | `0x1000` | A tag is present, any VID |
| `vlan_vid=100` | `0x1064` | (none) | Tag present with VID=100 (`0x064` plus bit 12) |
| `vlan_vid=100/0x0fff` | `0x0064` | `0x0fff` | VID=100, regardless of whether a tag is present |
| `vlan_vid=0x0000/0x1000` | `0x0000` | `0x1000` | No tag (alternative spelling) |

**Match examples.**

```
# Trunk-port dispatch by VLAN
priority=100,in_port=1,vlan_vid=100,actions=strip_vlan,resubmit(,10)
priority=100,in_port=1,vlan_vid=200,actions=strip_vlan,resubmit(,20)
priority=100,in_port=1,vlan_vid=0,actions=drop                   # untagged is not allowed

# Match any VLAN tag
priority=50,vlan_vid=0x1000/0x1000,actions=resubmit(,trunk_table)

# Modify VLAN ID (translation)
priority=100,vlan_vid=100,actions=mod_vlan_vid:200,output:2

# Push a new VLAN tag (legacy alias dl_vlan)
priority=100,in_port=2,actions=push_vlan:0x8100,mod_vlan_vid:300,output:1

# Strip the VLAN tag (untag)
priority=100,vlan_vid=100,actions=strip_vlan,output:eth0
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: distinguish `vlan_vid=N` from `vlan_vid=N/0x0fff` from `vlan_vid=0x1000/0x1000`; the three semantics are different. (2) **Edit**: use the right action: `strip_vlan` removes the tag, `push_vlan:0x8100` adds a tag header, `mod_vlan_vid:N` changes the VID, `mod_vlan_pcp:P` changes the PCP. (3) **Observe**: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep vlan_vid` counts trunk flows; `tcpdump -e -i eth0` shows the raw TCI. (4) **Debug**: when VLAN translation is wrong, run `ovn-trace` with a VLAN-tagged input packet and trace each action.

**Source code reference.** Function anchor `flow_set_dl_vlan` (`lib/flow.c`) handles VLAN write logic. Function `format_be16_masked` (`lib/match.c`) formats VID and PCP with present-flag handling. Branch baseline: OVS v2.17.9.

**Failure scenarios.** (a) Writing `vlan_vid=100` hoping it means "VID=100 regardless of tag presence" is wrong. The syntax requires `present=1` (bit 12 set). Use `vlan_vid=100/0x0fff` instead. (b) `mod_vlan_vid:100` applied to an untagged packet is a silent no-op (the action only modifies an existing tag, it does not push a new one). You need `push_vlan:0x8100,mod_vlan_vid:100` to create a new tag.

**Synthetic exercise.** A Mininet trunk topology: bridge `s1` with one host `h1` on a trunk port (untagged) and two hosts `h2` and `h3` on access ports for VLAN 100 and VLAN 200. Install on `s1`: `in_port=1(h1),vlan_vid=100,actions=strip_vlan,output:2(h2)` and the symmetric flow for VLAN 200. Verify ping between `h1` and `h2` works when `h1` sends tagged VLAN 100; verify ping between `h1` and `h3` works on VLAN 200; verify `h2` and `h3` cannot talk to each other (segmentation).

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux `vconfig add eth0 100` creates a subinterface `eth0.100` that handles VLAN 100, semantically equivalent to the rule `vlan_vid=100,strip_vlan`. Cisco IOS `switchport access vlan 100` on a port performs the strip-and-tag automatically in hardware. NSX-T uses Geneve overlay instead of VLAN trunks but still has a "VLAN Logical Switch" type for compatibility. In the pre-overlay era every datacenter relied on VLAN trunks; OVN reduces that dependence but still supports VLAN for provider networks.

---

### 4.8.X.6 `ip_proto`, the L4 protocol number inside the IPv4 or IPv6 header

**Concept.** `ip_proto` is an 8-bit match field that holds the "Protocol" byte (byte 9) of an IPv4 header (RFC 791) or the "Next Header" byte (byte 6) of an IPv6 header (RFC 8200). It identifies the payload protocol (TCP=6, UDP=17, ICMP=1, SCTP=132, OSPF=89, GRE=47, ESP=50, AH=51, ICMPv6=58, and so on). Legacy alias: `nw_proto`. This field is the **prerequisite** for almost every L4 field: to match `tp_src` or `tp_dst` (TCP or UDP), `tcp_flags`, `icmp_type` or `icmp_code`, or `sctp_src` or `sctp_dst`, the rule must first match `ip_proto` with the right value. Field type: **L3 header field**, subtype "L4 protocol classifier".

**History and background.** OF 1.0 (2009) introduced the field in the match struct under the name `nw_proto`, exact-match only. OF 1.2 (2011) standardised `OXM_OF_IP_PROTO` (`oxm_class=0x8000`, `oxm_field=10`) when it adopted the OXM TLV as the default match-field mechanism, and at the same time made the field apply to IPv6 (before that, OF 1.0 only defined it for IPv4). Stable since OF 1.2. The width and encoding have not changed. In upstream OVS, `NXM_OF_IP_PROTO` (code 6) has been present since OVS v1.1; the OXM equivalent was added in v1.7 (2014).

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.nw_proto` is 8-bit (`include/openvswitch/flow.h`). MFF: `MFF_IP_PROTO` is defined in `include/openvswitch/meta-flow.h` (type `u8`, prerequisite IP). The OVS classifier uses this field together with `eth_type` for a two-level dispatch: the subtable branches first by `eth_type` (IPv4 versus IPv6 versus ARP), then by `ip_proto` (TCP versus UDP versus ICMP), which keeps the TSS cross-product small.

**Role.** Primary role: classify the L4 protocol so the right parser path runs. Secondary role: prerequisite enforcement. OVS rejects a rule that references `tp_dst=80` without `ip_proto=6`, with the error "field tp_dst missing prerequisite ip_proto=6". Shorthand role: the aliases. `tcp` is `eth_type=0x0800,ip_proto=6`; `udp` is `eth_type=0x0800,ip_proto=17`; `icmp` is `eth_type=0x0800,ip_proto=1`; `tcp6`, `udp6`, and `icmp6` are the IPv6 equivalents.

**Why it exists.** Every IP packet carries a protocol byte that identifies the payload, and silicon has parsed this byte for dispatch for decades. OpenFlow needs to expose it to the control plane so a rule can distinguish protocols. Without `ip_proto`, every rule would have to dig into low-level L4 header bytes, which is extremely error-prone (TCP and UDP have different port-byte layouts; parsing the wrong one would corrupt). The OF 1.0 specification covered only IPv4; extending the field to IPv6 in OF 1.2 was a natural reaction to growing IPv6 deployment.

**Problems it solves.** Concretely: how do we ensure that an L4 rule applies only to the right L4 protocol, and how do we avoid parsing fields that do not exist (for example, ICMP has no port, so parsing `tp_src` on ICMP is junk). The answer: enforce a prerequisite chain. `tp_src` and `tp_dst` are only valid when `ip_proto=6` or `17`; `tcp_flags` is only valid when `ip_proto=6`; `icmp_type` and `icmp_code` are only valid when `ip_proto=1` (IPv4) or `ip_proto=58` (ICMPv6). Scope: all the large L4 protocols (TCP, UDP, SCTP, ICMP, ICMPv6) plus a handful of niche L4 protocols (GRE, ESP, AH, OSPF) which can be matched but do not have their own L4 sub-fields.

**Importance.** Medium tier, very high frequency (it appears in close to 100 percent of L4 rules). An operator must know: the common IANA Protocol number table (1=ICMP, 2=IGMP, 6=TCP, 17=UDP, 41=IPv6-in-IPv4, 47=GRE, 50=ESP, 51=AH, 58=ICMPv6, 89=OSPF, 132=SCTP), the fact that `ip_proto` is reused for both IPv4 and IPv6 (which saves pipeline complexity), and the prerequisite chain into L4 sub-fields.

**How it works.** The datapath parses the Ethernet header and reads `eth_type`. If `eth_type=0x0800` (IPv4), it parses byte 9 of the IPv4 header into `flow.nw_proto`. If `eth_type=0x86dd` (IPv6), it walks the Next Header chain: starting from the initial Next Header byte of the IPv6 header, if that byte is an extension header (Hop-by-Hop=0, Routing=43, Fragment=44, AH=51, ESP=50, Destination=60), it iterates through each extension header until it finds the real upper-layer protocol (TCP, UDP, ICMPv6). That final value goes into `flow.nw_proto`, and the `flow.ipv6_exthdr` bitmap records which extension headers were traversed.

**OXM TLV byte layout.** `oxm_class=0x8000`, `oxm_field=10`, `hasmask=0`, `length=1`, value 1 byte. Total 5 bytes. The specification does not allow `hasmask=1` (it would not make sense to mask an 8-bit IANA assignment).

**Match examples.**

```
# Filter for TCP only
priority=100,ip,ip_proto=6,actions=goto_table:10
# Same idea using the alias
priority=100,tcp,actions=goto_table:10

# UDP DNS
priority=100,ip,ip_proto=17,udp_dst=53,actions=output:dns_resolver
priority=100,udp,udp_dst=53,actions=output:dns_resolver  # alias

# ICMP echo for a ping monitor
priority=100,ip,ip_proto=1,icmp_type=8,actions=output:monitor

# Drop non-standard protocols (not TCP, UDP, or ICMP)
priority=10,ip,ip_proto=47,actions=drop  # block GRE
priority=10,ip,ip_proto=50,actions=drop  # block ESP

# OSPF for a router cluster
priority=100,ip,ip_proto=89,actions=output:ospf_neigh
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: memorise the core IANA number table (TCP, UDP, ICMP, SCTP, OSPF, GRE, ESP, AH); know that the aliases `tcp`, `udp`, and `icmp` implicitly include the right `eth_type`. (2) **Edit**: prefer the aliases in manual rules; for rules generated programmatically, use the explicit `ip_proto=N` so the rule does not depend on the parser. (3) **Observe**: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | awk '/ip_proto/{print}'` counts the distribution; `tcpdump -nn ip proto 6` cross-checks the result. (4) **Debug**: when you see "field X missing prerequisite ip_proto=Y", add the prerequisite explicitly. For IPv6, if `ip_proto=58` does not match ICMPv6 as expected, check whether the packet has an extension-header chain (Routing or Fragment can change the chain, but `flow.nw_proto` is still the final upper-layer protocol).

**Source code reference.** Function `flow_extract` (`lib/flow.c`) parses `nw_proto` from the raw packet. For the IPv6 extension-header chain, see `parse_ipv6_ext_hdrs` in the same file. Prerequisite validation lives in `mf_check_src_prereqs` (`lib/meta-flow.c`). Branch baseline OVS v2.17.9; specific line numbers may drift between versions, so use the function name as the anchor (Rule 14 Option C).

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom A: an L4 rule does not match. Check that the packet really uses that protocol with `tcpdump -nn ip proto N`. If the packet is right but the rule does not hit, suspect a prerequisite-chain mismatch. Symptom B: an IPv6 packet with an extension header (such as Routing) does not match a `tcp` rule. Confirm `flow.nw_proto` with `ofproto/trace`. A Fragment header before the TCP header can stop the parser if the packet is truncated. Symptom C: a rule combines `ip,ip_proto=58` (ICMPv6 with `eth_type=0x0800`). This is an impossible match (ICMPv6 requires `eth_type=0x86dd`). OVS does not reject the rule as a syntax error, but the rule will always miss.

**Tightly related to.** `eth_type` (the direct prerequisite), `tp_src`, `tp_dst`, `tcp_flags`, `icmp_type`, `icmp_code`, `sctp_src`, `sctp_dst` (downstream fields that need the right `ip_proto`), `ct_nw_proto` (the conntrack original tuple, parallel to `ip_proto`), and `ipv6_exthdr` (the IPv6 extension-header bitmap that influences the final `nw_proto`).

**Version differences.** OF 1.0 applied only to IPv4 (`nw_proto`). OF 1.2 extended the field to IPv6 and standardised the name `OXM_OF_IP_PROTO`. OVS keeps the `nw_proto` alias for backward compatibility. OVS 2.4 (2015) improved the IPv6 extension-header parser so that long chains (10 or more extensions) are not dropped. Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 plus OVN v22.03.8.

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,eth_type=0x0800,ip_proto=6,...'
Final flow: ...,nw_proto=6,...
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep ip_proto
 cookie=0x0, n_packets=42, ip,ip_proto=6,tp_dst=80,actions=...
$ tcpdump -nn -i any 'ip and not tcp and not udp and not icmp'   # capture unusual protocols
```

**Real-world incident.** A multi-AZ OVN 22.03 cluster reported a 20 percent drop in IPv6 traffic after a host-kernel upgrade, visible on the observability dashboard. Investigation: `ovn-trace inport==..,ip6,ip_proto=6,...` showed that the `tcp6` rule no longer matched some packets. Root cause: the new kernel changed the default IPv6 fragment-header threshold, so a subset of TCP-over-IPv6 packets now had a Fragment extension header in the chain. The old `flow_extract` parser in the previous OVS release stopped at Fragment instead of walking to TCP, so `nw_proto` ended up as 44 (Fragment) instead of 6. Fix: upgrade OVS to a version with a stable extension-header parser, and tune the host fragmentation MTU.

**Synthetic exercise.** Mininet bridge `s1` with two hosts `h1` and `h2`. Install four flows: `priority=100,tcp,actions=output:2`, `priority=100,udp,actions=output:2`, `priority=100,icmp,actions=output:2`, and `priority=10,ip,actions=drop`. Verify `h1 ping h2` (ICMP) works, `iperf -u` (UDP) works, and a custom GRE packet sent with `scapy` is dropped. Read each flow's counter and confirm the `ip_proto` dispatch is correct.

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) Wrong number (`ip_proto=4` instead of 6): the error log says "no match"; cross-check with IANA. (b) Forgetting `eth_type` while using `ip_proto` (a bare `ip_proto=6`): OVS rejects the rule. Fix: use `ip,ip_proto=6` or the `tcp` alias. (c) Applying `ip_proto=58` (ICMPv6) to IPv4: silent miss; use the `ipv6` alias.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables `-p tcp/udp/icmp/...` uses the same IANA protocol number. Cisco ACL `permit tcp/udp/icmp/89` puts the protocol as the second field after `permit` or `deny`. NSX-T Distributed Firewall has an abstract `service` field (HTTP, HTTPS, ICMP) that implicitly maps to `ip_proto` plus an L4 port. P4 uses a `parser` state machine: in the IPv4 state, after parsing the protocol byte, the parser does `transition select(hdr.ipv4.protocol)` to branch into the TCP, UDP, or ICMP state, which is a direct inheritance of the OpenFlow concept.

---

### 4.8.X.7 `nw_src`, the 32-bit IPv4 source address

**Concept.** `nw_src` (canonical alias `ipv4_src`, legacy alias `ip_src`) is a 32-bit match field that holds the IPv4 source address: the last 4 bytes of the IPv4 header (bytes 12 through 15 from the start of the IP header, RFC 791). Match formats: dotted decimal `10.0.0.5` (exact), CIDR `10.0.0.0/24` (prefix), or arbitrary bitwise mask `10.0.0.0/0xffff0000`. This field is one of the most heavily used fields in the entire OpenFlow catalog. It is the spine of source-based ACLs, NAT identification, anti-spoofing, and geo-fencing. Field type: **L3 header field**, subtype "endpoint identifier".

**History and background.** OF 1.0 (2009) defined `nw_src` as 32-bit but only supported a CIDR mask (prefix length). OVS 1.8 (2013) added support for an arbitrary bitwise mask (non-prefix), which is powerful for non-contiguous subnet rules. OF 1.2 (2011) standardised `OXM_OF_IPV4_SRC` (`oxm_class=0x8000`, `oxm_field=11`, `hasmask` supported). `NXM_OF_IP_SRC` (code 7) has existed since OVS v1.1 for backward compatibility. Stable since OF 1.2.

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.nw_src` is 32-bit big-endian (`be32`). MFF: `MFF_IPV4_SRC` (`include/openvswitch/meta-flow.h`, type `be32`, prefix lookup member `nw_src`). The OVS classifier uses this field as a **prefix-tree key**: the trie structure for prefix-based (CIDR) rules supports O(log n) longest-prefix match instead of an O(n) linear scan. An arbitrary bitwise mask does not enter the trie; it goes into a separate TSS subtable.

**Role.** Primary role: identify the source endpoint for any IPv4-based forwarding or filtering decision. Secondary roles: anti-spoofing (the rule `priority=200,in_port=N,ip,nw_src=10.0.N.0/24,actions=resubmit` allows only the right subnet on that port); NAT identification (the conntrack rule `ct_nw_src=<original_src>,ct_state=+est+rpl` matches reply traffic returning to a VIP); geo-fencing using a per-region prefix list. In OVN: the LR_IN_IP_ROUTING pipeline uses `ip4.dst` (which compiles to `nw_dst`) for route lookup; Logical_Switch ACLs use `ip4.src` (which compiles to `nw_src`).

**Why it exists.** Endpoint identification is the minimal requirement for any L3 forwarding rule, so OF 1.0 could not omit IPv4 source and destination. The early CIDR-only restriction came from silicon: traditional ASIC TCAM matches prefixes, and supporting an arbitrary mask would explode the entry count. OVS userspace has no TCAM constraint, which is why OVS 1.8 was able to unlock arbitrary masks.

**Problems it solves.** Concretely: how do we expose the IPv4 source address to policy rules. The answer: a 32-bit field with masking. Scope: every CIDR-based ACL use case (subnet to policy), and arbitrary masks for unusual deployments such as multihomed dual-prefix. Out of scope: stateful flow tracking (which requires `ct_state` and `ct_nw_src`); IPv6 (which uses the separate 128-bit `ipv6_src`).

**Importance.** Medium-high tier, very high frequency (it appears in almost every L3 rule). An operator must know: CIDR syntax (`10.0.0.0/24`), arbitrary masks (`10.0.0.0/0xffff0000`), exact match (`10.0.0.5`), the integration with the prefix-lookup classifier (trie), and the difference from `ct_nw_src` (which is the original tuple before NAT, not the current value).

**How it works.** The datapath parses IPv4 header bytes 12 through 15 into `flow.nw_src`. The classifier lookup: TSS subtables branch first; if the rule only uses a prefix mask, the classifier switches to the trie path (`classifier_lookup`, `lib/classifier.c`). The trie is maintained as a radix tree; each insertion updates both the trie and the subtable. The lookup walks the trie bit by bit from the most significant bit of the IPv4 address and returns the longest matching prefix.

**OXM TLV byte layout.** Exact: `oxm_class=0x8000`, `oxm_field=11`, `hasmask=0`, `length=4`, value 4 bytes big-endian. Total 8 bytes. Masked: `hasmask=1`, `length=8`, value 4 bytes plus mask 4 bytes. Total 12 bytes.

**Match examples.**

```
# Subnet ACL
priority=100,ip,nw_src=10.0.1.0/24,actions=resubmit(,allow_table)
priority=100,ip,nw_src=192.168.0.0/16,actions=drop      # block RFC 1918 private space on the uplink

# Exact host
priority=200,ip,nw_src=10.0.1.5,actions=output:monitor

# Anti-spoofing
priority=300,in_port=1,ip,nw_src=10.0.1.0/24,actions=resubmit(,L3_table)
priority=10,in_port=1,ip,actions=drop                   # source outside the subnet, drop

# Arbitrary mask (non-contiguous, multihomed)
priority=100,ip,nw_src=10.0.0.0/0xff00ff00,actions=...

# Modify (NAT)
priority=100,ip,nw_src=192.168.1.5,actions=mod_nw_src:10.0.0.5,output:uplink
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: distinguish CIDR, arbitrary mask, and exact match. (2) **Edit**: use `mod_nw_src` for static SNAT, or `ct(nat(src=...))` for stateful NAT (see 9.24). (3) **Observe**: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep nw_src` shows the distribution; `tcpdump -nn 'ip src 10.0.1.5'` cross-checks. (4) **Debug**: when a rule does not match, verify the prefix length, verify that the actual packet has the right source IP (`tcpdump`), and if you suspect NAT changed the source, use `ct_nw_src` to match the original.

**Source code reference.** Function `flow_extract` (`lib/flow.c`) parses the source bytes 12 through 15 into `flow.nw_src`. Function `classifier_lookup` (`lib/classifier.c`) performs the lookup; the trie path is in `trie_lookup`. Function `mf_set_value` for `MFF_IPV4_SRC` populates the match struct. Branch baseline OVS v2.17.9.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom A: an ACL does not hit for a subnet. Check the prefix length and check whether a higher-priority rule overlaps. Symptom B: NAT-ed traffic does not hit the downstream rule that matches the original `nw_src`. This is correct: NAT changed the source before the match. Use `ct_nw_src` to match the original. Symptom C: an arbitrary-mask rule does not hit, possibly because of endian confusion. OVS expects the mask in network byte order; double-check with `ovs-ofctl dump-flows --no-stats`.

**Tightly related to.** `eth_type` (prerequisite, `=0x0800`), `nw_dst` (the destination counterpart), `ct_nw_src` (the conntrack original tuple), the `mod_nw_src` action (static SNAT), the `nat()` action inside `ct()` (stateful NAT, see 9.24), and `arp_spa` (the ARP source protocol address; `nw_src` cannot replace it inside an ARP packet).

**Version differences.** OF 1.0 supported only the CIDR mask. OVS 1.8 (2013) opened up arbitrary bitwise masks. OF 1.2 standardised the OXM form with `hasmask=1`. OVS 2.4 (2015) optimised the prefix trie for high rule counts. Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 plus OVN v22.03.8.

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,ip,nw_src=10.0.1.5,nw_dst=10.0.2.10,...'
Final flow: ...,nw_src=10.0.1.5,...
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep 'nw_src=10.0'
 cookie=0x0, n_packets=128, ip,nw_src=10.0.1.0/24,actions=resubmit(,allow_table)
```

**Real-world incident.** A production OpenStack tenant network on OVN saw "random" outbound-traffic drops on some VMs. Investigation: `ovn-trace` showed an ACL deny matching `ip4.src` with an unexpected prefix. Root cause: two ACL rules at the same priority with overlapping prefixes (`10.0.1.0/24` allow versus `10.0.0.0/16` deny). The tie-break was not deterministic for the edge case. Fix: raise the priority of the allow rule (priority 300 versus 200), and audit every ACL for overlap. Tooling: `ovn-nbctl acl-list ls1 | sort -k priority -n` exposes the overlap.

**Synthetic exercise.** Mininet bridge `s1` with four hosts `h1` through `h4` in subnet `10.0.0.0/24`. Install three flows: `priority=200,ip,nw_src=10.0.0.1,actions=output:monitor` (h1 at high priority), `priority=100,ip,nw_src=10.0.0.0/24,actions=output:gateway`, and `priority=10,ip,actions=drop`. Verify that `h1` traffic goes to the monitor, `h2`, `h3`, and `h4` go to the gateway, and traffic outside the subnet is dropped. Replace the prefix with the arbitrary mask `10.0.0.0/0xfffffffc` (which only matches `h1` through `h4`) and observe the behaviour.

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) Wrong prefix length (`/24` versus `/16`): the rule matches too broadly or too narrowly. (b) Forgetting the `ip` alias or `eth_type=0x0800`: OVS rejects the rule. (c) Applying `nw_src` to an IPv6 packet: silent miss; use `ipv6_src` for IPv6.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables `-s 10.0.0.0/24` uses the same syntax. Cisco ACL `permit ip 10.0.0.0 0.0.0.255 any` uses the inverted wildcard mask. NSX-T DFW source fields support object group references and dynamic membership (a vCenter tag). P4 uses `transition select(hdr.ipv4.srcAddr)` for the lookup. OVS is unique in supporting an arbitrary bitwise mask for a non-contiguous address space; few other vendors offer it.

---

### 4.8.X.8 `nw_dst`, the 32-bit IPv4 destination address

**Concept.** `nw_dst` (alias `ipv4_dst`, legacy `ip_dst`) is a 32-bit match field that holds the IPv4 destination address: bytes 16 through 19 of the IPv4 header (RFC 791). The format and masking are identical to `nw_src` (CIDR, exact, arbitrary bitwise mask). This field is the **main input to the routing decision**: a longest-prefix match on `nw_dst` selects the next-hop output port. It is also the spine of destination-based ACLs, Load Balancer VIP matching, and DNAT identification. Field type: **L3 header field**, subtype "endpoint identifier (destination)".

**History and background.** A direct counterpart to `nw_src`: OF 1.0 (2009) introduced it CIDR-only, OVS 1.8 opened up arbitrary masks, OF 1.2 standardised `OXM_OF_IPV4_DST` (`oxm_field=12`, `hasmask` supported). `NXM_OF_IP_DST` (code 8) has existed since OVS v1.1. Stable since OF 1.2.

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.nw_dst` is 32-bit big-endian. MFF: `MFF_IPV4_DST` (`meta-flow.h`, type `be32`, prefix lookup member `nw_dst`). The classifier uses the same trie structure as `nw_src`, but with a separate tree for destinations. In the OVN pipeline, `nw_dst` is the input to the LR_IN_IP_ROUTING stage (route-table lookup); in the Load Balancer pipeline, `nw_dst` matches the VIP and rewrites to a backend (DNAT).

**Role.** Primary role: input to the routing-table lookup (next-hop selection). Secondary roles: destination-based ACLs (the rule `priority=100,ip,nw_dst=10.0.2.0/24,actions=drop`); LB VIP match (`priority=100,ip,nw_dst=10.0.0.100,actions=ct(commit,nat(dst=10.0.1.X)),output:backend`); DNAT identification for the reply path; multicast group match (`nw_dst=224.0.0.0/4` for IGMP multicast).

**Why it exists.** A routing decision on an IP packet boils down to "look up the destination and choose an output". Without `nw_dst` there is no L3 forwarding. The same reasoning that limited `nw_src` to CIDR initially (TCAM hardware constraints) applies here, and the same path led OVS userspace to allow arbitrary masks later.

**Problems it solves.** Concretely: how do we expose the IPv4 destination to routing, ACL, and NAT logic. The answer: a 32-bit field with masking, plus a trie-backed classifier. Scope: route lookup (longest-prefix match), exact match for host routes, prefix match for subnet routes, and arbitrary mask for unusual deployments. Combined with `ct(nat(dst=...))` for stateful DNAT.

**Importance.** Medium-high tier. Very high frequency in L3 forwarding rules. An operator must know: longest-prefix-match semantics. In rules that share the same `priority`, the longer prefix wins, but in OpenFlow `priority` is an explicit field and there is no implicit longest-match unless the operator assigns priority correctly. OVN `northd` compiles a route table with `priority = prefix_length` to emulate longest-prefix match.

**How it works.** The datapath parses bytes 16 through 19 into `flow.nw_dst`. The classifier lookup: TSS subtables; if the rule uses a prefix mask, the classifier dispatches into the trie. The trie traversal walks bit by bit from the most significant bit of the IP destination to the leaf, and returns the rule with the longest matching prefix (within the same subtable).

**OXM TLV byte layout.** Identical to `nw_src`: exact total 8 bytes (header 4 plus value 4); masked 12 bytes (header 4 plus value 4 plus mask 4). The only difference is `oxm_field=12` instead of 11.

**Match examples.**

```
# Default route
priority=10,ip,nw_dst=0.0.0.0/0,actions=output:default_gw

# Specific subnet route (higher priority for longer prefixes, emulating longest-prefix match)
priority=170,ip,nw_dst=10.0.1.0/24,actions=output:gw1
priority=240,ip,nw_dst=10.0.1.5,actions=output:host1     # exact /32 wins over /24

# Load Balancer VIP match
priority=100,ip,nw_dst=10.0.0.100,tcp,tp_dst=80,
                actions=ct(commit,nat(dst=10.0.1.5:8080)),output:backend

# Drop multicast if you do not want it
priority=200,ip,nw_dst=224.0.0.0/4,actions=drop

# Egress ACL
priority=100,ip,nw_dst=192.168.0.0/16,actions=drop  # block traffic to RFC 1918 on the external link
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: understand the priority-and-prefix-length relationship for longest-prefix match (OVN `northd` assigns `priority = mask_length`). (2) **Edit**: use `mod_nw_dst` for static DNAT, `ct(commit,nat(dst=...))` for stateful DNAT, or a direct `output` inside the route. (3) **Observe**: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep 'nw_dst=10.0' | sort -k priority -n` shows the route table on `br-int` compiled from the OVN Logical_Router. (4) **Debug**: when the route lookup is wrong, run `ovn-trace`. If `outport` is empty, the routing rule did not match; check whether the `Logical_Router_Static_Route` rule is configured.

**Source code reference.** Function `flow_extract` parses bytes 16 through 19. Function `route_table_add_route` (`lib/route-table.c`) builds the userspace route table. OVN compilation: `build_lrouter_flows` (`northd/northd.c`) emits route flows from `Logical_Router_Static_Route` plus `Logical_Router_Port`. Branch baseline OVS v2.17.9, OVN v22.03.8.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom A: traffic does not reach the destination. Run `ovn-trace` and check `outport`; if it is empty, the routing rule missed; verify that `Logical_Router_Static_Route` has the right route. Symptom B: LB VIP traffic does not rotate across backends. The `nw_dst=VIP` match is fine but `ct(commit,nat(dst=...))` picks the same backend each time; tune `algorithm=symmetric_l3l4` or check the hash distribution. Symptom C: a route is a black hole because of priority overlap (`/24` has higher priority than `/32`); fix the priority ordering by prefix length.

**Tightly related to.** `eth_type` (prerequisite, `=0x0800`), `nw_src` (the counterpart), `ct_nw_dst` (the original destination before DNAT), the `mod_nw_dst` action, `nat()` inside `ct()` (stateful DNAT), and `Logical_Router_Static_Route` in the OVN NBDB.

**Version differences.** OF 1.0 supported only the CIDR mask. OVS 1.8 added arbitrary masks. OF 1.2 standardised the masked OXM. OVS 2.6 (2017) optimised the prefix trie to O(prefix_length) instead of O(rule_count). Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 plus OVN v22.03.8.

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,ip,nw_src=10.0.1.5,nw_dst=10.0.2.10,...'
Datapath actions: ct(zone=...,nat),resubmit(,...), output:N
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep 'nw_dst=10.0' | sort -t= -k2
```

**Real-world incident.** A multi-tenant OVN cluster deployed an LB for a web service. Traffic to VIP `10.0.0.100:80` was sometimes unevenly balanced across ECMP (one backend received 80 percent of traffic). Investigation: `ovn-trace` showed that `ct(nat)` selects a backend based on a 5-tuple hash; because the client source-port range was narrow (the firewall does NAT), hash collisions were high and the distribution was uneven. Fix: tune `algorithm=symmetric_l3l4` (a symmetric hash for forward and reply). Reference: OVN `Load_Balancer.options:algorithm`.

**Synthetic exercise.** Mininet bridge `s1` with three hosts `h1`, `h2`, `h3` and a simulated route table: `priority=240,ip,nw_dst=10.0.0.1,actions=output:1` (host route to h1), `priority=170,ip,nw_dst=10.0.0.0/24,actions=output:gw`, and `priority=10,ip,nw_dst=0.0.0.0/0,actions=output:default`. Verify that a packet to `10.0.0.1` exits port 1 (the host route wins), `10.0.0.5` exits via the gateway, and `8.8.8.8` exits via the default route. Change the priorities to test longest-prefix versus random priority.

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) Wrong priority causing `/24` to win over `/32`: always assign `priority = prefix length` for routes. (b) Confusing `nw_dst` with `nw_src` in an ACL: check the packet direction (ingress versus egress). (c) Multicast `nw_dst=224.0.0.0/4` is not handled separately and floods widely; add an explicit drop rule or forward to the right group.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables `-d 10.0.0.0/24` uses the same syntax. The Linux routing table viewed with `ip route show` uses implicit longest-prefix match. Cisco IOS `ip route 10.0.0.0 255.255.255.0 next-hop` also uses longest match. NSX-T's Logical_Router routing table is similar to Linux. OVN is different: the route table is compiled into OpenFlow flows with `priority = prefix length`, making explicit what Linux leaves implicit.

---

### 4.8.X.9 `ip_dscp`, the 6-bit DSCP for QoS classification

**Concept.** `ip_dscp` is a 6-bit match field that holds the Differentiated Services Code Point: the upper 6 bits of the ToS byte (IPv4) or the Traffic Class byte (IPv6), defined by RFC 2474 to replace the original RFC 791 ToS semantics. Values run 0 through 63 and are usually expressed with named code points: BE (Best Effort) = 0; AF11, AF12, AF13 = 10, 12, 14; AF21 through AF43 (4 classes times 3 drop-precedence levels); EF (Expedited Forwarding) = 46; CS0 through CS7 (Class Selector, backward compatible with the old IP precedence). Field type: **L3 header field**, subtype "QoS marking".

**History and background.** RFC 791 (1981) defined the ToS byte with precedence (3 bits), ToS bits (4 bits), and reserved. RFC 2474 (1998) redefined it as DSCP (6 bits) plus 2 reserved bits (which later became ECN). OF 1.0 (2009) defined `nw_tos` as 8-bit (the entire ToS byte). OF 1.2 (2011) split it into `ip_dscp` (6-bit, `OXM_OF_IP_DSCP`, `oxm_field=8`) and `ip_ecn` (2-bit, `oxm_field=9`), shifted right by 2 bits to fit a 6-bit nibble. Important: OF 1.2 changed the semantics. `ip_dscp` is the logical value 0 through 63, right-shifted from byte 1 of the IPv4 ToS field. Before OF 1.2 you used the raw 8-bit `nw_tos` (`nw_tos=184` is DSCP 46 EF combined with ECN 00 in the raw byte).

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.nw_tos` is 8-bit (OVS keeps the raw byte format). When serialising to the `ip_dscp` OXM, OVS right-shifts by 2 bits. MFF: `MFF_IP_DSCP` (`meta-flow.h`, type `u8` low 6 bits, prerequisite IP, `OXM_OF_IP_DSCP(8)` since OF 1.2 and OVS v1.7). The pre-OF 1.2 form is the legacy `MFF_IP_DSCP_SHIFTED`.

**Role.** Primary role: classify traffic by QoS class and select a queue or scheduler. Secondary role: marking and policy enforcement (the rule `priority=100,ip,ip_dscp=46,actions=set_queue:1,output:N` for EF traffic; `priority=100,ip,ip_dscp=0,actions=set_queue:0,output:N` for BE). In OVN, the ACL action `set_qos` maps to `mod_nw_tos` at the OpenFlow level.

**Why it exists.** IP networks must distinguish traffic priority (voice, video, control versus bulk transfer); the marking field has to live inside the IP header so its meaning survives every hop (intra-domain QoS) and does not depend on the 5-tuple. RFC 2474 modernised the old ToS byte. OF 1.0 used the raw 8-bit `nw_tos`, which is confusing (DSCP 46 has to be computed as `46 << 2 = 184`). OF 1.2 fixed this by splitting and shifting, so an operator can simply think "DSCP=46".

**Problems it solves.** Concretely: how do we expose the 6-bit DSCP cleanly to QoS rules. The OF 1.2 answer: a 6-bit field `ip_dscp` with logical values 0 through 63. Scope: every marking and matching use case for QoS classes. Combined with the `set_queue` action and the `Queue` table in OVSDB for egress shaping (see the QoS section in part 9.x if present).

**Importance.** Medium tier. Frequency is lower than `nw_src`, `nw_dst`, or `ip_proto`, but it is critical in deployments that carry voice and video, that have multi-tenant SLAs, or that integrate with upstream MPLS or EVPN that uses DSCP as input to MPLS EXP. An operator must know: the named DSCP value table (BE, AF, EF, CS), the right-shifted semantics in OF 1.2 and later, and the difference from raw `nw_tos`.

**How it works.** The datapath parses the ToS byte (IPv4) or the Traffic Class byte (IPv6) into `flow.nw_tos`. Classifier comparison: when the match expression uses `ip_dscp=46`, OVS internally compares `(flow.nw_tos >> 2) == 46` (which is `0x2e`, equals 46). When the match uses the raw `nw_tos=184`, the compare is direct. The action `mod_nw_tos:N` sets the entire 8 bits (and overwrites ECN), so use it carefully. The action `set_field:46->ip_dscp` left-shifts by 2 bits and ORs into the ToS byte, preserving ECN.

**OXM TLV byte layout.** `oxm_class=0x8000`, `oxm_field=8`, `hasmask=0`, `length=1`, value 1 byte (low 6 bits are meaningful, the high 2 bits are zero). Total 5 bytes. The specification does not allow `hasmask=1` (DSCP is an enum, masking is not meaningful).

**Common DSCP code points.**

| Name | Decimal | Binary (6-bit) | Raw ToS byte (DSCP<<2) | Use case |
|-----|---------|----------------|------------------------|----------|
| BE (CS0) | 0 | `000000` | 0 | Best Effort (default) |
| CS1 | 8 | `001000` | 32 | Scavenger (low-priority bulk) |
| AF11 | 10 | `001010` | 40 | Class 1, low drop |
| AF21 | 18 | `010010` | 72 | Class 2, low drop (multimedia conferencing) |
| AF31 | 26 | `011010` | 104 | Class 3, low drop (multimedia streaming) |
| AF41 | 34 | `100010` | 136 | Class 4, low drop (real-time interactive) |
| CS5 | 40 | `101000` | 160 | Voice (legacy) |
| EF | 46 | `101110` | 184 | Expedited Forwarding (voice, critical) |
| CS6 | 48 | `110000` | 192 | Network control (routing protocol) |
| CS7 | 56 | `111000` | 224 | Network control (highest) |

**Match examples.**

```
# Match VoIP traffic (EF) into a priority queue
priority=200,ip,ip_dscp=46,actions=set_queue:1,output:wan
# Same idea using the legacy nw_tos
priority=200,ip,nw_tos=184,actions=set_queue:1,output:wan

# Class scheduler
priority=150,ip,ip_dscp=34,actions=set_queue:2,output:wan  # AF41 multimedia
priority=100,ip,ip_dscp=10,actions=set_queue:3,output:wan  # AF11 normal
priority=10,ip,actions=set_queue:0,output:wan              # BE default

# Mark egress traffic (set DSCP)
priority=100,in_port=voip_port,ip,actions=mod_nw_tos:184,output:N
# Or use set_field, which preserves ECN
priority=100,in_port=voip_port,ip,actions=set_field:46->ip_dscp,output:N
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: memorise the named DSCP table (BE, AF, EF, CS); convert a raw `nw_tos` to DSCP using `nw_tos >> 2`. (2) **Edit**: prefer `set_field:N->ip_dscp` over `mod_nw_tos`, because `set_field` preserves the ECN bits. (3) **Observe**: `tcpdump -nn -v 'ip and (ip[1] & 0xfc) >> 2 == 46'` filters for EF. (4) **Debug**: when a marking does not stick, check whether a downstream rule does `mod_nw_tos:0` and overwrites it. When upstream MPLS does not honour the remark, check the ISP trust boundary.

**Source code reference.** Function `flow_extract` parses byte 1 of IPv4 into `flow.nw_tos`. Function `format_be_uint8_masked` formats the shifted-right value for `ip_dscp` display. Functions `nx_pull_match` and `oxm_pull_match` (`lib/nx-match.c`) decode the OXM TLV. Branch baseline OVS v2.17.9.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom A: a VoIP packet does not enter the priority queue. Check that the rule matches `ip_dscp=46`, and check that the phone actually sets `DSCP=46` (`tcpdump -v` shows the ToS byte). Symptom B: DSCP is stripped at a tunnel. The GRE, VXLAN, or Geneve encapsulation may not copy DSCP from inner to outer; enable DSCP copy or use the `set_tunnel_tos` action. Symptom C: marking with `mod_nw_tos:184` accidentally clears ECN on an ECN-aware packet, which degrades TCP performance. Use `set_field:46->ip_dscp` instead of the whole-byte `mod_nw_tos:N`.

**Tightly related to.** `ip_ecn` (the remaining 2 bits of the ToS byte), `nw_tos` legacy 8-bit (the raw byte before OF 1.2 split), the `set_queue` action (selects an egress queue), the `Queue` table in OVSDB (defines rate limits per queue), and the tunnel options `tos` (for GRE, VXLAN, and Geneve to copy DSCP).

**Version differences.** OF 1.0 used the raw 8-bit `nw_tos` (DSCP 46 became `nw_tos=184`). OF 1.2 (2011) split and shifted: `ip_dscp=46` instead of raw 184. The `OXM_OF_IP_DSCP` semantics are right-shifted per the specification. OVS keeps the `nw_tos` alias for backward compatibility. Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 plus OVN v22.03.8.

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ tcpdump -nn -v -i any 'ip and (ip[1] & 0xfc) == 184'   # filter for DSCP=46 (EF)
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep 'ip_dscp\|nw_tos'
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,ip,ip_dscp=46,nw_src=...'
Final flow: ...,nw_tos=184,...        # OVS shows raw nw_tos internally
```

**Real-world incident.** An ISP customer reported degraded VoIP quality after migrating to an OVN 22.03 cluster. Investigation: a trace showed that the phone sent `ip_dscp=46` (EF), but after Geneve encapsulation the outer header had `nw_tos=0`. Root cause: by default, the tunnel does not copy DSCP from inner to outer; the ISP MPLS core therefore did not see the EF marking in the outer header, and the priority queue did not activate. Fix: set `Open_vSwitch.other_config:hwol-flow-tunnel-tos=inherit` (or `options:tos=inherit` per port on the tunnel port) so the outer header inherits DSCP from the inner. Reference: tunnel options in `ovs-vswitchd.conf.db`.

**Synthetic exercise.** Mininet bridge `s1` with two hosts `h1` and `h2`. Set up three flows and two queues: `set Port s1-eth1 qos=@q1 -- --id=@q1 create qos type=linux-htb queues=0=@bg,1=@ef -- --id=@bg create queue other-config:max-rate=1000000 -- --id=@ef create queue other-config:max-rate=10000000`. Flows: `priority=100,ip,ip_dscp=46,actions=set_queue:1,output:1` and `priority=10,ip,actions=set_queue:0,output:1`. Generate two streams: `iperf3 --tos 184` (EF) and `iperf3` (BE). Verify that the EF stream gets the bandwidth priority by reading `tc -s qdisc show`.

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) Using `nw_tos=46` (wrong); that is the raw byte. Use `ip_dscp=46` or `nw_tos=184`. (b) `mod_nw_tos:184` clears the ECN bits on a TCP-ECN packet. Use `set_field:46->ip_dscp` to change only the 6 DSCP bits. (c) Forgetting the `ip` prerequisite (`eth_type=0x0800`) makes OVS reject the rule.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables `-m dscp --dscp 46` matches the same way; `tc filter` also matches DSCP for HTB queues. Cisco MQC `match dscp ef` uses the full name. NSX-T QoS profiles have an abstract "DSCP rewrite" on a rule. P4 uses `match(hdr.ipv4.diffserv)`, the full 8-bit field, so application code must shift to extract the 6-bit DSCP.

---

### 4.8.X.10 `ip_ecn`, the 2-bit ECN field for congestion notification

**Concept.** `ip_ecn` is a 2-bit match field that holds the Explicit Congestion Notification bits: the lowest 2 bits of the ToS byte (IPv4) or the Traffic Class byte (IPv6), defined by RFC 3168. There are 4 values: `00` Non-ECT (Not ECN-Capable Transport), `01` ECT(1), `10` ECT(0), and `11` CE (Congestion Experienced). An ECN-capable endpoint sets ECT(0) or ECT(1) on outbound packets; a router or switch can change ECT to CE to signal congestion instead of dropping the packet. Field type: **L3 header field**, subtype "congestion signalling".

**History and background.** RFC 2474 (1998) reserved the lower 2 bits of the ToS byte as "Currently Unused". RFC 3168 (2001) formalised them as ECN. OF 1.0 used the 8-bit `nw_tos` and did not split ECN. OF 1.2 (2011) split `ip_ecn` (2-bit, `OXM_OF_IP_ECN`, `oxm_field=9`) from `ip_dscp` (6-bit). `NXM_NX_IP_ECN` (code 28) was added in OVS v1.4 (2013) as a Nicira extension before OXM. Stable since OF 1.2.

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.nw_tos` holds all 8 bits; ECN is the low 2 bits. MFF: `MFF_IP_ECN` (`meta-flow.h`, type `u8` low 2 bits, prerequisite IP; `OXM_OF_IP_ECN(9)` since OF 1.2 and OVS v1.7; `NXM_NX_IP_ECN(28)` since v1.4). OVS extracts `(flow.nw_tos & 0x03)` as the match value for `ip_ecn`.

**Role.** Primary role: detect and signal congestion without dropping the packet (an ECN-aware TCP reduces its `cwnd` on receiving CE). Secondary role: a rule can count CE-marked traffic for observability (`priority=10,ip,ip_ecn=3,actions=output:congestion_monitor`); a router or switch can mark CE instead of dropping when the queue is nearly full (AQM with RED, CoDel, and PIE all have ECN-aware variants). In OVS plus OVN, few control-plane rules touch `ip_ecn` directly, but the kernel datapath through tc and qdiscs plus AQM can remark CE. Tunnel encapsulation must copy ECN correctly so that end-to-end ECN semantics survive.

**Why it exists.** Before ECN, the only congestion signal was packet drop, which forces TCP to retransmit and reduce `cwnd`. ECN lets an intermediate router give cwnd feedback without dropping, reducing latency and goodput loss. Splitting ECN out of the raw `nw_tos` in OF 1.2 gives an operator semantic-clean tooling: `set_field:3->ip_ecn` marks CE by changing only the low 2 bits, leaving DSCP intact.

**Problems it solves.** Concretely: how do we expose the 2-bit ECN to monitoring and remark rules without disturbing DSCP. The answer: split ECN into a separate field. Scope: every use case for observing or marking CE, integrating with kernel AQM (RED, CoDel, PIE qdiscs). Out of scope: end-to-end ECN negotiation (the TCP layer); not every upstream router honours ECN.

**Importance.** Tier 3 peripheral upgraded to medium tier because of the increasing prevalence of DCTCP and L4S deployments (datacenter ECN-aware congestion control). An operator must know: the 4-value table (0 = Not-ECT, 1 = ECT(1), 2 = ECT(0), 3 = CE), the tunnel ECN-copy semantics of RFC 6040, and the behaviour of qdiscs `red`, `codel`, and `pie` in ECN mode.

**How it works.** The datapath parses the ToS byte and uses `& 0x03` to extract ECN. The action `set_field:3->ip_ecn` (mark CE) ORs `0x03` into the low 2 bits of the ToS byte and keeps the 6 DSCP bits. The action `mod_nw_ecn:N` is equivalent. Tunnel encapsulation (VXLAN, Geneve, GRE) has an ECN propagation policy per RFC 6040: outer ECN equals inner ECN (full copy), or outer ECN is 0 (legacy mode). On decap: if outer is CE and inner is ECT, set inner to CE (signal back to the endpoint).

**OXM TLV byte layout.** `oxm_class=0x8000`, `oxm_field=9`, `hasmask=0`, `length=1`, value 1 byte (low 2 bits are meaningful, the high 6 bits are zero). Total 5 bytes. The specification does not allow `hasmask=1`.

**ECN values.**

| Decimal | Binary (2-bit) | Name | Meaning |
|---------|----------------|------|---------|
| 0 | `00` | Not-ECT | Endpoint does not support ECN; congestion means drop |
| 1 | `01` | ECT(1) | Endpoint is ECN-capable, code point 1 |
| 2 | `10` | ECT(0) | Endpoint is ECN-capable, code point 0 |
| 3 | `11` | CE | Congestion Experienced (a router has marked the packet) |

**Match examples.**

```
# Count CE-marked packets (observability)
priority=10,ip,ip_ecn=3,actions=output:ce_monitor,resubmit(,L3)

# Strip ECN at the boundary into a legacy ECN-incapable domain
priority=100,in_port=ecn_domain,out_port=legacy,ip,
                actions=set_field:0->ip_ecn,output:legacy

# Match ECN-aware traffic
priority=100,ip,ip_ecn=2,actions=set_queue:dctcp,output:N

# Mark CE at a congested point (AQM qdiscs usually do this; here is the test pattern)
priority=200,in_port=src,ip,actions=set_field:3->ip_ecn,output:N
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: distinguish the 4 ECN values; recognise a DCTCP or L4S deployment from the ECN distribution (a high CE rate signals congestion). (2) **Edit**: use `set_field:N->ip_ecn` to avoid touching DSCP; for tunnel propagation, check `options:tos=inherit` on the tunnel port. (3) **Observe**: `tcpdump -nn -v 'ip and (ip[1] & 0x03) == 3'` filters for CE; `tc -s qdisc show` shows the RED or CoDel ECN-mark count. (4) **Debug**: when DCTCP does not raise `cwnd` as expected, check whether the outer tunnel copies ECN. If a legacy upstream router clears the ECN bits, the deployment must strip ECN at the edge.

**Source code reference.** Function `flow_extract` parses byte 1 of IPv4 into `flow.nw_tos`, and ECN is extracted with `& 0x03`. Function `nx_pull_match` decodes the OXM for `MFF_IP_ECN`. The tunnel ECN propagation logic lives in `tnl_ecn_decap` and `tnl_ecn_encap` (`lib/tnl-ports.c`), following RFC 6040. Branch baseline OVS v2.17.9.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom A: DCTCP performance is poor. Check the tunnel `options:tos=inherit` for ECN propagation; check that the egress qdisc is ECN-aware (`red ecn`, `codel ce_threshold`). Symptom B: TCP throughput drops suddenly. A packet capture may show a CE flood; the AQM may be marking too aggressively. Symptom C: the ECN bits are cleared at a legacy-router boundary and DCTCP receives no signal. The deployment must downgrade to a non-ECN congestion control (CUBIC) or isolate the ECN domain.

**Tightly related to.** `ip_dscp` (the high 6 bits of the ToS byte), `nw_tos` legacy 8-bit (the full byte), the tunnel ECN-copy semantics from RFC 6040 (`options:tos=inherit` on Geneve and VXLAN ports), the kernel qdisc AQMs (RED, CoDel, PIE in ECN mode), and the DCTCP and L4S congestion controls.

**Version differences.** OF 1.0 had no separate ECN field (it used the raw 8-bit `nw_tos`). OF 1.2 (2011) split out the 2-bit `ip_ecn`. OVS `NXM_NX_IP_ECN` has been available since v1.4, ahead of OXM. RFC 6040 tunnel ECN copy (2010) is fully implemented in OVS since v2.4 (2015). The Linux kernel has supported TCP DCTCP since v3.18 (2014); L4S (RFC 9330 and friends) is in kernel 5.18 (2022). Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 plus OVN v22.03.8.

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ tcpdump -nn -v -i any 'ip and (ip[1] & 0x03) == 3'   # filter for CE
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep ip_ecn
$ tc -s qdisc show dev eth0   # count "marked" packets in the RED or CoDel ECN mode
qdisc red 8001: dev eth0 root limit 1000 ... marked 142 ecn
```

**Real-world incident.** A multi-rack DCTCP cluster reported good intra-rack RTT (under 1 ms) but a 40 percent cross-rack throughput drop. Investigation: `tcpdump` showed that the outer Geneve ECN bits were always 0 even when inner was ECT(0) or CE. Root cause: the OVS tunnel port did not set `options:tos=inherit`, so the default copied DSCP=0 for the entire outer ToS byte. Fix: `ovs-vsctl set Interface ovn-xx options:tos=inherit`; verify with `tcpdump` that the outer ECN matches the inner. Reference: tunnel options in `ovs-vsctl(8)` and RFC 6040.

**Synthetic exercise.** Mininet bridge `s1` with two hosts `h1` and `h2` running DCTCP (`sysctl net.ipv4.tcp_congestion_control=dctcp`). Install a RED-ECN qdisc on the link from `s1` to `h2`: `tc qdisc add dev s1-eth2 root red limit 100000 min 30000 max 90000 burst 50 ecn`. Install a CE-counting flow: `priority=100,ip,ip_ecn=3,actions=output:ce_count_port`. Generate traffic with `iperf3 -c h2` and gradually increase the number of concurrent streams. Watch the counter on `ce_count_port` rise as RED begins marking. Verify that the sender reduces `cwnd` instead of retransmitting (low `RetransSegs` and stable `OutSegs` in TCP counters).

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) Using `mod_nw_tos:N` overwrites the ECN of an end-to-end packet and breaks DCTCP. Use `set_field:N->ip_dscp` instead if you only want to change DSCP. (b) The tunnel does not copy ECN, so DCTCP and L4S do not work across tunnels. Set `options:tos=inherit`. (c) Matching `ip_ecn=3` to find CE never hits because the next-hop router reset ECN to 0 (it does not honour ECN). The deployment must strip ECN at the edge.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables `-m ecn --ecn-ip-ect` matches the ECN bits. Cisco WRED has an `ecn` keyword for ECN-aware marking instead of drop. NSX-T does not expose ECN directly in the UI but the underlying datapath honours it. A P4 program reads `hdr.ipv4.diffserv` (the full 8-bit field), so the application must mask `& 0x03` to extract ECN. OVS is unique in fully integrating RFC 6040 tunnel propagation.

### 4.8.X.11 `tp_src`, the shared L4 source port for TCP, UDP, and SCTP

**Concept.** `tp_src` (transport source port) is the generalised name for the 16-bit L4 source port. It is an alias for three concrete fields, dispatched by `ip_proto`: `tcp_src` when `ip_proto=6` (TCP), `udp_src` when `ip_proto=17` (UDP), and `sctp_src` when `ip_proto=132` (SCTP). OVS accepts `tp_src=N` in a rule and dispatches to the right concrete field based on the matched `ip_proto`. Format: integer 0 through 65535 (the IANA port range), bitwise mask supported. Field type: **L4 header field**, subtype "endpoint identifier (source)".

**History and background.** OF 1.0 (2009) introduced a single 16-bit field named `tp_src`, used for both TCP and UDP, at bytes 0 and 1 of the L4 header after the IPv4 or IPv6 header. OF 1.2 (2011) split it into three separate OXMs (`OXM_OF_TCP_SRC=13`, `OXM_OF_UDP_SRC=15`, `OXM_OF_SCTP_SRC=17`) so an operator can express the protocol context clearly and the classifier dispatches without ambiguity. OVS keeps `tp_src` as a backward-compatible alias: a rule like `tcp,tp_src=80` is internally rewritten to `eth_type=0x0800,ip_proto=6,tcp_src=80` by the parser. NXM legacy: `NXM_OF_TCP_SRC(9)` and `NXM_OF_UDP_SRC(11)` since OVS v1.1 (2011); `OXM_OF_TCP_SRC` since OVS v1.7 (2014); `OXM_OF_SCTP_SRC` since v2.0 (2014).

**Position in the architecture.** OVS userspace does not have a separate `flow.tp_src` per protocol; `struct flow` has a single `flow.tp_src` 16-bit big-endian member (`include/openvswitch/flow.h`). It is one field, but MFF splits it into three views (`MFF_TCP_SRC`, `MFF_UDP_SRC`, and `MFF_SCTP_SRC` in `meta-flow.h`), and `mf_check_src_prereqs` enforces the right `ip_proto` before allowing the match. The TSS classifier subtables branch first by `eth_type` and `ip_proto`, and `tp_src` is only looked up in the subtable for the chosen L4 branch.

**Role.** Primary role: identify the L4 source endpoint for forward-direction rules. Secondary roles: source-port ACLs (`priority=100,tcp,tp_src=22,actions=drop` blocks outbound traffic from an SSH server), NAT identification (the source port of the connection before SNAT, stored together with the 5-tuple in conntrack), DDoS heuristics (a narrow random source-port range plus a flood is a scan or SYN-flood pattern), and debug filters (`tcpdump` and `ovs-ofctl` counts by source port).

**Why it exists.** Every L4 rule needs to know both endpoint ports (source and destination) to express the direction of a connection. A server listens on a well-known destination port (HTTP=80, SSH=22, DNS=53), and the client uses a random source port. A rule has to distinguish "traffic to the server" (`tp_dst=80`) from "traffic out of the server" (`tp_src=80`); without one of these fields you cannot write a stateless reply rule. OF 1.0 gave the field the compact name `tp_*`; OF 1.2 split it explicitly because TCP and UDP have different payload structures, and a hardware classifier with two separate parsers is cleaner.

**Problems it solves.** Concretely: how do we expose a 16-bit source port in a rule while keeping the semantics correct per L4 protocol. The answer: the `tp_src` alias rewrites to a concrete OXM based on `ip_proto`. Scope: every ACL, NAT, and LB rule that needs a source-port dimension. Out of scope: the QUIC port inside the UDP payload (which needs a higher-layer parser), and ICMP, which has no port (use `icmp_type` and `icmp_code` instead).

**Importance.** Medium tier, high frequency in stateful and reply-path rules. An operator must know: the `tp_src` alias is only valid after the rule has a clear `ip_proto` (or one of the aliases `tcp`, `udp`); `tp_src` cannot match ICMP or GRE packets; a bitwise mask is useful for matching a port range (such as the ephemeral high-bit range).

**How it works.** The datapath parses bytes 0 and 1 of the L4 header (offset equals IP header length times 4) into `flow.tp_src`. The classifier inspects `flow.nw_proto` to choose the MFF view (`MFF_TCP_SRC`, `MFF_UDP_SRC`, or `MFF_SCTP_SRC`). A rule that uses the `tp_src` alias is rewritten to the right MFF during `mf_check_src_prereqs` (if the prerequisite is missing, the parser rejects the rule). Action `mod_tp_src:N` sets the field (legacy form) and `set_field:N->tcp_src` is the OXM-style form. Both are only valid when `ip_proto` is right. In NAT, `ct(commit,nat(src=...))` implicitly rewrites `flow.tp_src` according to the conntrack rule.

**OXM TLV byte layout.** Tuỳ context:

```
TCP source port (ip_proto=6):
| oxm_class=0x8000 | oxm_field=13 | hasmask=0 | length=2 | value(2) |        Total 6 byte
| 0x80 0x00       | (13<<1)=0x1A | 0         | 0x02     | port_be   |

UDP source port (ip_proto=17):
| oxm_class=0x8000 | oxm_field=15 | hasmask=0 | length=2 | value(2) |        Total 6 byte
| 0x80 0x00       | (15<<1)=0x1E | 0         | 0x02     | port_be   |

SCTP source port (ip_proto=132):
| oxm_class=0x8000 | oxm_field=17 | hasmask=0 | length=2 | value(2) |        Total 6 byte
| 0x80 0x00       | (17<<1)=0x22 | 0         | 0x02     | port_be   |
```

Masked variant length=4 (header+value+mask).

**Match examples.**

```
# Block traffic from a DNS server (source port 53)
priority=100,udp,tp_src=53,actions=output:dns_log,resubmit(,ACL)

# Allow replies from an HTTP server (source port 80)
priority=100,tcp,tp_src=80,ct_state=+est,actions=output:client

# Match the ephemeral port range (32768 to 60999 by default on Linux, masking the high bit)
priority=10,tcp,tp_src=0x8000/0xc000,actions=goto_table:lb

# Use tcp_src explicitly instead of the alias
priority=100,tcp,tcp_src=22,actions=drop  # block outbound SSH from the server
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: distinguish the context. `tp_src=80` inside a `tcp` rule is different from inside `udp` or `sctp`; OVS dispatches based on `ip_proto`. (2) **Edit**: prefer explicit `tcp_src`, `udp_src`, or `sctp_src` in programmatic rules; the `tp_src` alias is fine for short manual rules. (3) **Observe**: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep -E '(tp_src|tcp_src|udp_src)'` shows the source-port distribution in the rules; `tcpdump -nn 'tcp src port 80'` cross-checks. (4) **Debug**: a miss on `tp_src=N` is usually a missing `ip_proto`; run `ovs-appctl ofproto/trace` and check that `Matched flow` shows the right `tcp_src=N`.

**Source code.** Function `flow_extract` (`lib/flow.c`) parses `tp_src` from the first L4 header byte. Prerequisite validation lives in `mf_check_src_prereqs` (`lib/meta-flow.c`). The MFF definitions `MFF_TCP_SRC`, `MFF_UDP_SRC`, and `MFF_SCTP_SRC` are in `include/openvswitch/meta-flow.h`. Branch baseline OVS v2.17.9; cite via function or MFF name as the stable anchor (Rule 14 §14.4 Option C).

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom A: a rule `tcp,tp_src=80` does not match a server reply. Check that the packet really is TCP (TLS-over-QUIC is UDP). Symptom B: parser error "tp_src missing prerequisite". Add `tcp` or `ip,ip_proto=6` in front. Symptom C: a bitwise mask such as `tp_src=0x0050/0xfff0` matches nothing because of endian confusion. The IANA value is intuitively little-endian, but OVS stores it big-endian; verify with `ofproto/trace`.

**Tightly related to.** `ip_proto` (the direct prerequisite, values 6, 17, or 132), `tp_dst` (the destination-port counterpart), `tcp_flags` (the same TCP L4 context), `ct_tp_src` (the original `tp_src` before NAT), the `set_field` action (rewrites `tp_src`), and `nat(src=ip:port)` inside the `ct()` action (NAT rewrite).

**Version differences.** OF 1.0 used the shared 16-bit `tp_src`. OF 1.2 (2011) split it into separate OXMs for TCP, UDP, and SCTP. OVS v1.1 added `NXM_OF_TCP_SRC` and `NXM_OF_UDP_SRC`; v1.7 added `OXM_OF_TCP_SRC` and `OXM_OF_UDP_SRC`; v2.0 added `OXM_OF_SCTP_SRC(17)`. Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 plus OVN v22.03.8.

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,tcp,tp_src=80,tp_dst=12345,...'
Matched flow: ...,tcp,tp_src=80,...
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep tp_src
$ tcpdump -nn -i any 'tcp src port 80'
```

**Real-world incident.** An OVN cluster applied a deny-all egress ACL to a tenant, plus the rule `priority=100,udp,tp_src=53,actions=allow` to permit DNS-server replies. The dashboard reported DNS resolution failures. Investigation: `ovn-trace` showed the DNS response (`udp,tp_src=53`) matching the allow rule, but the connection-tracking lookup on the packet returning to the ephemeral client port (`tp_dst`) was returning `+inv`. The reply packet had `tp_src=53`, but the conntrack original tuple stored a different value because the client first sent through the local DNS server and the reply was forwarded. Fix: switch to `ct(commit,zone=N)` on the outbound side so conntrack preserves the original tuple.

**Synthetic exercise.** Mininet bridge `s1` with host `h1` (client) and `h2` (HTTP server). Install two flows: `priority=100,tcp,tp_dst=80,actions=ct(commit,zone=1),output:2` and `priority=100,tcp,tp_src=80,ct_state=+est,actions=output:1`. Verify that a request from `h1` to `h2:80` matches flow 1, and the response from `h2` (with `tp_src=80`) matches flow 2. Change `tp_src=80` to `tp_src=8080` and verify that the response cannot pass.

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) Forgetting `ip_proto` (a bare `tp_src=80`) is rejected by the parser. (b) Endian confusion in hex (`tp_src=0x5000`); OVS expects host byte order in the rule text. (c) Applying `tp_src` to an ICMP packet is a silent miss because ICMP has no port; use `icmp_type` and `icmp_code` instead.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables `--sport N` (the alias works for both TCP and UDP). A Cisco ACL `permit tcp any eq 80 any` uses `eq 80` implicitly as the source port. NSX-T DFW has a `service` field that abstracts source and destination ports together. P4 dispatches the port through a per-protocol header parser (`hdr.tcp.src_port` and `hdr.udp.src_port` are separate), inherited from the OF 1.2 decision to split the field by protocol.

---

### 4.8.X.12 `tp_dst`, the shared L4 destination port for TCP, UDP, and SCTP

**Concept.** `tp_dst` is the destination-port counterpart of `tp_src`, a 16-bit L4 destination port that dispatches to `tcp_dst` (`ip_proto=6`), `udp_dst` (`ip_proto=17`), or `sctp_dst` (`ip_proto=132`). The format and masking are the same as `tp_src`. This is **the most heavily used field in production OpenFlow rules**: it identifies the service (HTTP=80, HTTPS=443, SSH=22, DNS=53, NTP=123, more recently QUIC over UDP=443, DoH=443), it matches the Load Balancer VIP port, and it filters ACLs by service. Field type: **L4 header field**, subtype "endpoint identifier (destination)".

**History and background.** A direct counterpart of `tp_src`: OF 1.0 introduced the shared name `tp_dst`; OF 1.2 split it into `OXM_OF_TCP_DST=14`, `OXM_OF_UDP_DST=16`, and `OXM_OF_SCTP_DST=18`. NXM legacy: `NXM_OF_TCP_DST(10)` and `NXM_OF_UDP_DST(12)` since OVS v1.1; the OXM equivalents arrived in v1.7 (TCP and UDP) and v2.0 (SCTP).

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.tp_dst` is 16-bit big-endian at the same place as `flow.tp_src` (offset bytes 2 and 3 of the L4 header). MFF splits into `MFF_TCP_DST`, `MFF_UDP_DST`, and `MFF_SCTP_DST` (`include/openvswitch/meta-flow.h`). The classifier uses the same dispatch logic by `eth_type` plus `ip_proto` as `tp_src`, but with separate subtable lookups for destinations.

**Role.** Primary role: identify the service and route the packet into the right service-handling pipeline (HTTP traffic into the HTTP ACL, SSH into the SSH ACL, DNS into the DNS resolver). Secondary roles: LB VIP port match (`priority=100,tcp,tp_dst=80,actions=ct(commit,nat(dst=backend:8080))`); DNAT identification for the reverse path (`ct_tp_dst` stores the original); per-service rate limiting (the rule `priority=100,udp,tp_dst=53,actions=meter:1,output:dns`).

**Why it exists.** A server-side service listens on a well-known port, the contract between client and server (the RFC 6335 IANA registry). A rule that wants to identify the service must read the destination port. Without `tp_dst`, you would have to parse the application-level payload (HTTP method, DNS query), which is too expensive on CPU and breaks the modular layering. OF 1.0 paired `tp_dst` with `tp_src` as a basic pair from the start. OF 1.2 split it per protocol because hardware parsers and pipeline silicon are cleaner when the field is explicit.

**Problems it solves.** Concretely: how do we route and filter traffic by service using only L3 plus L4 header matching. The answer: a 16-bit field with a bitwise mask, dispatched by `ip_proto`. Scope: every TCP, UDP, or SCTP service in the IANA registry. Out of scope: services running on a non-standard port (HTTP on 8080) need an explicit rule; services multiplexed onto a single port (multi-tenant HTTPS with SNI, gRPC over HTTP/2) need application-level parsing beyond OVS.

**Importance.** A central match field for L4 forwarding, though as an OF match field on its own it sits at a medium tier of the catalog because `tp_dst` is already anatomized at the system level in section 4.8.8. Frequency is very high: most L4 rules in OVN, OpenStack, and Kubernetes OVN-K (other than pure L2 or L3 paths) carry `tp_dst`. Operator must-know list: the IANA well-known top-30 port table (HTTP, HTTPS, SSH, DNS, NTP, SMTP, IMAP, POP3, RDP, MySQL, Postgres, Redis, Memcached, etcd, Kubernetes API), bitwise mask for port range (1024 and up = high bit), conntrack zone interaction.

**Cơ chế hoạt động.** Datapath parse byte 2-3 của L4 header vào `flow.tp_dst`. Classifier subtable lookup chỉ valid sau khi `eth_type` + `ip_proto` đã match. Rule alias `tp_dst` rewrite về MFF cụ thể trong `mf_check_src_prereqs`. NAT rewrite (`ct(nat(dst=ip:port))`) đổi `flow.tp_dst` ngầm và lưu original vào `ct_tp_dst` cho reverse mapping ở reply path.

**OXM TLV byte layout.** Tuỳ context (cùng layout với `tp_src` nhưng oxm_field +1):

```
TCP destination port  : oxm_field=14, total 6 byte
UDP destination port  : oxm_field=16, total 6 byte
SCTP destination port : oxm_field=18, total 6 byte
```

Masked variant length=4. Tổng 8 byte.

**Ví dụ match.**

```
# Route HTTP to web pipeline
priority=100,tcp,tp_dst=80,actions=goto_table:web_acl

# Load Balancer VIP match
priority=200,tcp,tp_dst=80,nw_dst=10.0.0.100,
                actions=ct(commit,nat(dst=10.0.1.5:8080)),output:backend

# Block all UDP except DNS (53), NTP (123), DHCP (67/68)
priority=100,udp,tp_dst=53,actions=output:dns
priority=100,udp,tp_dst=123,actions=output:ntp
priority=100,udp,tp_dst=67,actions=output:dhcp_server
priority=100,udp,tp_dst=68,actions=output:dhcp_client
priority=10,udp,actions=drop

# Match high port range (ephemeral)
priority=10,tcp,tp_dst=0x8000/0x8000,actions=goto_table:lb
```

**Cách kỹ sư vận hành thành thạo.** (1) **Đọc**: nhớ bảng IANA port top-30; biết rằng QUIC-over-UDP cùng port 443 cần phân biệt qua `ip_proto` thay vì port. (2) **Sửa**: ưu tiên `tcp_dst/udp_dst/sctp_dst` explicit khi rule generated bởi controller (OVN northd luôn explicit); alias `tp_dst` cho rule debug manual. (3) **Quan sát**: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep -E 'tp_dst=|tcp_dst=|udp_dst=' | sort -t= -k2 -n` xem distribution port. (4) **Debug**: nếu LB VIP traffic không tới backend, ovn-trace check `tp_dst` được rewrite chưa; nếu service nghe trên port phi-tiêu-chuẩn (8080, 9090, ...), rule phải explicit value đó.

**Source code.** Function `flow_extract` (`lib/flow.c`) parse `tp_dst`. NAT rewrite trong `conntrack_execute` + `nat_packet` (`lib/conntrack.c`); reverse mapping `ct_tp_dst` lưu trong conntrack entry. OVN compile LB rule sinh `tp_dst` match trong `build_lb_vips_lflows` (`northd/northd.c`). Branch baseline OVS v2.17.9.

**Khi xảy ra sự cố.** Triệu chứng A: traffic to VIP không tới backend — ovn-trace check rule LB có match `tp_dst=VIP_port` đúng; check `nat(dst=...)` rewrite `flow.tp_dst` thành backend port; nếu VIP và backend cùng port số nhưng IP khác, vẫn cần rewrite IP. Triệu chứng B: service trên port phi-tiêu-chuẩn miss rule — rule chỉ match port standard (`tp_dst=80`), update rule. Triệu chứng C: QUIC traffic (UDP 443) bị block do rule chỉ allow `tcp,tp_dst=443` — thêm `udp,tp_dst=443` cho QUIC.

**Liên quan mật thiết tới.** `ip_proto` (prerequisite), `tp_src` (đối ứng), `tcp_flags` (cùng TCP context), `ct_tp_dst` (original trước NAT), `nat(dst=ip:port)` action, `set_field` action, OVN `Load_Balancer.vips` schema (key là `IP:port` bao gồm tp_dst).

**Khác biệt giữa các phiên bản.** OF 1.0 chung `tp_dst`. OF 1.2 (2011) tách 3 OXM. OVS v1.1 NXM_OF_TCP_DST/UDP_DST; v1.7 OXM_OF_TCP_DST/UDP_DST; v2.0 OXM_OF_SCTP_DST. Curriculum baseline OVS v2.17.9 + OVN v22.03.8.

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,tcp,tp_src=12345,tp_dst=80,...'
Matched flow: ...,tcp,tp_dst=80,actions=...
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | awk '/tp_dst=80/{print}'
$ tcpdump -nn -i any 'tcp dst port 80'
```

**Trường hợp sự cố thực tế.** OVN LB cấp VIP `10.0.0.100:443` cho HTTPS service, backend `10.0.1.5:8443`. Sau migrate thêm gRPC service trên cùng VIP nhưng port 443 (HTTP/2). Investigation: rule LB chỉ rewrite `tp_dst` từ 443 sang 8443; gRPC request tới VIP 443 cũng được rewrite, vì gRPC backend nghe ở port khác (9090). Fix: tạo VIP riêng `10.0.0.101:443` cho gRPC trỏ tới backend 9090, hoặc cùng VIP nhưng routing layer phía trên (Envoy SNI).

**Bài tập synthetic.** Mininet bridge `s1`, h1 client, h2/h3 backend nghe HTTP port 8080. Cài LB rule: `priority=100,tcp,tp_dst=80,nw_dst=10.0.0.100,actions=ct(commit,nat(dst=10.0.0.2:8080)),output:2` và rule reverse path. Verify `curl http://10.0.0.100/` từ h1 tới được h2:8080. Đếm packet qua `dump-flows`, kiểm `n_packets` flow LB tăng.

**Lỗi thường gặp + tín hiệu chẩn đoán.** (a) `tp_dst=N` đứng một mình thiếu protocol — parser reject. (b) Nhầm port well-known (HTTPS = 443 chứ không phải 442) — kiểm IANA registry. (c) Service phi-standard không cập nhật rule — log "no match" khi traffic flow qua bridge.

**So sánh với hệ khác.** Linux iptables `--dport N`. Cisco ACL `permit tcp any any eq 80` (`eq 80` ngầm là dst port nếu đứng cuối). NSX-T DFW abstract `service` (HTTP, HTTPS) ánh xạ sang `tp_dst` cụ thể. P4 parser dispatch tương tự sau khi parse IPv4/IPv6 → TCP/UDP state.

---

### 4.8.X.13 `tcp_flags` — bitmap cờ điều khiển TCP 12-bit

**Khái niệm.** `tcp_flags` là match field 16-bit (low 12 bit có ý nghĩa) chứa các cờ điều khiển trong header TCP: byte 13 của TCP header (RFC 9293). 9 bit định nghĩa lịch sử và 3 bit reserved cho tương lai (data offset shift trừ ra). Bit name (LSB to MSB): FIN(0), SYN(1), RST(2), PSH(3), ACK(4), URG(5), ECE(6), CWR(7), NS(8). Spec OVS quy định "low 12 bits" để dự phòng bit mở rộng tương lai; bit 9-11 hiện reserved zero. Format match expression dùng alias name + dấu `+`/`-`: `tcp_flags=+syn-ack` nghĩa là SYN set + ACK clear, các bit khác wildcard. Loại field: **L4 TCP header field**, sub-loại "control flags bitmap".

**Lịch sử + bối cảnh.** OF 1.0 không có. NXM_NX_TCP_FLAGS(34) đưa vào OVS v2.1 (2014) như Nicira extension. OF 1.3 thêm `ONFOXM_ET_TCP_FLAGS(42)` qua ONF experimenter từ OVS v2.4. OF 1.5 (2015) chính thức hoá `OXM_OF_TCP_FLAGS(42)` từ OVS v2.3. Mục đích: cho phép rule stateless TCP sanity check + half-open connection detection mà không cần conntrack state machine.

**Vị trí trong kiến trúc.** `struct flow.tcp_flags` 16-bit big-endian (`include/openvswitch/flow.h`). MFF: `MFF_TCP_FLAGS` (`meta-flow.h`, type be16 low 12 bits, prereqs TCP, **read-only** — không có action set field này, lý do: TCP flags là semantic của connection state, override bằng OF action sẽ phá TCP). Classifier nhánh sau khi đã match `ip_proto=6`.

**Vai trò.** Vai trò chính: stateless TCP filtering + sanity check (drop packet có flag combination không hợp lệ như SYN+FIN, hoặc all-flags-zero). Vai trò phụ: half-open detection (`+syn-ack` = SYN-only initiate connection request); RST flood detection (`+rst` count); fin_timeout integration (`+fin` trigger conntrack timeout shorter); stateful firewall pre-filter trước khi vào `ct()` (giảm load cho conntrack table).

**Vì sao sinh ra.** Pre-`tcp_flags`, rule muốn distinguish SYN-only packet phải parse byte 13 TCP header thủ công qua extension match — tedious + error-prone. Conntrack stateful giải quyết phần lớn case nhưng overhead non-trivial: mỗi packet vào conntrack tốn CPU + memory cho entry. Stateless `tcp_flags` match cho phép pre-filter packet rõ-ràng-bất-thường trước khi bỏ vào conntrack: SYN flood drop ngay, malformed flag combo drop ngay.

**Vấn đề giải quyết.** Cụ thể: làm sao rule biểu đạt "match packet có cờ X set + cờ Y clear" mà không parse byte raw. Cách giải: 16-bit bitmap với bitwise mask + alias name `+syn-ack`. Phạm vi: tất cả use case TCP filtering không cần connection state. Không giải quyết: full stateful firewall (cần conntrack); ECN-CE marking trên TCP packet (cần `ip_ecn`).

**Tầm quan trọng.** Tier 2 medium. Frequency: cao trong rule firewall + DDoS mitigation; thấp trong OVN baseline pipeline (OVN dùng conntrack thay). Engineer must-know: bảng 9 cờ + alias name; combinations chính: `+syn-ack` (SYN-only), `+ack` (handshake hoàn tất hoặc data), `+fin+ack` (graceful close), `+rst` (abort), invalid combos (`+syn+fin`, `+syn+rst`, all-zero).

**Cơ chế hoạt động.** Datapath parse byte 13 TCP header (offset = ip_hl + 13) vào `flow.tcp_flags` 16-bit. Classifier match qua bitwise: rule `tcp_flags=+syn-ack` tương đương value `0x002 mask 0x012` (SYN bit 1 = 0x002, ACK bit 4 = 0x010, mask hai bit = 0x012, value chỉ SYN = 0x002). Format string parser (`lib/meta-flow.c::mf_parse_tcp_flags__`) decode `+name`/`-name` thành value+mask. Read-only: không có MFF_TCP_FLAGS write hooks; rule action `set_field:N->tcp_flags` bị reject.

**OXM TLV byte layout.**

```
| oxm_class=0x8000 | oxm_field=42 | hasmask=0/1 | length=2 hoặc 4 | value(2)[mask(2)] |
| 0x80 0x00       | (42<<1)=0x54 | 0 hoặc 1     | 0x02 hoặc 0x04   | tcp_flags_be      |
```

Tổng 6 byte (exact) hoặc 8 byte (masked). Bitwise mask cực phổ biến vì engineer hiếm khi quan tâm tất cả 9 cờ cùng lúc.

**Bảng cờ TCP + alias.**

| Bit | Hex | Tên | Ý nghĩa | Dùng nhiều trong rule? |
|-----|-----|-----|---------|------------------------|
| 0 | 0x001 | FIN | End connection (graceful close) | Có (fin_timeout, conntrack tear-down) |
| 1 | 0x002 | SYN | Initiate connection | Rất nhiều (handshake detect, SYN flood) |
| 2 | 0x004 | RST | Reset/abort | Có (RST flood, conntrack invalid) |
| 3 | 0x008 | PSH | Push buffer to app | Hiếm |
| 4 | 0x010 | ACK | Acknowledge prev seq | Rất nhiều (cặp với SYN/FIN) |
| 5 | 0x020 | URG | Urgent pointer valid | Hiếm (legacy) |
| 6 | 0x040 | ECE | ECN Echo (RFC 3168) | DCTCP/L4S |
| 7 | 0x080 | CWR | Congestion Window Reduced | DCTCP/L4S |
| 8 | 0x100 | NS | Nonce Sum (RFC 3540) | Rất hiếm |

**Ví dụ match.**

```
# Drop SYN flood (SYN-only nhiều lần từ cùng src)
priority=200,tcp,tcp_flags=+syn-ack,actions=meter:syn_rate_limit,goto_table:next

# Block invalid flag combination
priority=300,tcp,tcp_flags=+syn+fin,actions=drop
priority=300,tcp,tcp_flags=+syn+rst,actions=drop
priority=300,tcp,tcp_flags=0/0xfff,actions=drop  # all-zero (XMAS scan reverse)

# Accelerate FIN handshake với fin_timeout
priority=100,tcp,tcp_flags=+fin,actions=fin_timeout(idle_timeout=5),output:N

# DCTCP signal monitor
priority=10,tcp,tcp_flags=+ece,actions=output:dctcp_log,resubmit(,fwd)

# Match handshake hoàn tất (cho ACL allow-related)
priority=100,tcp,tcp_flags=+ack,actions=output:N
```

**Cách kỹ sư vận hành thành thạo.** (1) **Đọc**: hiểu cặp cờ chính (SYN+ACK = handshake step 2; FIN+ACK = graceful close); biết invalid combo (XMAS, NULL, SYN+FIN). (2) **Sửa**: không thể write field này (read-only); để control flag, tạo packet mới hoặc dùng RST inject ở controller. (3) **Quan sát**: `tcpdump -nn 'tcp[tcpflags] & tcp-syn != 0 and tcp[tcpflags] & tcp-ack == 0'` filter SYN-only; OVS dùng `tcp_flags=+syn-ack`. (4) **Debug**: rule không hit như expected, dùng `ofproto/trace` show `Matched flow: ...,tcp_flags=0xN` để verify giá trị decode.

**Source code.** Function `flow_extract_l4_tcp` parses TCP header byte 13 into `flow.tcp_flags`. Function `mf_parse_tcp_flags__` (`lib/meta-flow.c`) decodes the `+name` or `-name` syntax into a value plus mask. Function `format_tcp_flags` prints the bitmap in human-readable form. MFF: `MFF_TCP_FLAGS` is defined in `meta-flow.h` with the source comment "OXM: ONFOXM_ET_TCP_FLAGS(42) since OF1.3 and v2.4, OXM_OF_TCP_FLAGS(42) since OF1.5 and v2.3, NXM: NXM_NX_TCP_FLAGS(34) since v2.1". Branch baseline OVS v2.17.9.

**Khi xảy ra sự cố.** Triệu chứng A: SYN flood vẫn vào conntrack table → table fill nhanh → packet drop OOM. Fix: thêm rule pre-filter `tcp_flags=+syn-ack,actions=meter:rate,goto_table:fwd` hoặc rate limit qua `set_queue`. Triệu chứng B: rule `tcp_flags=+syn` (mong "any packet with SYN set") không hit packet SYN+ACK → vì cú pháp `+syn` không clear bit khác, OVS hiểu "SYN bit must be 1, others wildcard". Đúng cú pháp: `+syn` đã đúng — nếu vẫn miss, kiểm tra `ip_proto=6` đúng. Triệu chứng C: action `set_field:0x002->tcp_flags` bị OVS reject "field is read-only" — dùng controller-injected packet thay.

**Liên quan mật thiết tới.** `ip_proto=6` (prerequisite), `tcp_src/tcp_dst` (cùng TCP context), `fin_timeout` action (kích hoạt khi flag FIN đến), `ct_state` (conntrack derives state từ tcp_flags timeline), `ip_ecn` (ECE+CWR cờ phối hợp với ECN bit IP layer).
