**Khác biệt giữa các phiên bản.** OF 1.0/1.1 không có. OVS v2.1 (2014) thêm NXM_NX_TCP_FLAGS(34). OVS v2.3 thêm OXM_OF_TCP_FLAGS(42) (OF 1.5 draft). OVS v2.4 thêm ONFOXM_ET_TCP_FLAGS(42) (OF 1.3 experimenter). OF 1.5 (2015) chính thức hoá. Flag NS (bit 8, RFC 3540) deprecated từ RFC 9293 (2022) nhưng OVS vẫn parse cho legacy. Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9.

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,tcp,tcp_flags=0x002,...'
Matched flow: ...,tcp_flags=syn,actions=...
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep tcp_flags
$ tcpdump -nn -i any 'tcp[tcpflags] == tcp-syn'   # bắt SYN-only
```

**Trường hợp sự cố thực tế.** Cluster OVN cấp ACL allow-established cho tenant; rule `priority=100,tcp,ct_state=+est,actions=allow` cho reply. Một service backend bị SYN flood, conntrack table fill 95%, packet legit bị drop. Investigation: SYN flood packet đi qua `ct(commit)` tạo entry mới mỗi packet, không có pre-filter. Fix: thêm rule `priority=300,tcp,tcp_flags=+syn-ack,actions=meter:syn_rate_5pps,resubmit(,ct)` trước stage ct, rate limit SYN-only ở 5pps/source. Conntrack table giảm xuống 30%.

**Bài tập synthetic.** Mininet, h1 client tạo TCP connection sang h2:80. Trên s1 bridge cài 4 flow đếm: `priority=100,tcp,tcp_flags=+syn-ack,actions=note:0x01,output:2`, `+syn+ack` -> note 0x02, `+ack` (chỉ ACK) -> note 0x03, `+fin+ack` -> note 0x04. Dùng `ovs-ofctl dump-flows` đếm `n_packets` mỗi flow sau request HTTP GET. Verify timeline: 1 SYN-only + 1 SYN+ACK + nhiều ACK + 1 FIN+ACK ở đầu close.

**Lỗi thường gặp + tín hiệu chẩn đoán.** (a) Cú pháp sai (`tcp_flags=syn` thiếu dấu `+`) — parser warning, có thể fall-through thành value nguyên. (b) Tin rằng có thể `set_field` field này — silent ignore hoặc error. (c) Quên prerequisite `tcp` — reject parser.

**So sánh với hệ khác.** Linux iptables `-m tcp --tcp-flags SYN,ACK SYN` (mask SYN+ACK, value chỉ SYN). Cisco IOS ACL `match tcp ... eq established` (high-level abstraction tương đương `+ack`). NSX-T abstract qua "TCP State" rule. P4 program đọc `hdr.tcp.flags` trực tiếp, application logic cụ thể từng bit. OVS unique ở alias `+name/-name` thân thiện với engineer.

---

### 4.8.X.14 `ct_mark` — nhãn 32-bit gắn vào conntrack entry

**Khái niệm.** `ct_mark` là field 32-bit metadata gắn với conntrack entry (không phải với packet riêng lẻ): khi packet được commit vào conntrack table qua `ct(commit, exec(set_field:0xN->ct_mark))`, giá trị này được lưu trong conntrack entry và áp dụng cho tất cả packet thuộc cùng connection (cả forward + reply direction). Packet sau, khi đi qua `ct()` action với `ct_state=+trk`, sẽ có `flow.ct_mark = entry.ct_mark`, cho phép rule downstream match. Loại field: **conntrack metadata field**, sub-loại "tag-based marker per connection".

**Lịch sử + bối cảnh.** Conntrack mark là khái niệm Linux netfilter có từ kernel 2.6.x (`xt_CONNMARK`). OVS thêm conntrack action vào v2.5 (2016) với `NXM_NX_CT_MARK(107)` — không có OXM tương ứng vì conntrack không phải standard OF concept. ONF không chuẩn hoá `ct_*` field; OVS giữ NXM extension. Stable từ OVS v2.5 đến nay.

**Vị trí trong kiến trúc.** `struct flow.ct_mark` 32-bit (`include/openvswitch/flow.h`). MFF: `MFF_CT_MARK` (`meta-flow.h`, type be32, prereqs none — không yêu cầu IP, vì có thể conntrack chạy cho non-IP traffic hypothetically; thực tế prereq ngầm là `ct_state=+trk`). Storage thực tế: conntrack entry `struct nf_conn` trong kernel datapath hoặc `struct conn` trong userspace conntrack (`lib/conntrack.c`). Khi packet vào `ct()` action recirculate, datapath populate `flow.ct_mark` từ entry.

**Vai trò.** Vai trò chính: persistent tagging connection cho ACL downstream. Rule pattern phổ biến: tag connection lúc commit (`ct(commit,exec(set_field:0x1234->ct_mark))`), rồi rule downstream match `ct_mark=0x1234` để áp policy không phụ thuộc 5-tuple — kể cả khi NAT đổi address, mark vẫn theo connection. Vai trò phụ: cross-pipeline communication (OVN dùng `ct_mark` cho LB session affinity, port security verify); state migration (mark tagged "trusted" qua commit rồi datapath fast-path bypass deeper inspection).

**Vì sao sinh ra.** Stateful firewall cần "đính kèm" thông tin classification vào connection để rule sau không phải re-classify mỗi packet. Pre-`ct_mark`, mỗi packet phải re-evaluate ACL từ đầu (CPU expensive với rule table lớn). Linux netfilter giải quyết qua CONNMARK; OVS port khái niệm sang flow table với `ct_mark` field. Đặc biệt hữu dụng cho NAT scenario: post-NAT 5-tuple thay đổi nhưng mark giữ nguyên → rule reverse path không cần kiến thức về NAT mapping.

**Vấn đề giải quyết.** Cụ thể: làm sao gắn 32-bit metadata persistent với connection. Cách giải: lưu trong conntrack entry, populate vào `flow.ct_mark` mỗi khi packet đi qua `ct()`. Phạm vi: tag-based ACL (allow/deny per connection class), session affinity, NAT-resilient marker. Không giải quyết: per-packet metadata (dùng `metadata` field 64-bit thay), cross-host mark synchronization (conntrack là local kernel state).

**Tầm quan trọng.** Tier 2 medium. Frequency: trung bình cao trong OVN deployment (`Logical_Switch_Port.security` dùng `ct_mark` để verify port security state); rất cao trong stateful firewall pattern. Engineer must-know: chỉ valid sau `ct_state=+trk`, set qua `exec(set_field:N->ct_mark)` bên trong `ct()` action, persistent across packet trong cùng connection.

**Cơ chế hoạt động.** Lúc commit: rule `ct(commit, exec(set_field:0x1234->ct_mark))` recirculate packet vào datapath conntrack handler, tạo `struct conn` entry + lưu mark = 0x1234. Lúc reply path / subsequent packet: packet đi qua `ct()` action lookup conntrack entry by 5-tuple, populate `flow.ct_mark = entry.mark`, recirculate vào pipeline với `ct_state=+trk`. Update mark qua action mới: `ct(commit, exec(set_field:0x5678->ct_mark))` overwrite (bitwise mask supported: `set_field:0x10/0xff->ct_mark` chỉ đổi byte thấp).

**OXM TLV byte layout.** Chỉ NXM (không có OXM):

```
| nxm_class=0x0001 | nxm_field=107 | hasmask=0/1 | length=4 hoặc 8 | value(4)[mask(4)] |
| 0x00 0x01       | (107<<1)=0xD6 | 0 hoặc 1     | 0x04 hoặc 0x08   | mark_be           |
```

Tổng 8 byte (exact) hoặc 12 byte (masked). Bitwise mask hữu dụng cho `ct_mark` chia thành sub-field (8-bit class + 8-bit subclass + 16-bit reserved chẳng hạn).

**Ví dụ match + set.**

```
# Commit connection với tag class=0x10 (web tier)
priority=200,tcp,tp_dst=80,actions=ct(commit,zone=1,exec(set_field:0x10->ct_mark)),output:N

# Reply path: match ct_mark để allow related
priority=100,tcp,ct_state=+est+trk,ct_mark=0x10,actions=output:client

# Update mark ở rule sau (escalate priority class)
priority=150,tcp,ct_mark=0x10,tp_src=443,actions=ct(commit,exec(set_field:0x20/0xff->ct_mark))

# Quarantine connection: tag suspicious
priority=300,tcp,tcp_flags=+syn,actions=ct(commit,exec(set_field:0xff->ct_mark)),controller

# Policy enforcement downstream
priority=10,ct_mark=0xff,actions=drop  # drop quarantined
```

**Cách kỹ sư vận hành thành thạo.** (1) **Đọc**: hiểu rule pattern "commit-then-tag" + "match-tag-downstream"; biết bitwise mask cho phép set sub-field độc lập. (2) **Sửa**: dùng `exec(set_field:N->ct_mark)` bên trong `ct()` (không phải standalone `set_field:N->ct_mark` — OVS reject vì cần conntrack context); update qua `ct(commit, exec(...))` mới. (3) **Quan sát**: `ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack | grep mark=` xem mark per entry; `ofproto/trace` show `ct_mark=0xN` ở Final flow. (4) **Debug**: nếu rule downstream không match, check `dpctl/dump-conntrack` xem entry có mark đúng chưa; nếu mark không update, kiểm cú pháp `exec()` đúng (ví dụ `set_field:0x10->ct_mark`, không phải `set_field:0x10->NXM_NX_CT_MARK[]`).

**Source code.** Function `conn_update_state` (`lib/conntrack.c`) update mark trong `struct conn`. Function `xlate_ct_action` (`ofproto/ofproto-dpif-xlate.c`) xử lý `ct(exec(...))` thực thi nested action. OVN compile ACL qua `build_acls` (`northd/northd.c`) sinh `ct_mark` rule trong stage `LS_OUT_ACL_HINT`. Branch baseline OVS v2.17.9.

**Khi xảy ra sự cố.** Triệu chứng A: rule `ct_mark=0x10` không hit dù vừa commit — kiểm packet đã đi qua `ct(zone=N)` recirculate chưa, `ct_state` có `+trk` không. Triệu chứng B: mark giảm về 0 ngẫu nhiên — conntrack entry timeout + tái tạo (timer expire); kéo dài timeout qua `ct(timeout=name)` với named timeout policy. Triệu chứng C: `set_field:0x10->ct_mark` standalone reject — phải nằm trong `ct(exec(...))`.

**Liên quan mật thiết tới.** `ct_state` (prerequisite ngầm `+trk`), `ct_zone` (mark scope theo zone, mark cùng giá trị ở zone khác là entry độc lập), `ct_label` (đối ứng 128-bit), `ct()` action với `commit` + `exec()` clause, OVN `Logical_Switch_Port.security` schema, Linux netfilter CONNMARK target.

**Khác biệt giữa các phiên bản.** OVS v2.5 (2016) thêm `NXM_NX_CT_MARK(107)`. OVS v2.6 thêm bitwise mask support. OVS v2.10 thêm named `ct(timeout=...)` integration (mark có thể survive longer trong flow-specific timeout policy). Không có OXM equivalent (ONF không chuẩn hoá conntrack). Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 + OVN v22.03.8.

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack | grep -E 'mark|10\.0'
tcp,orig=(src=10.0.0.1,dst=10.0.0.2,sport=12345,dport=80),
    reply=(src=10.0.0.2,dst=10.0.0.1,sport=80,dport=12345),
    protoinfo=(state=ESTABLISHED),mark=0x10,zone=1
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,tcp,...,ct_state=+est+trk,ct_mark=0x10'
Matched flow: ...,ct_mark=0x10,actions=output:N
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep ct_mark
```

**Trường hợp sự cố thực tế.** OVN tenant dùng ACL allow-related với `ct_mark` tag để phân biệt connection web (mark=0x10) và DB (mark=0x20). Sau upgrade OVS từ 2.13 lên 2.17, một số connection mất mark random. Investigation: `dpctl/dump-conntrack` cho thấy entry vẫn tồn tại nhưng `mark=0x0`. Root cause: rule update mark dùng `set_field:0x10->ct_mark` standalone (không trong `ct(exec(...))`); OVS 2.13 silent ignore + giữ mark cũ; OVS 2.17 reset mark về 0 khi gặp standalone set_field. Fix: sửa rule thành `ct(commit,exec(set_field:0x10->ct_mark))`.

**Bài tập synthetic.** Mininet `s1` với 2 host h1, h2. Rule: (a) `priority=100,tcp,tp_dst=80,actions=ct(commit,zone=1,exec(set_field:0x10->ct_mark)),output:2`; (b) `priority=100,tcp,ct_state=+trk,ct_mark=0x10,actions=output:1` (reply). Verify HTTP GET h1 → h2 thành công. `dpctl/dump-conntrack | grep mark=0x10` confirm entry. Lặp test với mark khác (0x20 cho port 22 SSH); verify rule (b) không match SSH connection.

**Lỗi thường gặp + tín hiệu chẩn đoán.** (a) `set_field:N->ct_mark` standalone — OVS reject hoặc silent ignore. (b) Quên `ct_state=+trk` khi match — rule không hit packet chưa qua conntrack. (c) Mark scope nhầm zone — connection cùng 5-tuple zone khác là entry độc lập, mark không share.

**So sánh với hệ khác.** Linux netfilter CONNMARK target `-j CONNMARK --set-mark 0x10` cùng concept (kernel ancestor). NSX-T abstract qua "Tags" object ở SDN policy layer (cao hơn OVS). Cisco IOS Zone-Based Firewall có "class-map" với metadata tagging tương đương. P4 program lưu metadata trong custom struct, application logic tự quản lý lifetime.

---

### 4.8.X.15 `ct_label` — nhãn 128-bit gắn vào conntrack entry

**Khái niệm.** `ct_label` là field 128-bit metadata gắn với conntrack entry, ý nghĩa và workflow giống `ct_mark` nhưng width lớn hơn 4 lần (32 bit → 128 bit). Hỗ trợ tagging connection với nhiều phân loại độc lập đồng thời (ví dụ: 16 bit tenant ID + 16 bit security class + 16 bit application + 16 bit reserved + 64 bit policy version), hoặc lưu UUID dạng compact 128-bit. Set qua `ct(commit,exec(set_field:0xN->ct_label))`, match qua `ct_label=0xN` với bitwise mask. Loại field: **conntrack metadata field**, sub-loại "extended tag-based marker per connection".

**Lịch sử + bối cảnh.** OVS v2.5 (2016) thêm cùng đợt `ct_mark`: `NXM_NX_CT_LABEL(108)`. Mục đích: cho phép tagging granular hơn `ct_mark` 32-bit khi có nhiều dimension classification (multi-tenant + multi-class + version stamp). 128-bit chọn vì là multiple của 32 (alignment) và đủ lớn cho UUID. Không có OXM tương đương (ONF không chuẩn hoá). Stable từ v2.5.

**Vị trí trong kiến trúc.** `struct flow.ct_label` 128-bit (`union ct_label`, big-endian). MFF: `MFF_CT_LABEL` (`meta-flow.h`, type be128, prereqs none — ngầm `ct_state=+trk`). Storage thực tế: conntrack entry `struct conn.label[16]` (16 byte = 128 bit). Pipeline: packet vào `ct()` recirculate → datapath populate `flow.ct_label` từ entry.

**Vai trò.** Vai trò chính: extended tagging connection cho complex policy (multi-dimensional classification). Vai trò phụ: UUID storage (tracker session ID), policy version stamp (rollout feature flag per connection), audit metadata (lưu source rule ID hoặc admin user ID lúc commit). Trong OVN: `Logical_Switch_Port.security` + ACL hint dùng `ct_label` cho stateful annotation phức tạp; Load_Balancer session affinity dùng partial `ct_label` cho hash key.

**Vì sao sinh ra.** `ct_mark` 32-bit đủ cho single-dimension classification nhưng quá hẹp cho multi-tenant cloud: 1 connection cần tag (tenant_id 16-bit) + (security_class 8-bit) + (qos_class 8-bit) → đã hết 32-bit, không còn slot cho version stamp hay audit. 128-bit `ct_label` giải quyết bằng cách tăng vùng metadata, kết hợp bitwise mask cho phép set/match từng sub-field độc lập như `ct_label=0x10/0xff` (chỉ low byte = 0x10).

**Vấn đề giải quyết.** Cụ thể: làm sao gắn metadata phong phú (multi-dimension hoặc UUID) với connection mà không bị giới hạn 32-bit. Cách giải: 128-bit be128 type, hỗ trợ bitwise mask. Phạm vi: tất cả use case cần > 32-bit metadata per connection. Không giải quyết: cross-host metadata sync (conntrack vẫn local), payload-based classification (cần parse application).

**Tầm quan trọng.** Tier 2 medium. Frequency: thấp hơn `ct_mark` (không phải case nào cũng cần > 32-bit), nhưng critical trong OVN advanced ACL với hint encoding (xem `northd/northd.c` `build_acl_hint_lflows`). Engineer must-know: width 128-bit yêu cầu syntax hex extended (`ct_label=0x0123456789abcdef0123456789abcdef`), bitwise mask để partial set/match, `set_field` luôn trong `ct(exec(...))`.

**Cơ chế hoạt động.** Lúc commit: `ct(commit, exec(set_field:0x10->ct_label))` set 128-bit field (giá trị scalar được zero-pad lên 128-bit). Bitwise mask `set_field:0x10/0xff->ct_label` chỉ đổi 8 bit thấp, các byte cao giữ nguyên. Match phía sau: `ct_label=0x10/0xff` so sánh masked value. Datapath storage: `struct conn.label[16]` byte array; lookup qua entry pointer trong `nf_conn` (kernel) hoặc `struct conn` (userspace conntrack).

**OXM TLV byte layout.** Chỉ NXM (không có OXM):

```
| nxm_class=0x0001 | nxm_field=108 | hasmask=0/1 | length=16 hoặc 32 | value(16)[mask(16)] |
| 0x00 0x01       | (108<<1)=0xD8 | 0 hoặc 1     | 0x10 hoặc 0x20     | label_be128         |
```

Tổng 20 byte (exact) hoặc 36 byte (masked). Đây là một trong các MFF có TLV size lớn nhất cùng `MFF_IPV6_SRC/DST`.

**Pattern phân chia 128-bit.**

| Pattern | Bit range | Use case |
|---------|-----------|----------|
| Tenant ID + Security class + QoS class + Version stamp | 16+8+8+96 | Multi-tenant cloud ACL |
| UUID (RFC 4122) | 128 | Session tracker, audit ID |
| Bitmap policy flags (64 flag) + reserved | 64+64 | Feature flag per connection |
| OVN ACL hint (per stage) | 64+64 | Stage matching cho multi-table pipeline |

**Ví dụ match + set.**

```
# Commit với multi-dimensional tag: tenant=0x100 (low 16 bit), security=0x05 (next 8 bit)
priority=200,tcp,tp_dst=443,actions=ct(commit,zone=1,
                exec(set_field:0x05000100/0xffffffff->ct_label)),output:N

# Match tenant only (mask high 112 bit)
priority=100,tcp,ct_state=+est+trk,ct_label=0x100/0xffff,actions=output:tenant_log

# Match security class only
priority=100,tcp,ct_state=+est+trk,ct_label=0x05000000/0xff000000,actions=output:high_sec

# Set audit UUID (full 128-bit)
actions=ct(commit,exec(set_field:0x123e4567e89b12d3a456426614174000->ct_label))

# Update partial: chỉ đổi tenant không động security
priority=150,ct_label=0x05000000/0xff000000,
                actions=ct(commit,exec(set_field:0x200/0xffff->ct_label))
```

**Cách kỹ sư vận hành thành thạo.** (1) **Đọc**: thiết kế bit layout trước khi deploy (document rõ tenant 16 bit thấp, security 8 bit kế, ...); dùng bitwise mask consistent. (2) **Sửa**: luôn `ct(exec(...))`; partial update qua mask. (3) **Quan sát**: `ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack -m | grep label=` (cờ `-m` show metadata). (4) **Debug**: nếu match miss, check value endian — hex string đọc big-endian, OVS lưu big-endian, output `ofproto/trace` show MSB-first.

**Source code.** Function `conn_update_label` (`lib/conntrack.c`) update label trong `struct conn`. Function `xlate_ct_action` (`ofproto/ofproto-dpif-xlate.c`) xử lý nested set. OVN compile ACL hint qua `build_acl_hints_lflows` (`northd/northd.c`) sinh `ct_label` rule với bit pattern mã hoá per ACL stage. Branch baseline OVS v2.17.9.

**Khi xảy ra sự cố.** Triệu chứng A: hex value 128-bit nhập sai (thiếu byte) — OVS pad zero-MSB; có thể không phải intent. Verify qua `dpctl/dump-conntrack -m`. Triệu chứng B: bitwise mask sai layout (set sub-field A nhưng mask cover sub-field B) — partial update accidentally clear B; document layout chặt chẽ. Triệu chứng C: rule downstream không hit do byte order — OVS hex string là big-endian, các tool khác (Linux netfilter conntrack labels có alias name) có thể differ; cross-check qua `ofproto/trace`.

**Liên quan mật thiết tới.** `ct_state` (prerequisite ngầm `+trk`), `ct_zone`, `ct_mark` (đối ứng 32-bit), `ct()` action `commit` + `exec()`, OVN `build_acl_hints_lflows`, Linux netfilter conntrack labels (`nf_connlabels` cho user-space tagging API).

**Khác biệt giữa các phiên bản.** OVS v2.5 (2016) thêm `NXM_NX_CT_LABEL(108)`. OVS v2.6 cải thiện bitwise mask. OVS v2.7 OVN bắt đầu dùng `ct_label` cho ACL hint (`build_acl_hints_lflows`). Không có OXM equivalent. Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 + OVN v22.03.8.

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack -m | grep -E 'label|10\.0'
tcp,orig=(src=10.0.0.1,dst=10.0.0.2,sport=12345,dport=443),
    reply=(...),protoinfo=(state=ESTABLISHED),
    mark=0,zone=1,labels=0x05000000000000000000000000000100
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,tcp,...,ct_state=+est+trk'
Final flow: ...,ct_label=0x05000000000000000000000000000100,...
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep ct_label
```

**Trường hợp sự cố thực tế.** OVN tenant deploy ACL hint encoding tenant_id ở bit 0-15 + security_class bit 16-23. Sau upgrade rule controller, một số connection mất security class (về 0). Investigation: rule mới dùng `set_field:0x100/0xffffffff->ct_label` với mask `0xffffffff` (32-bit) — clear 96 byte cao + chỉ giữ 32 bit thấp; security class ở bit 16-23 nằm trong mask phạm vi nên bị overwrite. Fix: mask chính xác `0xffff` cho tenant only (`set_field:0x100/0xffff`), giữ security class nguyên.

**Bài tập synthetic.** Mininet bridge với 2 host. Cài 3 flow: (a) commit với label = `0x10` (tenant 0x10), (b) update security class qua mask `0x0500/0xff00` (security 0x05 ở bit 8-15), (c) match `ct_label=0x10/0xff,ct_label=0x500/0xff00` (kết hợp). Verify connection có cả tenant 0x10 + security 0x05 đi qua flow (c). Test variant: nếu mask sai (b dùng `0x0500/0xffff`), tenant bị clear.

**Lỗi thường gặp + tín hiệu chẩn đoán.** (a) Mask quá rộng làm clear sub-field khác — log `dpctl/dump-conntrack` show label đột ngột thay đổi. (b) Hex string quá ngắn (<32 hex char) — OVS pad zero MSB; test endian. (c) Standalone `set_field:N->ct_label` ngoài `ct(exec(...))` — reject hoặc silent ignore.

**So sánh với hệ khác.** Linux netfilter `nf_connlabels` cho user-space label API (giới hạn 128 bit từ kernel 3.13+). NSX-T abstract qua "Tags" object multi-dimension. P4 program dùng custom metadata struct với explicit bit field. OVS unique ở `ct_label` cho phép bitwise mask granular trong rule text mà không cần parse application payload.

---

### 4.8.X.16 `vlan_pcp`, the 3-bit Priority Code Point of an 802.1Q VLAN tag

**Concept.** `vlan_pcp` is a 3-bit match field that holds the **Priority Code Point (PCP)** value in the Tag Control Information (TCI) of an 802.1Q VLAN tag (IEEE 802.1Q-2018 §9.6). The PCP occupies bits 13 to 15 of the 16-bit TCI, alongside the 12-bit VID (bits 0 to 11) and the 1-bit DEI (bit 12). Values 0 to 7 map directly to the eight priority levels defined by IEEE 802.1p for L2 QoS, where 0 is the lowest (Best Effort) and 7 is the highest (Network Control). The legacy alias is `dl_vlan_pcp`. Field type: **L2 header field**, subtype "per-frame QoS classifier".

**History and background.** PCP originates from IEEE 802.1p (1998), the first QoS standard for Ethernet. It was later merged into 802.1Q-2003 and has been stable since. OpenFlow 1.0 (2009) defined `OFPMT_DL_VLAN_PCP` as an 8-bit struct field (3 low bits used, 5 high bits reserved as zero). OF 1.2 (2011) standardized it as the OXM TLV `OXM_OF_VLAN_PCP` (oxm_class=0x8000, oxm_field=7). In upstream OVS the MFF identifier `MFF_VLAN_PCP` is defined in `include/openvswitch/meta-flow.h`. The block documentation states `OXM_OF_VLAN_PCP(7) since OF1.2 and v1.7`, a `u8` type with 3 low bits used, the prerequisite is the VLAN VID, and there is no NXM equivalent. Stable from OF 1.2 onward.

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.vlan_tcis[0]` is a 16-bit big-endian field that holds VID, PCP, and DEI together. The OVS parser extracts the PCP through the macro `vlan_tci_to_pcp` by taking the top 3 bits of the TCI. The MFF is `MFF_VLAN_PCP` in `meta-flow.h`. In the 4.8 taxonomy this field belongs to Group D, the L2 Ethernet fields, alongside `vlan_vid` (covered in §4.8.X.5). Note that `vlan_pcp` is not an independent field on the wire. It is always co-located with VID and DEI inside the same 4-byte VLAN header (TPID 0x8100 followed by the TCI). The datapath extracts the PCP through a bit shift at the same time as the VID.

**Role.** The primary role is to classify frames by L2 QoS priority so that different queueing or policing policies can be applied. In OVS, the rule `priority=100,vlan_pcp=7,actions=set_queue:7,output:N` directs Network Control traffic into priority queue 7 (configured through `ovs-vsctl set port eth0 qos=@newqos ...`). In OVN provider networks where a `localnet` port carries a VLAN tag, `vlan_pcp` is carried through from the tenant VM out to the physical fabric (Cisco or Juniper) for consistent QoS. A secondary role is detecting misconfigured marking. For example, an audit rule `priority=10,vlan_pcp=0,actions=resubmit(,policy_audit)` logs packets that arrive without QoS marking from an untrusted tenant. The scope is per-frame and applies only to frames with a VLAN tag. Untagged frames have no PCP.

**Why it exists.** Before 802.1p, Ethernet had no L2 priority mechanism. Every frame received the same treatment, so important traffic (Network Control, voice) competed for queue capacity against best-effort traffic. 802.1p was introduced so that hardware QoS could operate at the L2 switch (eight queues per egress port, with a PCP-to-queue mapping) before the packet reached an L3 router that could inspect DSCP. OpenFlow exposes the PCP to the control plane so that flow rules can match and rewrite it. This is useful for boundary policy: re-marking when traffic leaves the tenant for the fabric, or stripping when the tenant marker cannot be trusted.

**Problems it solves.** The concrete question is how to enforce programmable L2 QoS in OVS, instead of relying only on the hardware silicon of an ASIC switch. The solution is to expose `vlan_pcp` as a first-class field, combined with the action `set_field:N->vlan_pcp` (the standard rewrite) or `mod_vlan_pcp:N` (the legacy alias). The scope covers the eight classic 802.1p priority levels for L2 QoS in data center and campus networks. It does not cover L3 QoS (which uses `ip_dscp` and `ip_ecn`, see §4.8.X.9 and §4.8.X.10) or application-layer QoS (which would require parsing the application payload).

**Importance.** Medium. The frequency is medium in enterprise data centers that carry voice and video traffic and lower in cloud-native data centers, where most Geneve overlay traffic does not carry the VLAN PCP in the outer header. An operator must know the eight 802.1p levels (0 = BE Best Effort, 1 = BK Background, 2 = EE Excellent Effort, 3 = CA Critical Applications, 4 = VI Video less than 100 ms, 5 = VO Voice less than 10 ms, 6 = IC Internetwork Control, 7 = NC Network Control). They must know that `vlan_vid` must be present as a prerequisite (an untagged frame has no PCP), and the interaction with the `set_queue` action and the OVS QoS framework.

**How it works.** The datapath parses the Ethernet header. If bytes 12 to 13 are `0x8100` (the VLAN TPID), it parses the next 4-byte VLAN header. Bytes 14 to 15 carry the 16-bit TCI. The datapath shifts `(tci >> 13) & 0x07` to extract the 3-bit PCP and populates `flow.vlan_tcis[0]` with both PCP and VID. A match `vlan_pcp=N` compares the exact 3 bits. The action `set_field:N->vlan_pcp` modifies the TCI in place on the existing VLAN tag without pushing a new tag. If the frame is untagged, the action silently has no effect. In the OVS classifier, `vlan_pcp` rarely creates its own subtable, because the 3-bit cardinality is low. It is usually grouped with VID into the same subtable.

**OXM TLV byte layout.** `oxm_class=0x8000` (OPENFLOW_BASIC), `oxm_field=7`, `hasmask=0`, `oxm_length=0x01`, value 1 byte (3 low bits = PCP, 5 high bits reserved as zero):

```
| 0x80 0x00 | 0x0E | 0x01 | 0x07 |    # OXM_OF_VLAN_PCP, length=1, value PCP=7 (Network Control)
| oxm_class | f|hm | len  | val  |
```

Total TLV: 5 bytes. The OF 1.5 specification does not allow `hasmask=1` (a 3-bit space is too small for masking to be useful in practice). NXM: not defined (PCP is OXM-only from OF 1.2 onward).

**Eight 802.1p priority levels (IEEE 802.1Q-2018 Annex I).**

| PCP | Name | Typical use case |
|-----|------|------------------|
| 0 | BE (Best Effort) | Default unmarked traffic |
| 1 | BK (Background) | Bulk transfer (backup, batch) |
| 2 | EE (Excellent Effort) | Business-critical low-priority |
| 3 | CA (Critical Applications) | Transactional databases, ERP |
| 4 | VI (Video, less than 100 ms) | Streaming video, conferencing |
| 5 | VO (Voice, less than 10 ms) | VoIP, real-time voice |
| 6 | IC (Internetwork Control) | Routing protocol packets |
| 7 | NC (Network Control) | Switch heartbeat, BFD, STP |

**Match and write examples.**

```
# Direct voice traffic into priority queue 5 on port eth0 (OVS hardware QoS)
priority=100,vlan_vid=100,vlan_pcp=5,actions=set_queue:5,output:eth0

# Strip PCP coming from an untrusted tenant before sending to the fabric (re-mark to BE)
priority=200,in_port=tap_untrusted,vlan_vid=100,actions=set_field:0->vlan_pcp,output:trunk

# Match Network Control PCP=7 for monitoring and audit
priority=150,vlan_vid=4094,vlan_pcp=7,actions=resubmit(,nc_audit_table)

# Re-mark video PCP 4 up to 5 for a high-priority lab
priority=120,vlan_pcp=4,actions=mod_vlan_pcp:5,output:N

# Multi-VLAN policy: VLAN 100 allows PCP 0 to 3, drops PCP 4 to 7
priority=100,vlan_vid=100,vlan_pcp=4,actions=drop
priority=100,vlan_vid=100,vlan_pcp=5,actions=drop
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read.** Memorize the eight 802.1p levels and their use cases. Understand the PCP-to-queue mapping of the hardware switch (Cisco maps PCP to queue by default; Cisco NX-OS may be configured otherwise). (2) **Edit.** Use `set_field:N->vlan_pcp` (the standard form) or `mod_vlan_pcp:N` (the legacy form). Always include a fallback rule that strips PCP from untrusted ports. (3) **Observe.** Use `tcpdump -e -i eth0 'vlan'` to view the TCI hex and decode the PCP from the high byte. Use `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep vlan_pcp` to count the distribution. (4) **Debug.** If QoS is not working, verify that the frame is actually tagged (`tcpdump -e` shows the 802.1Q line), that the PCP has the expected value, and that `ovs-vsctl list qos` confirms the queue mapping is configured correctly.

**Classification.** L2 header field (real wire bit), exact-match only (no `hasmask`), readable and writable (through `set_field` or `mod_vlan_pcp`). Prerequisite: `vlan_vid` must be present (an untagged frame has no PCP slot). Different from `ip_dscp` and `ip_ecn` (L3 QoS, with an IP prerequisite) and from `metadata` and `reg*` (metadata, not header).

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,vlan_vid=100,vlan_pcp=5,...'
Final flow: ...,vlan_tci=0x10a4,...   # 0x10a4 = present(0x1000) | pcp(5<<13)>>... | vid(100)
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | awk '/vlan_pcp/{print}'
$ tcpdump -e -nn -i eth0 'vlan'
14:23:01.123456 aa:bb:..  vlan 100, p 5, ethertype IPv4, ...   # "p 5" = PCP 5
```

**Source code reference.** The function `vlan_tci_to_pcp` (`include/openvswitch/flow.h`) shifts 3 bits to extract the PCP from the TCI. The function `flow_set_vlan_pcp` (`lib/flow.c`) is the write path used by the `set_field` action. The function `format_vlan_tci` (`lib/match.c`) formats the output of `dump-flows`. Branch baseline: OVS v2.17.9. The block documentation in `meta-flow.h` for `MFF_VLAN_PCP` states `OXM_OF_VLAN_PCP(7) since OF1.2 and v1.7`. Per Rule 14.4 Option C, function names are used as stable anchors instead of line numbers.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom A: a rule `vlan_pcp=5` does not hit even though `tcpdump` shows the tag. Check whether `vlan_vid` was specified as a prerequisite. Add either `vlan_vid=N/0x1000` (which checks only the present flag, any VID) or a specific VID. Symptom B: the action `set_field:5->vlan_pcp` is a no-op on an untagged frame. The failure is silent. Use `push_vlan:0x8100,set_field:N->vlan_pcp` instead so that the action takes effect. Symptom C: PCP carry-through is lost when the packet traverses a Geneve tunnel. This is expected, because the Geneve outer header does not carry the inner PCP. The workaround is to encapsulate the value through `tun_metadata` or to use DSCP propagation instead of PCP.

**Tightly related to.** `vlan_vid` (the direct prerequisite, covered in §4.8.X.5), the DEI bit (1-bit Drop Eligible Indicator in the same TCI; OVS does not expose it separately), `ip_dscp` (the L3 QoS counterpart), the `set_queue` action (which consumes PCP to choose a hardware queue), the OVS QoS framework (`ovs-vsctl set port qos=@q`), and the 802.1p specification.

**Version differences.** OF 1.0 used `dl_vlan_pcp`, an 8-bit struct (with 3 useful bits). OF 1.2 standardized it as `OXM_OF_VLAN_PCP`, a 1-byte field. OF 1.3 onward did not change it. OVS v1.7 (2012) added OXM support; before that it used the OF 1.0 struct. Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 plus OVN v22.03.8.

**Real-world incident.** An enterprise data center used OVS as the edge bridge for a VoIP gateway, with voice frames marked PCP=5 toward the fabric. After migrating from an ASIC switch to OVS, voice quality degraded (jitter increased). Investigation: `tcpdump -e -i trunk 'vlan'` showed the frame correctly tagged with PCP=5, but `ovs-ofctl dump-flows` showed that the QoS rule was not hitting. Root cause: the existing rule `priority=100,vlan_pcp=5,...` was missing the `vlan_vid` prerequisite. OVS silently rejected the rule, so it was never installed. Fix: add `vlan_vid=100/0x0fff,vlan_pcp=5,...`. The rule then became active and voice quality recovered.

**Synthetic exercise.** Use a Mininet bridge `s1` with two hosts h1 and h2 on a trunk port. Configure h1 to send traffic on VLAN 100 with PCP=5 (use `vconfig` plus `tc qdisc` to set the skb priority). Install two flows on s1: (a) `priority=100,vlan_vid=100,vlan_pcp=5,actions=set_field:7->vlan_pcp,output:2`, (b) `priority=10,vlan_vid=100,actions=output:2`. Verify that h2 receives the frame with PCP=7 (re-marked). Read the counter on flow (a) to confirm the match. Test variant: send PCP=3. Flow (a) misses, flow (b) hits, and h2 receives the original PCP=3.

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) Forgetting the `vlan_vid` prerequisite. OVS rejects the rule with `Failed to parse: ...` at `add-flow`. (b) Applying `mod_vlan_pcp` to an untagged frame. The action is a silent no-op. Confirm with `tcpdump -e` that no tag is present. (c) A PCP value greater than 7. OVS rejects the parse because of the 3-bit space. (d) A downstream hardware switch overriding the PCP (for example, Cisco trusts DSCP by default rather than CoS). Configure `mls qos trust cos` on the port to honor the PCP from the OVS upstream.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux: `ip link add link eth0 name eth0.100 type vlan id 100 egress-qos-map 0:0 1:1 ... 7:7` maps the skb priority to the PCP. Cisco IOS: `mls qos trust cos` plus a per-PCP `policy-map` for hardware QoS. NSX-T abstracts this through a QoS Profile object whose `class_of_service` field maps implicitly to the PCP. A P4 program defines a parser state for VLAN that parses the TCI field and uses `transition select(tci.pcp)` for dispatch, which inherits the OpenFlow concept.

---

### 4.8.X.17 `ipv6_src`, the 128-bit source IPv6 address of a packet

**Concept.** `ipv6_src` is a 128-bit match field that holds the source IPv6 address: 16 bytes at offset 8 to 23 of the IPv6 header (RFC 8200 §3). Match formats include colon-separated hex `2001:db8::1` (exact match), CIDR prefixes such as `fe80::/10` (for link-local), `2001:db8::/32` (an RIR allocation), `::/0` (any), or a 128-bit bitwise mask for non-prefix matches (rare). The semantics mirror IPv4 `nw_src`, but the width is four times larger. Field type: **L3 header field**, subtype "IPv6 endpoint identifier".

**History and background.** OpenFlow 1.2 (2011) added full IPv6 support with a dedicated set of IPv6 match fields (OF 1.0 and 1.1 covered only IPv4). OVS shipped earlier support through the NXM extension: `NXM_NX_IPV6_SRC` (code 19) appeared in OVS v1.1 (2011). When OXM was standardized, OVS added `OXM_OF_IPV6_SRC` (code 26) in the same OF 1.2 release. The block documentation in `meta-flow.h` for `MFF_IPV6_SRC` records both `NXM_NX_IPV6_SRC(19) since v1.1` and `OXM_OF_IPV6_SRC(26) since OF1.2 and v1.1`. Stable since. OVS v1.8 (2013) added arbitrary bitwise masking for IPv6; before that only CIDR prefixes were supported.

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.ipv6_src` is 128 bits wide (`union ovs_be32[4]` or `struct in6_addr`). The MFF is `MFF_IPV6_SRC` in `meta-flow.h`, type `be128`, prerequisite IPv6 (eth_type=0x86dd). The prefix lookup member `ipv6_src` enables a trie-based longest-prefix match. The OVS classifier has its own trie for IPv6 prefixes, in parallel with the IPv4 trie. The two tries do not share nodes; dispatch happens at the eth_type level above. In the 4.8 taxonomy this field belongs to Group G, the L3 IPv6 fields, alongside `ipv6_dst` (covered in §4.8.X.18) and `ipv6_label`, `ipv6_exthdr`, and `nd_target` (covered natively in §4.8.7).

**Role.** The primary role is to identify the source endpoint for any IPv6-based forwarding or filtering decision, equivalent to `nw_src` for IPv4. Secondary roles include IPv6 anti-spoofing (the rule `priority=200,in_port=N,ipv6,ipv6_src=2001:db8:tenant1::/64,actions=resubmit` lets only the correct subnet enter the port), matching link-local for NDP (`ipv6_src=fe80::/10`), matching unique-local (`fc00::/7`), and matching multicast (`ff00::/8`). In OVN, the ACL `ip6.src == 2001:db8::/64` compiles to the flow `ipv6_src=2001:db8::/64` at stage `LS_IN_ACL`. The stage `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` uses `ip6.dst` (compiled to `ipv6_dst`) for route lookup.

**Why it exists.** IPv6 deployment grew rapidly from 2011 to 2015 with cloud and mobile rollouts (T-Mobile and Comcast enabled IPv6 by default). OF 1.0 and 1.1 had no IPv6 support, so any rule that needed IPv6 had to fall back to OVS NORMAL, which is not programmable. OF 1.2 and the OVS NXM extension solved this by adding a parallel IPv6 set of match fields. The 128-bit width (versus 32-bit IPv4) required a new `be128` MFF format. OVS invested in a parser and printer for the colon-hex format and a four-times-larger prefix tree to handle IPv6 routing.

**Problems it solves.** The concrete question is how to enforce programmable ACL, routing, and anti-spoofing for IPv6 in OVS. The solution is `ipv6_src` as a first-class field, a dedicated IPv6 prefix tree in the classifier, and a 16-byte OXM TLV format (four times the IPv4 size). The scope covers every L3 IPv6 use case in OVS and OVN. It does not cover the IPv6 mobility extension header chain (the `ipv6_exthdr` field handles that, see §4.8.7.3) or embedded IPv4 inside IPv6 (a 4-in-6 tunnel requires separate parsing).

**Importance.** Medium, with medium-to-high frequency in dual-stack deployments (cloud providers such as GCP and Azure are dual-stack by default, and on-prem data centers in 2024 onward are often dual-stack). An operator must know the IPv6 format (the RFC 5952 compression rule with `::`), the common prefix table (`::1/128` loopback, `::/128` unspecified, `fe80::/10` link-local, `fc00::/7` ULA, `2000::/3` global unicast, `ff00::/8` multicast, `2002::/16` 6to4 deprecated, `2001:db8::/32` documentation), and the option of an arbitrary bitwise mask for the rare non-prefix case (for example, matching the interface ID portion).

**How it works.** The datapath parses the Ethernet header. If `eth_type=0x86dd` (IPv6), it parses bytes 8 to 23 of the IPv6 header into `flow.ipv6_src` (16 bytes big-endian). The classifier uses the IPv6 prefix tree for `O(log n)` longest-prefix matching of CIDR prefix rules, and arbitrary bitwise masks fall into separate TSS subtables. A match `ipv6_src=PREFIX/LEN` compares `(packet.ipv6_src & mask) == (rule.ipv6_src & mask)`, with `mask` being the 128-bit prefix mask. The action `set_field:ADDR->ipv6_src` writes 16 bytes into the header. This is rare in practice and is usually used only for IPv6 NAT, which is itself rare.

**OXM TLV byte layout.** Exact match (16-byte value, hasmask=0):

```
| 0x80 0x00 | 0x34 | 0x10 | <16 byte IPv6 src> |
| oxm_class | f|hm | len  | value              |
# OXM_OF_IPV6_SRC, oxm_field=26, hasmask=0, length=16
```

Masked variant (hasmask=1, 16-byte value + 16-byte mask, length=32):

```
| 0x80 0x00 | 0x35 | 0x20 | <16 byte value> | <16 byte mask> |
# oxm_field=26, hasmask=1, length=32
```

Total exact: 20 bytes. Masked: 36 bytes. This is the largest TLV size in the catalog, alongside `MFF_IPV6_DST` and `MFF_CT_LABEL`. NXM: `NXM_NX_IPV6_SRC` (oxm_class=0x0001, code 19, with the same format) provides legacy support for controllers that use OF 1.0 or 1.1 with the NXM extension.

**Match and write examples.**

```
# IPv6 anti-spoofing: allow only the tenant1 subnet through port tap1
priority=200,in_port=tap1,ipv6,ipv6_src=2001:db8:tenant1::/64,actions=resubmit(,10)
priority=0,in_port=tap1,ipv6,actions=drop

# Block link-local from the uplink (link-local stays inside the local subnet)
priority=150,in_port=uplink,ipv6,ipv6_src=fe80::/10,actions=drop

# Allow multicast NDP
priority=100,ipv6,ipv6_src=fe80::/10,nw_proto=58,icmpv6_type=135,actions=resubmit(,nd_table)

# Match the documentation prefix (RFC 3849) for testing
priority=50,ipv6,ipv6_src=2001:db8::/32,actions=output:test_log

# Non-prefix bitwise mask (rare): match interface ID bytes 56 to 63 equal to 0x0001
priority=80,ipv6,ipv6_src=::1/0x0000000000000000ffffffffffffffff,actions=output:N

# IPv6 NAT (rare; set the source for egress translation)
actions=set_field:2001:db8:public::1->ipv6_src,output:uplink
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read.** Master the RFC 5952 compression rules (lower-case hex, leading-zero suppression, `::` used only once per address, on the longest run of zeros). Know the prefix tiers (global versus ULA versus link-local versus multicast). (2) **Edit.** Prefer CIDR prefixes for subnet rules (`/64` for a host network, `/48` for a site, `/32` for an RIR). Use an arbitrary bitwise mask only when truly needed, with a clear reason documented in a rule comment. (3) **Observe.** Use `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep ipv6_src` to view the distribution. Use `tcpdump -nn ip6 src 2001:db8::/64` to cross-check. (4) **Debug.** If a rule does not hit despite a packet with the correct prefix, check that the `eth_type=0x86dd` prerequisite is set (the `ipv6` alias implies it), that the IPv6 extension header chain is not confusing the parser (very rare), and that the IPv6 hex string is written correctly (the `::` compression appears only once).

**Classification.** L3 header field (real wire bits), CIDR prefix mask or arbitrary bitwise mask, readable and writable (write is rare). Prerequisite: `eth_type=0x86dd` (alias `ipv6`). Different from `ipv6_dst` (the destination counterpart, see §4.8.X.18), `nw_src` (IPv4 32-bit counterpart, see §4.8.X.7), `ipv6_label` (the 20-bit Flow Label, native §4.8.7.2), and `nd_target` (the 128-bit ND option, native §4.8.7.4).

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,ipv6,ipv6_src=2001:db8::1,ipv6_dst=2001:db8::2,...'
Final flow: ...,ipv6_src=2001:db8::1,ipv6_dst=2001:db8::2,...
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | awk '/ipv6_src/{print}'
$ tcpdump -nn -i any 'ip6 src net 2001:db8::/32'
```

**Source code reference.** The function `flow_extract` (`lib/flow.c`) parses the IPv6 header into `flow.ipv6_src`. The function `mf_set_value` (`lib/meta-flow.c`) is the write path for `MFF_IPV6_SRC`. The functions `format_ipv6_addr` and `parse_ipv6_addr` (`lib/match.c`) handle the RFC 5952 text format. The function `classifier_lookup` (`lib/classifier.c`) performs the trie longest-prefix match for IPv6 prefix rules. Branch baseline: OVS v2.17.9. The block documentation for `MFF_IPV6_SRC` in `meta-flow.h` records NXM=19, OXM=26, and the prefix lookup member `ipv6_src`.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom A: a rule does not hit even though `tcpdump` shows the correct IPv6 source. Check the `ipv6` prerequisite (eth_type=0x86dd), that the hex compression is correct, and that the prefix length is correct (a `/65` typo where `/64` was intended shifts the boundary). Symptom B: a rule with an arbitrary bitwise mask compiles slowly or fails. The OVS classifier expands such rules into their own subtable instead of using the trie. With many rules of this kind, the performance impact is noticeable, so prefer CIDR. Symptom C: the action `set_field:ADDR->ipv6_src` has no effect. Verify that the packet is actually IPv6. If it is IPv4, the action is a silent no-op.

**Tightly related to.** `eth_type` (the direct prerequisite), `ipv6_dst` (the destination counterpart), `ipv6_label` (Flow Label), `ipv6_exthdr` (the bitmap of the extension header chain), `nd_target` (the Neighbor Discovery option), `nw_src` (the IPv4 counterpart), `ct_ipv6_src` (the conntrack original-tuple variant), and the classifier prefix tree in `lib/classifier.c`.

**Version differences.** OF 1.0 and 1.1 had no IPv6 match fields. OF 1.2 (2011) added `OXM_OF_IPV6_SRC`. OVS v1.1 (2011) added the NXM extension first. OVS v1.8 (2013) added arbitrary bitwise masking. OVS v2.4 (2015) optimized the IPv6 prefix tree for large rule sets. Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 plus OVN v22.03.8.

**Real-world incident.** A production OVN dual-stack cluster reported that the IPv6 ACL `ip6.src == 2001:db8:tenant1::/64` did not block spoofed traffic. Investigation: the dumped flow `ipv6_src=2001:db8:tenant1::/64,actions=drop` was correctly installed, but a test packet with source `2001:db8:tenant1:beef::1` still passed. Root cause: the drop rule had a lower priority than the rule that allowed IPv6 NDP, `priority=1000,nw_proto=58,actions=resubmit`. The spoofed packet was a TCP packet, but it carried a Routing Header (an extension header) at position 0. The older OVS 2.13 parser interpreted `nw_proto=43` (Routing) instead of `6` (TCP), so the packet matched the NDP-allow rule rather than the drop rule. Fix: upgrade OVS to 2.17 (which parses the full extension header chain), and add an explicit drop that includes the `ipv6_exthdr` flag.

**Synthetic exercise.** Use a Mininet bridge `s1` with two dual-stack hosts h1 (`2001:db8::1/64`) and h2 (`2001:db8::2/64`). Install three flows on s1: (a) `priority=200,ipv6,ipv6_src=2001:db8::/64,actions=output:2`, (b) `priority=100,ipv6,actions=drop`, (c) `priority=50,arp,actions=flood` (the IPv4 fallback). Verify that `ping6` between h1 and h2 works through flow (a). Test spoofing: on h1, run `ip -6 addr add 2001:db8:bad::1/64 dev h1-eth0`, then `ping6 -I 2001:db8:bad::1 2001:db8::2`. Verify that the packet drops at flow (b). Read the counter on each flow.

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) Forgetting the `ipv6` prerequisite (eth_type=0x86dd). OVS rejects with `field ipv6_src missing prerequisite`. (b) Wrong hex format (multiple `::` compressions). The parser rejects it. (c) An off-by-one prefix length (`/63` instead of `/64`). The rule matches a larger prefix than intended. (d) A multicast prefix mistyped as `ff::/8` instead of `ff00::/8`. The match is wrong. (e) Overuse of arbitrary bitwise masks slows down the classifier.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux: `ip6tables -s 2001:db8::/32` is equivalent and uses the same prefix syntax. Cisco IOS: `permit ipv6 2001:db8::/32 any` is the standard ACL form. NSX-T DFW has a `source_groups` field that contains IPv6 IP sets, which is semantically equivalent. A P4 model defines an IPv6 parser state and after parsing uses `transition select(hdr.ipv6.src_addr[127:64])` for prefix dispatch, inheriting the OpenFlow IPv6 match-field concept.

---

### 4.8.X.18 `ipv6_dst`, the 128-bit destination IPv6 address of a packet

**Concept.** `ipv6_dst` is a 128-bit match field that holds the destination IPv6 address: 16 bytes at offset 24 to 39 of the IPv6 header (RFC 8200 §3). It is the destination counterpart of `ipv6_src` and is semantically equivalent to `nw_dst` in IPv4 but with a 128-bit width. This is the key field for L3 IPv6 routing: a router looks up the IPv6 FIB by `ipv6_dst` to find the next hop and the outgoing interface. Match formats include colon-hex exact, CIDR prefix, and arbitrary bitwise mask. Field type: **L3 header field**, subtype "IPv6 destination identifier".

**History and background.** The history mirrors `ipv6_src`: `NXM_NX_IPV6_DST` (code 20) appeared in OVS v1.1 (2011), and `OXM_OF_IPV6_DST` (code 27) appeared in OF 1.2 and OVS v1.1. The block documentation for `MFF_IPV6_DST` in `meta-flow.h` records `NXM_NX_IPV6_DST(20) since v1.1` and `OXM_OF_IPV6_DST(27) since OF1.2 and v1.1`, type `be128`, prerequisite IPv6, prefix lookup member `ipv6_dst`. Stable since. OVS v1.8 added arbitrary bitwise masking. The `/64` prefix pattern is especially common because the default IPv6 host network is `/64` per RFC 4291.

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.ipv6_dst` is 128 bits wide. The MFF is `MFF_IPV6_DST` in `meta-flow.h`. The prefix lookup member enables the trie longest-prefix match. In the 4.8 taxonomy this field belongs to Group G, the L3 IPv6 fields, alongside `ipv6_src` (§4.8.X.17). Note that `ipv6_dst` matters more than `ipv6_src` for routing. Every packet must have a destination so that the path can be chosen, while the source is only needed for ACL or the return path. The OVS classifier uses the IPv6 destination trie for route lookup. CIDR prefix masks share the same trie, which is separate from the source trie.

**Role.** The primary role is L3 IPv6 routing decisions, equivalent to `nw_dst` for IPv4. In OVN, the stage `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` (table 11 of the LR pipeline) emits one flow per static or dynamic route with a match such as `ip6.dst == 2001:db8::/64` and actions `outport=lr1-lsp1; reg0=next_hop_ipv6; output;`. This is the IPv6 FIB of the distributed virtual router. In standalone OVS, a rule such as `priority=100,ipv6,ipv6_dst=2001:db8:dc1::/48,actions=output:N` forwards by prefix region. Secondary roles include detecting multicast (`ipv6_dst=ff00::/8` covers every multicast group), matching the all-nodes link-local address (`ff02::1`), and matching the all-routers link-local address (`ff02::2`).

**Why it exists.** L3 IPv6 routing is the most fundamental function when moving from IPv4-only to dual-stack or IPv6-only. OF 1.2 plus the OVS NXM extension expose `ipv6_dst` so that the controller can program the entire IPv6 FIB instead of falling back to OVS NORMAL. The 128-bit width required a new `be128` MFF and a dedicated IPv6 prefix tree in the classifier. The `/64` host-network pattern differs significantly from the IPv4 `/24` pattern, so the trie depth differs. OVS v2.4 (2015) optimized for the common `/64` boundary.

**Problems it solves.** The concrete question is how to enforce programmable IPv6 routing on the distributed virtual router in OVN. The solution is that `ovn-northd` knows the `Logical_Router.static_routes` table and the connected subnets from `Logical_Switch_Port`, then compiles one flow per route with `ip6.dst==PREFIX,actions=outport=NEXT_HOP; output;` at stage `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING`. The scope covers connected routes (directly attached subnets) and static routes (declared in the Northbound database). It does not cover dynamic routing protocols (BGP and OSPF are out of scope per the CLAUDE.md North Star).

**Importance.** Medium, and a foundation of the IPv6 LR pipeline. The stage `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` is the route-lookup point. Every east-west or north-south IPv6 flow that traverses an LR must hit it. An operator must know the difference between unicast and multicast prefixes (`ff00::/8`), the OVN compile pattern (priority is `prefix length` plus a base, so a longer prefix gets higher priority for longest-match), and that link-local `fe80::/10` is not routable across links (the LR drops it by default).

**How it works.** The datapath parses bytes 24 to 39 of the IPv6 header into `flow.ipv6_dst`. The classifier uses the IPv6 trie for longest-prefix matching: the rule with the longest matching prefix wins. In OVN, the function `build_lrouter_routes_lflows` (`northd/northd.c`) iterates the static routes and the connected routes and emits one flow per route, with the prefix length encoded into the priority (`priority = 100 + prefix_len`). The actions set `reg0` to the next hop and the output port. In standalone OVS, rules are installed manually through `add-flow`.

**OXM TLV byte layout.** Same shape as `ipv6_src`. Exact: 20 bytes (`oxm_class=0x8000`, `oxm_field=27`, `hasmask=0`, `length=16`, value 16 bytes). Masked: 36 bytes (`hasmask=1`, `length=32`, 16-byte value plus 16-byte mask).

```
# Exact match
| 0x80 0x00 | 0x36 | 0x10 | <16 byte IPv6 dst> |
# OXM_OF_IPV6_DST, oxm_field=27, hasmask=0

# Prefix /64 (mask: high 64 bits = 1, low 64 = 0)
| 0x80 0x00 | 0x37 | 0x20 | <16 byte value> | ff..ff 00..00 |
```

Legacy NXM: `NXM_NX_IPV6_DST` (oxm_class=0x0001, code 20).

**Match examples.**

```
# IPv6 routing (OVN-style): a connected /64 route
priority=164,ipv6,metadata=0xLR1,ipv6_dst=2001:db8:dc1:1::/64,actions=load:0xPORT->NXM_NX_REG15[],resubmit(,32)
# A static /48 route (priority lower than the /64 connected route)
priority=148,ipv6,metadata=0xLR1,ipv6_dst=2001:db8:other::/48,actions=load:0xUPLINK->NXM_NX_REG15[],resubmit(,32)
# The default route ::/0 (lowest priority)
priority=100,ipv6,metadata=0xLR1,actions=load:0xDEFAULT_GW->NXM_NX_REG15[],resubmit(,32)

# All-nodes link-local multicast
priority=200,ipv6,ipv6_dst=ff02::1,actions=flood

# Drop link-local across routers (the LR does not route fe80::)
priority=300,ipv6,ipv6_dst=fe80::/10,actions=drop

# Match the documentation prefix
priority=50,ipv6,ipv6_dst=2001:db8::/32,actions=output:doc_log

# IPv6 NAT (DNAT), rare; rewrite the destination before forwarding
actions=set_field:2001:db8:internal::5->ipv6_dst,output:internal
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read.** Understand the OVN compile pattern (priority = base + prefix_len). Read flows with `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=11 | grep ipv6_dst`. (2) **Edit.** In OVN, use `ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr1 PREFIX NEXT_HOP`. In standalone OVS, install rules manually with `add-flow`. (3) **Observe.** Use `ovs-ofctl dump-flows | sort -k1 -t= | grep ipv6_dst` to see rules sorted by priority and prefix length. Read the per-route packet and byte counters to detect asymmetric traffic. (4) **Debug.** If a VM cannot reach an IPv6 destination, run `ovn-trace --ovs --minimal -- 'inport==..,ip6.dst==2001:db8::1,...'` to trace from the source LSP through the LR routing stage. Verify that the correct route is hit by inspecting the `Reaches` output.

**Classification.** L3 header field, CIDR prefix mask (the main form) or arbitrary bitwise mask, readable and writable (write is rare and used only for IPv6 NAT). Prerequisite: `eth_type=0x86dd` (alias `ipv6`). Different from `ipv6_src` (the source counterpart, see §4.8.X.17), `nw_dst` (the IPv4 counterpart), and `nd_target` (the target address of an ND message, native §4.8.7.4, which is not the packet header destination).

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,ipv6,ipv6_src=2001:db8::1,ipv6_dst=2001:db8::2,...'
Final flow: ...,ipv6_dst=2001:db8::2,...
Datapath actions: ...
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=11 | grep ipv6_dst    # OVN LR_IN_IP_ROUTING
$ ovn-nbctl lr-route-list lr1                              # IPv6 static and connected routes
$ ovn-trace ls1 'inport=="lsp1" && ip6.dst==2001:db8:dc1:1::5'
```

**Source code reference.** The function `flow_extract` parses the IPv6 destination. The function `mf_set_value` is the write path for `MFF_IPV6_DST`. The function `build_lrouter_routes_lflows` (`northd/northd.c`) compiles OVN routes into `ipv6_dst` rules. The function `classifier_lookup_prefix_v6` (anchor; the actual name may be `trie_lookup` with IPv6 parameters in `lib/classifier-private.h`) performs the trie longest-prefix match. Branch baseline: OVS v2.17.9 plus OVN v22.03.8.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom A: a rule does not hit even though the packet has the correct prefix. Check the `ipv6` prerequisite, the prefix length, and the hex compression format. Symptom B: longest-prefix matching is wrong (a longer-prefix rule does not win). Verify the OVN priority encoding (`priority = base + len`). For manually installed rules, check that priorities decrease properly with the prefix length. Symptom C: a packet to `ff02::1` (all-nodes) is being dropped. Verify that the multicast-permit rule has a higher priority than the default-deny rule.

**Tightly related to.** `eth_type` (the prerequisite), `ipv6_src` (the source counterpart), `ipv6_label` (Flow Label, used for ECMP hashing), `nw_dst` (the IPv4 counterpart), `nd_target` (the ND message target, with different semantics), `ct_ipv6_dst` (the conntrack original tuple), the OVN `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` stage, and `build_lrouter_routes_lflows`.

**Version differences.** Not present in OF 1.0 or 1.1. Added in OF 1.2. The NXM extension shipped first in OVS v1.1. OVS v1.8 added arbitrary bitwise masking. OVS v2.4 optimized the IPv6 prefix tree for the `/64` boundary. OVN v20.03 (2020) improved the IPv6 routing compile pattern for large route tables. Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 plus OVN v22.03.8.

**Real-world incident.** A dual-stack OVN 22.03 cluster with more than 5,000 static IPv6 routes reported that `ovn-controller` was running at 100% CPU after a tenant added 2,000 new routes in bulk. Investigation: `ovs-appctl coverage/show | grep classifier` showed an unusually large number of IPv6 destination subtables. The generated flows used arbitrary bitwise masks instead of CIDR (the tenant tool had converted the prefixes incorrectly). Root cause: arbitrary masks do not go into the trie. Each such rule becomes its own subtable, so the classifier expanded too much. Fix: convert the masks to strict CIDR, restart `ovn-controller`, and CPU usage returned to normal.

**Synthetic exercise.** Use Mininet with OVN: one LS `ls1` (`2001:db8::/64`) and one LR `lr1` with the static route `2001:db8:other::/48 next-hop 2001:db8::100`. Verify the route is active: `ovn-nbctl lr-route-list lr1` should show one entry. Trace a packet from a VM in `ls1` to destination `2001:db8:other::5`: `ovn-trace ls1 'inport=="vm1" && ip6.dst==2001:db8:other::5'` should confirm the static route is hit. Variant: add a more specific `/64` route in the same prefix and verify that longest-match wins (the `/64` rule wins).

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) Forgetting the `ipv6` prerequisite. OVS rejects the rule. (b) Wrong prefix length (`/65` instead of `/64`). The match prefix is shifted. (c) A mistyped multicast prefix. The match misses the intended group. (d) Overuse of arbitrary bitwise masks slows the classifier (see the incident above). (e) Wrong hex compression (multiple `::`). The parser rejects it.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux: `ip -6 route add 2001:db8::/64 dev eth0` adds an IPv6 route. The kernel FIB radix tree handles the longest-match. Cisco IOS: `ipv6 route 2001:db8::/64 ETH0` is equivalent. NSX-T LogicalRouter has a `route_advertisement_types` field for IPv6 route summarization. A P4 program defines an LPM table with a 128-bit key, which is equivalent to the OVS trie classifier. OVN is distinctive in that routing is distributed, so the FIB is spread across all chassis; each chassis stores only the routes that are relevant to it.

---

### 4.8.X.19 `ct_nw_proto`, the L3 protocol byte of the original conntrack tuple

**Concept.** `ct_nw_proto` is an 8-bit match field that holds the "Protocol" byte (IPv4 header byte 9) or the "Next Header" byte (IPv6 header byte 6) of the **original direction tuple** stored in the conntrack entry. It also reflects the parent connection when the packet belongs to a related connection (FTP, SIP, and other helpers). It differs from `ip_proto` and `nw_proto`. `nw_proto` is the protocol of the **current packet** under processing, which a NAT or translation step may have changed. `ct_nw_proto` always refers to the original tuple committed when the connection started. Field type: **conntrack metadata**, sub-type "L3 protocol of the original tuple".

**History and background.** OVS v2.8 (2017) added `NXM_NX_CT_NW_PROTO` code 119 in the same batch that expanded the conntrack original-tuple fields (`ct_nw_src/dst/proto`, `ct_ipv6_src/dst`, `ct_tp_src/dst`). The block comment in `meta-flow.h` for `MFF_CT_NW_PROTO` reads: `"NXM_NX_CT_NW_PROTO(119) since v2.8"`, `"OXM: none"`, `"Access: read-only"`. There is no OXM equivalent because ONF did not standardize the conntrack fields. They are an OVS-specific extension. The field has been stable from v2.8 to now. It is read-only because `set_field` cannot overwrite a value that came from an already-committed conntrack entry. Modifying it would break lookup integrity.

**Position in the architecture.** `struct flow.ct_nw_proto` is an 8-bit `u8`. The MFF entry is `MFF_CT_NW_PROTO` (`meta-flow.h`, type `u8`, prereq CT, meaning the packet must have passed through a `ct()` action and have `ct_state=+trk`; matching is not possible until the packet is tracked). The actual storage is in `struct conn` in `lib/conntrack.c` (userspace) or in `nf_conn` (kernel). The `nw_proto` byte of the original tuple is stored permanently from commit time. Pipeline flow: a packet enters `ct()`, the datapath looks up the conntrack table by the current 5-tuple, finds the entry, and populates `flow.ct_nw_proto` from `entry.original_tuple.nw_proto`.

**Role.** The main role is to distinguish a "NAT-translated current packet" from the "original connection tuple". A typical use case is stateful NAT64. An IPv6 packet enters from a tenant. After NAT64 it becomes IPv4. `nw_proto` is still 6 if the connection is TCP. The original tuple is stored as IPv6 with the same protocol. A match on `ct_nw_proto=6` confirms that the original tuple is TCP. Another use case is the FTP protocol helper. The data-channel packet (port 20 in active mode, ephemeral in passive mode) is a related connection. The data-channel `ct_nw_proto` is 6 (TCP). The parent connection `ct_nw_proto` is also 6. The field is not useful for helper detection on its own. Use `ct_state=+rel` instead. A practical use case is OVN load-balancer hairpin. A packet returns to the VIP. The packet `nw_proto` is 6 (TCP). The parent CT entry `ct_nw_proto` is also 6. The field helps distinguish a fresh connection from a related one.

**Why it exists.** Before OVS 2.8, conntrack only exposed `ct_state`, `ct_zone`, `ct_mark`, and `ct_label`. There was no original 5-tuple. A rule that wanted to test the original tuple (for example, "drop the reply if the original source is not in the tenant subnet") could not be written. OVS 2.8 expanded the metadata so that `ovn-northd` could compile rules of the form `ct.dnat && ct.original.dst==VIP` to flow rules of the form `ct_state=+dnat,ct_nw_dst=VIP_orig`. `ct_nw_proto` shipped in the same batch for consistency. To match the original source or destination, you also need the original protocol for an accurate lookup. ONF did not standardize this because conntrack is an OVS-specific extension, not a generic OpenFlow concept.

**Problems it solves.** The specific problem is how to reference the original L3 protocol of a connection after NAT has changed it. This is rare but it occurs with NAT64 or with the inner tuple of an ICMP error packet. The solution is that conntrack stores the original tuple in the entry, the `ct()` action populates it into flow metadata, and a downstream rule matches `ct_nw_proto`. The scope covers any rule that must distinguish the original protocol from the current packet protocol. Common cases include NAT64, IPv4-only legacy CGNAT gateways, and ICMP error packets (whose inner tuple represents the original connection that produced the error). The field does not solve protocol upgrades within the same family. A TCP-to-UDP change never happens mid-connection.

**Importance.** Medium. The field is rare in pure cloud-native dual-stack OVN deployments (NAT64 is uncommon). It is more common at the CGNAT or legacy IPv4-only edge. Operator must-know items: distinguish `ct_nw_proto` (original tuple) from `ip_proto` (current packet); the read-only nature (no `set_field`); the implicit prereq `ct_state=+trk` (the packet must already be tracked); and the same value semantics as `ip_proto` (the same IANA protocol numbers).

**How it works.** The datapath handles the `ct()` action by looking up the conntrack table on the packet's current 5-tuple. On a hit, the datapath populates the metadata: `flow.ct_state`, `flow.ct_zone`, `flow.ct_mark`, `flow.ct_label`, and the original 5-tuple `flow.ct_nw_src/dst/proto`, `flow.ct_ipv6_src/dst`, `flow.ct_tp_src/dst`. All of these come from `entry.original_tuple`. After `ct()`, the classifier evaluates rules that match `ct_nw_proto=N`. The packet recirculates through the pipeline with this metadata. A match on `ct_nw_proto=N` is an exact 8-bit comparison, like `ip_proto`.

**OXM TLV byte layout.** NXM only (no OXM):

```
| 0x00 0x01 | nxm_field=119 << 1 = 0xEE | hasmask=0 | length=1 | value 1 byte |
| nxm_class | header                    |           |          |              |
# NXM_NX_CT_NW_PROTO, hasmask=0, length=1
```

Total TLV size: 5 bytes. The specification does not allow `hasmask=1`. With an 8-bit IANA assignment, a mask has no practical meaning, the same as for `ip_proto`.

**Match examples.**

```
# Match a TCP connection by the original tuple (NAT may have changed the current src/dst, but the original tuple is TCP)
priority=100,ip,ct_state=+trk+est,ct_nw_proto=6,actions=output:tcp_audit

# Match a UDP original tuple (a DNS reply back to the tenant)
priority=100,ip,ct_state=+trk+est,ct_nw_proto=17,udp_dst=53,actions=output:dns_log

# Distinguish an ICMP error (the inner tuple original is TCP or UDP)
priority=200,icmp,ct_state=+trk+rel,ct_nw_proto=6,actions=resubmit(,tcp_err_handler)
priority=200,icmp,ct_state=+trk+rel,ct_nw_proto=17,actions=resubmit(,udp_err_handler)

# OVN-style hairpin: match a reply to the VIP with TCP as the original protocol
priority=150,ip,ct_state=+trk+est+dnat,ct_nw_proto=6,ct_nw_src=10.0.0.5,actions=...

# Combine the original source plus protocol to identify connection scope
priority=120,ip,ct_state=+trk+est,ct_nw_src=10.0.tenant1.0/24,ct_nw_proto=6,actions=output:tenant1_audit
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read.** Understand the difference between `ip_proto` (the current packet) and `ct_nw_proto` (the original tuple). Know when to use which. Most cases need only `ip_proto`. NAT64, CGNAT, and ICMP error scenarios are the cases that need `ct_nw_proto`. (2) **Modify.** A rule can only match the field; the field is read-only. To "change" the value, you must `ct(commit)` a new connection with the desired protocol. (3) **Observe.** `ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack -m` shows each entry with its original-tuple protocol. Cross-check against `ct_nw_proto` with `ofproto/trace`. (4) **Debug.** If a rule with `ct_nw_proto=6` does not match, verify the following: (a) the `ct_state=+trk` prereq is satisfied; (b) the packet really belongs to a committed connection; (c) the conntrack entry has not expired (the default `tcp_established` timeout is five days).

**Classification.** A conntrack metadata field, exact-match only, **read-only** (no `set_field`). Implicit prerequisite: `ct_state=+trk` (the packet must have passed through a `ct()` action and have a tracked entry). It differs from `ip_proto` (the current packet, read and write), `ct_state` (a state bitfield), `ct_zone` (a 16-bit zone separator), and `ct_nw_src/dst` (the original 3-tuple network fields added in the same v2.8 batch).

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack -m | grep -E '10\.0\.0\.5'
tcp,orig=(src=10.0.0.5,dst=10.0.0.2,sport=12345,dport=80),
    reply=(...),protoinfo=(state=ESTABLISHED),mark=0,zone=1
# orig.dst=10.0.0.2 and protocol "tcp" map to ct_nw_proto=6 and ct_nw_src=10.0.0.5

$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=1,ip,ct_state=+trk+est,...'
Final flow: ...,ct_state=0x22,ct_nw_proto=6,ct_nw_src=10.0.0.5,...

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep ct_nw_proto
 cookie=0x0, n_packets=N, ct_state=+trk+est,ct_nw_proto=6,actions=...
```

**Source code reference.** Function `conntrack_execute` (`lib/conntrack.c`) looks up the entry and populates `flow.ct_nw_proto` from `conn.key.nw_proto`. The `key.nw_proto` field is the protocol of the original tuple stored in the entry. Function `xlate_ct_action` (`ofproto/ofproto-dpif-xlate.c`) handles the `ct()` action and calls `conntrack_execute`. Function `mf_check_src_prereqs` (`lib/meta-flow.c`) validates the CT prerequisite for the match. The block comment for `MFF_CT_NW_PROTO` in `meta-flow.h` records NXM=119, no OXM, read-only access, and the CT prerequisite. Branch baseline: OVS v2.17.9.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom A: a rule does not hit even though the connection is committed. Verify the `ct_state=+trk` prereq. A packet that has not passed through `ct()` has no `ct_nw_proto`. Add `ct()` before the rule, or change the rule to use `ip_proto` if the intent is the current packet protocol. Symptom B: assuming that `ct_nw_proto` always equals `ip_proto` and that the field is unnecessary. This is wrong when NAT64 or CGNAT changes the protocol. Be careful in the rule logic. Symptom C: trying `set_field:N->ct_nw_proto`. OVS rejects this because the field is read-only. To get a connection with a different protocol, you must `ct(commit)` a new entry with a different 5-tuple.

**Tightly related to.** `ct_state` (the direct `+trk` prereq), `ct_zone`, `ct_nw_src/dst` (the original 3-tuple network fields from the same v2.8 batch), `ct_ipv6_src/dst`, `ct_tp_src/dst` (the original transport ports), `ip_proto` and `nw_proto` (the packet-side counterparts, read and write), the `ct()` action with `commit`, `zone`, and `nat`, and the `dpctl/dump-conntrack` tool. In OVN: `build_lswitch_lflows_pre_acl` and `build_lswitch_lflows_acl_hint` reference the original-tuple fields for stateful ACL.

**Version differences.** OVS v2.8 (2017) added `NXM_NX_CT_NW_PROTO(119)` in the same batch that expanded the original-tuple fields. OVS v2.10 (2018) is when OVN started using it for the reverse-direction ACL hint. ONF did not standardize the field, so there is no OXM equivalent. Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 plus OVN v22.03.8.

**Real-world incident.** A tenant deployed a stateful OVN firewall rule of the form "allow reply if the original was TCP from the tenant subnet". `ovn-northd` compiled it as `ct.original.proto==6 && ct.original.src==10.0.tenant.0/24,actions=allow`. After a host kernel upgrade, some reply packets were dropped at random. Investigation: `dpctl/dump-conntrack -m` showed the entry existed with `protoinfo=tcp`. However, `ofproto/trace` showed `ct_nw_proto=0`. Root cause: the conntrack table had been reset under RAM pressure. New entries were re-created from packets in the middle of a connection (no `+new`), so `ct_state=+est` was set without a complete `+trk`. The `+trk` prereq was satisfied implicitly, but `ct_nw_proto` was populated as empty from the partial entry. Fix: set `tcp_loose=0` so that conntrack only tracks connections that show a SYN, and tune RAM so the conntrack table does not get evicted.

**Synthetic exercise.** Use a Mininet bridge `s1` with two hosts `h1` and `h2`, with OVS conntrack zone 1. Install the following rules: (a) `priority=100,ip,ct_state=-trk,actions=ct(commit,zone=1)`, (b) `priority=100,ip,ct_state=+trk+new,tcp,actions=output:NORMAL`, (c) `priority=100,ip,ct_state=+trk+est,ct_nw_proto=6,actions=output:NORMAL`, and (d) `priority=10,ip,ct_state=+trk+est,actions=drop`. Test (a): HTTP from `h1` to `h2` (TCP). Verify that traffic passes through flow (b) at first, then through flow (c) on the reply. Read the counter on (c) to confirm that the match `ct_nw_proto=6` worked. Test variant: send a UDP DNS query. Flow (c) misses (`proto=17`), and flow (d) drops the packet. Verify that the counter on (c) does not increment for UDP traffic.

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) Forgetting the `+trk` prereq: the rule does not hit for packets that have not gone through `ct()`. (b) Confusing `ct_nw_proto` with `nw_proto` or `ip_proto` for a packet-side rule: a semantic confusion. Document the intent in a rule comment. (c) Trying `set_field:N->ct_nw_proto`: OVS rejects this with a read-only error. (d) An abrupt conntrack table eviction that leaves an empty entry: `ct_nw_proto=0` even though `ct_state=+trk+est`. Tune `tcp_loose` and monitor RAM. (e) Using `ct_nw_proto` in a zone different from the committed one: the entry does not exist, so the match misses.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux netfilter conntrack offers the `nfct_get_attr_u8(ct, ATTR_ORIG_L4PROTO)` API, which returns the L4 protocol of the original tuple. Linux conntrack stores the same concept. NSX-T DFW has a rule field named `service`, but it does not expose the original tuple to other rules. NSX-T runs at a higher abstraction level. P4 has no built-in conntrack. A P4 simulator needs an extern table with a 5-tuple key. OVS is unique in that `ct_nw_proto` lets a downstream pipeline rule match on the original protocol without parsing the application. This is important in NAT64 and CGNAT scenarios.

---

### 4.8.X.20 `in_phy_port`, the underlying physical port when `in_port` is logical (patch, bond, or tunnel)

**Concept.** `in_phy_port` is a 32-bit OXM match field that holds the OpenFlow port number of the **physical port** on which the packet actually arrived. It is meaningful when `in_port` refers to a logical port (a bond, tunnel port, or patch port). Spec ID: `OXM_OF_IN_PHY_PORT(1)` (oxm_class=`0x8000`, oxm_field=`1`, length=4), separate from `OXM_OF_IN_PORT(0)`. OF 1.2 specification §A.2.3.1 says: if `in_port` points to a logical port (for example a LAG or bond), `in_phy_port` points to the specific physical NIC member. If `in_port` is already physical, the two fields are equal and the specification allows the switch to omit `in_phy_port`. Field type: **pipeline metadata**, sub-type "ingress identifier (physical view)".

**History and background.** OF 1.2 (December 2011) added the OXM TLV format and at the same time split `IN_PHY_PORT` so that a spec-compliant controller could distinguish logical from physical. In the OVS source tree, the header `include/openflow/openflow-1.2.h` defines `OFPXMT_OFB_IN_PHY_PORT = 1` (the value 1 in the `OFPXMT_OFB_*` enum). However, `meta-flow.h` in v2.17.9 has no separate `MFF_IN_PHY_PORT` entry. OVS implements only one MFF for `in_port` (`MFF_IN_PORT` 16-bit for OF 1.0, plus `MFF_IN_PORT_OXM` 32-bit for OF 1.1 and later). In other words, the OF 1.2 specification has the concept but OVS does not expose a corresponding match field. The OVS datapath has no first-class "logical port wrapping a physical NIC". Bonds are implemented by the Linux kernel underneath (active-backup, balance-tcp). A patch port is a peer pair across two bridges. A tunnel port is a vif interface. When a packet enters OVS, it always comes from one single port.

**Position in the architecture.** The OXM ID has a value but the MFF does not exist. A rule that writes `in_phy_port=N` in `ovs-ofctl` is rejected by the parser with the error `unknown field`. You can try, but the rule will not install. There is no separate `in_phy_port` field in `struct flow` either. Spec compliance: OVS declares partial OF 1.2 support for this feature.

**Role.** Specification role: let a controller distinguish between member NICs when `in_port` is a LAG or aggregate. Actual role on OVS: almost unused. An operator who wants to debug "the packet arrived via a tunnel port, but which NIC did the underlay traffic go through" must use a different workaround (see the observation section).

**Why it exists.** Commercial hardware switches (Broadcom Trident, Tofino chips) often have a "trunk group" or "LAG port" concept exposed to the controller as a logical port. The controller needs to know which member received traffic for load-balancing analytics or fault-domain isolation. The OF 1.2 specification met this need.

**Problems it solves.** The specific problem (specification view) is to let a rule distinguish across members of a LAG or bond. The solution (specification view) is to split into two OXMs, with the second one optional for a switch to implement. The scope on OVS: not actually applicable, because OVS has no logical-port-aggregate concept at the OpenFlow layer.

**Importance.** Peripheral. Frequency in production OVS: about 0%. Operator must-know items: be aware that the field exists in the specification so that reading OF 1.2 documentation does not cause confusion; know that `ovs-ofctl` cannot install a rule with `in_phy_port=N` because there is no MFF; know the workaround when you need to debug the source physical NIC.

**How it works.** The specification says: the switch fills `in_phy_port` with the OpenFlow port number of the physical member that received the packet, and `in_port` with the OpenFlow port number of the logical port (LAG or tunnel) that dispatched it. OVS ignores this entirely because it has no logical-wraps-physical architecture.

**Match example (specification only; this does not work on OVS).**

```
# Specification-style rule. OVS rejects this at install time.
priority=100,in_port=2,in_phy_port=5,actions=goto_table:1
# Test on OVS:
$ ovs-ofctl add-flow br-int 'priority=100,in_port=2,in_phy_port=5,actions=goto_table:1'
ovs-ofctl: in_phy_port: unknown field
```

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read the OF 1.2 specification §A.2.3.1.** Understand the concept exists, so that you can handle a heterogeneous-switch deployment. (2) **OVS workaround.** If you must know which NIC a tunnel packet traversed, use `tcpdump -i ethX 'udp port 6081'` (Geneve) or `'udp port 4789'` (VXLAN) on each NIC to narrow it down. The OVS-native alternative is to run `ovs-appctl ofproto/trace`, read the `tunnel(...)` metadata along with `tun_src` and `tun_dst` to identify the tunnel, then cross-check the host routing table (`ip route get TUN_DST`) to find the NIC. (3) **Do not confuse this with `tun_src` and `tun_dst`.** Those two fields exist and hold the IP of the underlay tunnel endpoint. They are useful in multi-NIC debugging.

**Classification.** A pipeline metadata field per the specification. There is no MFF on OVS. Read-only per the specification. No prerequisite.

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovs-ofctl --version | head -1                       # confirm the OVS version
$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep in_phy_port      # always empty on OVS
$ ovs-appctl ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=42,...'    # tunnel debug uses tun_src/dst
```

**Source code reference.** The header `include/openflow/openflow-1.2.h` defines `OFPXMT_OFB_IN_PHY_PORT = 1`. The function `mf_from_oxm_header` (`lib/nx-match.c`) returns NULL for `OXM_OF_IN_PHY_PORT` because no MFF corresponds to it. `meta-flow.h` in v2.17.9 has no `MFF_IN_PHY_PORT` entry. Branch baseline: OVS v2.17.9.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom: a rule imported from a spec-compliant controller that contains `in_phy_port` is rejected by OVS. Drop the field, or remap it to `in_port`.

**Tightly related to.** `in_port` (the logical-view counterpart), `tun_src` and `tun_dst` (alternative tunnel-underlay debugging fields), and `OXM_OF_IN_PORT(0)` (the sister OXM).

**Version differences.** OF 1.2 (2011) added `OXM_OF_IN_PHY_PORT(1)`. OF 1.3 through 1.5 retained it. OVS from v1.7 through v3.3 has not exposed a corresponding MFF. Curriculum baseline: OVS v2.17.9 plus OVN v22.03.8.

**Real-world incident.** A vendor moved a standard ONOS controller from a hardware switch to an OVS soft switch in the lab. The rule template included `in_phy_port=N` for per-NIC traffic debugging. After the migration, rule import failed. Investigation: the `ovs-ofctl` log showed "unknown field in_phy_port". Fix: the ONOS module has an "OF feature negotiate" configuration. Set `IN_PHY_PORT_SUPPORT=false` for the OVS dpid, and the controller skips generating that field.

**Synthetic exercise.** Try installing `ovs-ofctl add-flow br0 in_phy_port=5,actions=drop` on a Mininet OVS v2.17 setup and record the `unknown field` error. Compare with the rule `in_port=5,actions=drop`, which installs successfully. Read OF 1.2 specification §A.2.3.1 and write a one-line reason why OVS can skip implementing this field.

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) Assuming that OVS implements `in_phy_port` because the field is in the OpenFlow specification. You only learn the truth after testing. (b) Using `recirc_id` as a workaround. It is unrelated. (c) Confusing `in_phy_port` with `tun_src` (the underlay IP). They are semantically different.

**Comparison with other systems.** The Cisco Nexus 9k OpenFlow agent exposes `IN_PHY_PORT` in full because the hardware has the port-channel (LAG) concept. NSX-T does not use OpenFlow, so this field does not apply. A P4 program defines `metadata.ingress_port` per pipeline. It can split logical from physical by design, with more flexibility than the OpenFlow specification.

---

### 4.8.X.21 `ipv6_flabel` (alias `ipv6_label`), the 20-bit IPv6 Flow Label

**Concept.** `ipv6_flabel` is a 20-bit OXM match field that holds the Flow Label value in the IPv6 header (RFC 6437; bytes 0 through 3 of the IPv6 header carry 4 bits of Version, 8 bits of Traffic Class, and 20 bits of Flow Label). The sender sets the Flow Label at ingress to tag a specific IPv6 flow (the equivalent of a 5-tuple). Intermediate routers use it for ECMP hashing without parsing the L4 header. This is useful when packets are fragmented or when IPsec ESP hides the 5-tuple.
